Objection and Reply: Circularity of the Second Argument
Objection and answer regarding the second argument's circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the second argument for the need for a cause is circular, and the defence that extends the argument to all possibles by priority.

Core Idea
A circular argument can be defended by extending it from temporal to eternal possibles.

This passage presents a sharp objection to the second argument: it seems to assume the very conclusion it tries to prove. The objection is followed by a defence that the argument works for temporal events and can be extended to all possibles by priority. Let's examine the logic carefully.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims the argument's necessary conclusion is that the possible needs a cause, but the real cause of need is contingency, not the argument's steps. So the argument is circular. Read the translation and commentary to see how.
Notice the distinction between 'necessary' (lāzim) and 'required' (maṭlūb).
2
Explore the defence
The defence responds by focusing on temporal preponderance (events coming into existence). Even if one restricts the argument to temporal possibles, it can be extended to eternal possibles by priority, because no one distinguishes between the two types.
The Arabic term 'bi-ṭarīq al-ūlā' indicates a fortiori reasoning.
3
Reflect on the limitations
The commentator notes that the defence itself has issues ('wa fīhi mā fīhi'). Consider why this might be a weak response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( الطريق الثاني فيه نظر؛ لأن اللازم من هذا الطريق أن الممكن محتاج إلى المؤثر، وأما علة الاحتياج هو الإمكان فلا، فالمطلوب غير لازم واللازم غير مطلوب. قوله : (لأنه حصل بعد ما لم يكن الخ فإن قلت : هذا إنما يتم في ترجح الحادث، كما يدل عليه قوله لجاز أن لا تكون الحركة بعد السكون الخ، فلا يجري الدليل في الصفات القديمة الممكنة على رأي الأشاعرة مع أن المدعى عام قلت لو سلم فلا قائل بالفصل، فعلية : الإمكان في الحادث تستلزم العلية في غيره بطريق الأولى وفيه ما فيه.
His saying: '(The second path has a problem; because what is necessary from this path is that the possible needs the effecter, but the cause of the need is contingency, not the path, so the required thing is not necessary and the necessary thing is not required.)' His saying: '(Because it occurred after it was not... )' – if you say: this only applies to the preponderance of the temporal, as indicated by his saying 'so it would be permissible for motion not to be after rest, etc.', so the argument does not apply to eternal possibles according to the Ashʿarites, while the claim is general; I say: even if conceded, no one distinguishes; so causality in the temporal necessitates causality in others by way of priority, but there is something in it.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the second argument and its answer. The objector says the argument is problematic because it concludes that the possible needs an effecter (cause), but the real cause of need is contingency itself, not the argument's steps; thus the argument fails to prove what is required and what it necessarily implies is not what is demanded. In other words, the argument assumes the need for a cause, which is exactly what is disputed. The answer is given via an explanation of 'because it occurred after it was not': if one objects that this reasoning only works for temporal possibles (events), not for eternal possibles (like divine attributes on the Ashʿarite view), the response is that even if conceded, no one makes a distinction between temporal and eternal possibles in needing a cause; in fact, contingency in a temporal being entails causality in others by way of priority (a fortiori). However, the commentator notes that this answer itself has something to it (i.e., it is not without difficulty).

Key Terms
نظر
اللازم
المطلوب
علة الاحتياج
الإمكان
الترجح الحادث
الصفات القديمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second argument only works for temporal events, not for eternal possibles on the Ash'ari view.
Correction: The defence says that even if conceded, no one distinguishes between temporal and eternal possibles; causality in the temporal necessitates causality in others by priority.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection presented against the second argument?
A) The argument assumes the existence of time before the universe.
B) The argument concludes that the possible needs a cause, but the real cause of need is contingency, not the argument's steps.
C) The argument only works for eternal possibles, not temporal ones.
D) The argument relies on a definition of preponderance that is not agreed upon.

Key Takeaway
The second argument faces an objection of circularity, but the defence uses an a fortiori extension from temporal to eternal possibles, though the commentator hints at its weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you present arguments for your beliefs, are you ever guilty of proving something other than what you set out to prove? How does this lesson's exposure of the 'required vs. necessary' mismatch challenge you to examine your own reasoning?
?
The commentator hints at the weakness of the defence ('wa fīhi mā fīhi'). In your spiritual life, are you willing to admit when a defence of your faith is weak, or do you cling to it out of comfort? Write down one area where you might be doing this.
?
This lesson shows that even logical arguments can be met with serious objections. Does your reliance on reasoning ever overshadow your direct relationship with the Creator? What will you do to keep your heart attached to the Truth itself, not just the arguments?

Objection and Answer: The Nature of Preponderance
Objection and detailed answer about the nature of preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the preponderance (tarajjuḥ) cannot be an existential attribute simultaneous with existence, and why it is instead a mental consideration.

Core Idea
Preponderance is not a real attribute but a mental consideration.

In this passage, al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī addresses a subtle objection: if the preponderance (tarajjuḥ) occurs simultaneously with the existence of the possible, then it would be an existential attribute inhering in the possible while it exists, which seems to invalidate the need for a separate cause. The objection and its answer lead to a deeper distinction between dialectical and veridical replies, culminating in the view that preponderance is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī) rather than an extramental reality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part. The objection is that if preponderance is simultaneous with existence, it would be an existential attribute—but such attributes must be posterior to the subject's existence.
The objection tries to force a contradiction: if preponderance is both simultaneous and existential, it breaks the rule of existential attributes.
2
See the initial dialectical response
The commentator notes that accepting the opponent's premise (that preponderance is existential) leads to admitting the inference is invalid. This is a dialectical (jadalī) answer.
A dialectical answer grants the opponent's assumption to show that it still does not support the conclusion.
3
Grasp the veridical answer
The text then gives the truth (ḥaqq): preponderance is not existential but a mental consideration (iʿtibārī). If it were external, absurdities would follow like multiple preponderances.
This is the final position: preponderance is a mind-dependent attribute, not a real quality.
4
Consider the further objection and its resolution
A final objection from the second principle of al-Talwiḥāt is raised: the non-posteriority of preponderance would still invalidate its subsistence in the possible. The answer is to simply deny that preponderance is existential.
The correct answer remains the denial of existentiality, as the commentator (Sharīf) mentions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بل يترجح مع الوجود) وماقيل من أن الترجح إذا كان موجوداً لا يكون مع الوجود، إذ قد تقرر أن الصفة الوجودية يجب تأخرها عن وجود الموصوف، فليس بشيء؛ لأن فيه اعترافاً ببطلان الاستدلال؛ لأنه حينئذ يكون قديماً بالأثر متأخراً عن وجوده . قوله : ( فالترجح السابق الخ أي الترجح الذي سلم سبقته، فما قيل أن السبقة ينافي كونه صفة الوجود فيه اعتراف ببطلان الاستدلال . قوله : ( والحق) الخ ما مر كان جواباً جدلياً مبنياً على تسليم كونه وجودياً، كما أثبته الخصم، وهذا الجواب تحقيقي فلذا قال : والحق . قوله : (قد يتجدد ) كالعمى بعد البصر. قوله : (اعتباري) إذ لو كان موجوداً في الخارج يلزم ترتب الترجحات الموجودة في الخارج وكون الحركة بعد السكون والعلم بعد الجهل موجودين ليس دائراً على تجددهما ولظهوره ترك ذكره . قوله : ( يتصف به أي الاتصاف به انتزاعي، ومصداقه الأثر الموجود في الخارج. قوله : ( لا عدمه مطلقاً) فيجوز عليه العدم المستمر، بل هو متصف به عند التحقيق . قوله : (بل يترجح مع الوجود فيه بحث لأنه قد مر في القاعدة الثانية التي ذكرها صاحب التلويحات : أن الموجودات لا تقوم إلا بمحل سابق عليها بالوجود ولو بالذات، فعدم تأخر الترجح عن وجود الممكن يكفي في إبطال قيامه على تقدير وجوده بالممكن، فالصواب في الجواب منع وجوديته كما ذكره الشارح .
His saying: '(Rather, it becomes preponderant with existence). And what was said that if the preponderance is existent, it cannot be with existence, since it is established that an existential attribute must be posterior to the existence of its subject, is nothing; because it contains an admission of the invalidity of the inference; because it would then be posterior to the effect, delayed from its existence. His saying: '(So the preceding preponderance... )' i.e., the preponderance whose priority is conceded; so what was said that priority contradicts its being an attribute of existence contains an admission of the invalidity of the inference. His saying: '(And the truth... )' what has passed was a dialectical answer based on conceding that it is existential, as the opponent established, and this answer is a veridical one, therefore he said 'and the truth'. His saying: 'it may become renewed' like blindness after sight. His saying: '(considerational) because if it were existent externally, it would necessitate the ordering of preponderances existing externally, and that motion after rest and knowledge after ignorance are existent not depending on their renewal, and due to its obviousness he omitted mention. His saying: 'it qualifies it' i.e., the qualification is abstractive, and its concrete instantiation is the effect existing externally. His saying: 'not its absolute non-existence' so perpetual non-existence is permissible for it, rather it qualifies it upon investigation. His saying: '(Rather it becomes preponderant with existence) has a discussion; because it has passed in the second principle mentioned by the author of al-Talwihat that existents subsist only in a locus that is prior to them in existence, even if in essence; so the non-posteriority of the preponderance from the existence of the possible suffices to invalidate its subsistence, on the assumption of its existence, in the possible; so the correct answer is to deny its existentiality as the commentator mentioned.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that the preponderance (tarajjuḥ) occurs simultaneously with existence, not before it. If so, the preponderance could inhere in the possible itself while it exists, eliminating the need for a separate cause. The answer begins by noting an inconsistency: if the preponderance is an existential attribute, it must be posterior to the existence of its subject (the possible), but if it is simultaneous, it contradicts this principle. The opponent's claim that priority contradicts its being an attribute of existence is shown to be an admission that the inference is invalid. The commentator distinguishes between a dialectical answer (jadalī) that concedes the existentiality of preponderance to the opponent and a veridical answer (taḥqīqī) which is the truth: that preponderance is not existential but a mental consideration (iʿtibārī). Evidence for this is that if it were external, we would have multiple external preponderances, and things like motion after rest would be existent regardless of renewal, which is not the case. The qualification is abstractive (intizāʿī) with an external instantiation being the effect itself. Finally, a further objection from the second principle of al-Talwiḥāt is answered: since existents require a prior locus, the non-posteriority of preponderance to existence would still invalidate its subsistence in the possible if we assume it is existential, so the correct answer is simply to deny that preponderance is existential, as the Sharīf (al-Jurjānī) mentions.

Key Terms
الترجح مع الوجود
صفة وجودية
جواب جدلي
تحقيقي
اعتباري
انتزاعي
القاعدة الثانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the preponderance is a real existential attribute that can be simultaneous with existence.
Correction: The correct view is that preponderance is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī), not an extramental attribute. If it were existential, it would have to be posterior to the subject's existence, causing absurdities.

Checkpoint
According to the veridical answer (taḥqīqī), what is the nature of the preponderance (tarajjuḥ)?
A) It is an existential attribute inherent in the possible.
B) It is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī) and not externally existent.
C) It is the same as the effect itself.
D) It is a necessary attribute of the cause.

Key Takeaway
The correct view is that preponderance (tarajjuḥ) is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī), not an existential attribute; this resolves the apparent conflict with the simultaneity of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that preponderance (tarajjuḥ) is a mental consideration, not a real attribute. Does this conviction permeate your daily actions? When you achieve something by your efforts, do you genuinely attribute the success to Allah's creation of that effect, or do you still feel it was 'your doing'? What specific change can you make today to internalize this truth?
?
The lesson teaches that the true answer is veridical (taḥqīqī), not merely dialectical. In your own life, are you content with superficial intellectual concessions about theological truths, or are you seeking the veridical understanding that transforms your worldview? What is holding you back from that deeper certainty?
?
Consider the principle that existential attributes must be posterior to their subject. You exist; all your attributes—your knowledge, your power, your life—depend on your prior existence. What does that tell you about your complete dependence on the Self-Existent One? How does this lesson challenge any lingering sense of self-sufficiency?

The Third Argument: Qualified vs. Absolute Non-Existence
Sanusi presents al-Razi's premise distinguishing absolute from qualified non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between absolute and qualified non-existence and how it supports the contingency of time.

Core Idea
Time's non-existence is impossible only under a qualification, not absolutely.

Sanusi presents the opening of al-Razi's third argument, which is built on a premise from the philosophers: time cannot be non-existent before or after its existence. This lesson unpacks that premise and shows why the impossibility is only qualified, not absolute, thereby preserving time's contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the passage is incomplete; focus on the distinction between absolute and qualified non-existence.
The passage cuts off before stating the full conclusion.
2
Identify the two kinds of non-existence
The passage contrasts 'absolute non-existence' (al-ʿadam al-muṭlaq) with 'qualified non-existence' (al-ʿadam al-muqayyad). Understand the difference: absolute means in every respect; qualified means under a specific condition.
The argument hinges on this distinction.
3
Understand the consequence of absolute impossibility
If time's non-existence were absolutely impossible, time would be necessary by itself (wājib bi-dhātihī). But the philosophers only claim impossibility under the qualification of being before or after existence.
This preserves time's contingency.
4
Reflect on the incompleteness
The argument is incomplete; the next part would likely show that time's qualified impossibility implies it has a cause. Consider how this fits into the overall proof for a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث له ) أي للإمام الرازي ذكره في الأربعين، وقد بناه على قول الفلاسفة: أنه يمتنع عدم الزمان قبل وجوده أو بعده ) أي يمتنع عدمه مقيداً بهذا القيد وهو أن يكون قبل وجوده أو بعده لا عدمه مطلقاً، وإلا كان واجباً بذاته ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يمتنع كون عدمه قبل
The third path: to him (i.e., Imam al-Razi) mentioned in al-Arbaʿīn, and he built it on the statement of the philosophers: that it is impossible for time to be non-existent before its existence or after it – i.e., its non-existence is impossible when qualified by this condition, that it be before its existence or after it, not its absolute non-existence; otherwise it would be necessary by itself. (Otherwise) i.e., if its non-existence before is not impossible, then...
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage begins the third argument from Imam al-Razi, found in his work al-Arbaʿīn. The argument is built on the philosophers' premise that time cannot be non-existent before its existence or after it. This means that the non-existence of time is impossible if we qualify it as being before or after its existence; but its absolute non-existence (without qualification) is not impossible—if it were absolutely impossible, time would be necessary by itself. The text breaks off at 'if its non-existence before is not impossible...' likely continuing with the consequence. The argument aims to show that time, as a contingent, requires a cause because its non-existence before or after must be impossible, which implies it is caused. However, the argument is incomplete in this window.

Key Terms
الزمان
العدم المطلق
العدم المقيد
وامتناع عدمه
واجب بذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this argument proves time is eternal or necessary.
Correction: The argument explicitly says only qualified non-existence is impossible; absolute non-existence could be possible, so time is not necessary by itself.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, what would it imply if the non-existence of time were absolutely impossible (i.e., impossible in every respect)?
A) Time would be contingent.
B) Time would be necessary by itself (wājib bi-dhātihī).
C) Time would have a beginning.
D) Time would be an accident.

Key Takeaway
The third argument distinguishes absolute from qualified non-existence to avoid making time necessary, preserving its contingency and need for a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live your life assuming time will continue. But this lesson shows that time's existence is contingent—it could not have been at all. When you waste time, are you treating it as a trivial resource, or do you see each moment as a gift from the One who sustains it? Will you start your next hour with that awareness?
?
The philosophers argued that time cannot be non-existent before or after. Do you act as if time is an eternal container, or do you feel the weight of its contingency? If time itself depends on Allah, what does that mean for your hurriedness and anxiety about deadlines? How will you slow down today to honor the Giver of time?
?
If absolute non-existence of time would make it necessary—that is, uncreated and independent of Allah—then your ability to imagine a timeless state is not a proof that time is eternal. Are you willing to let go of the assumption that time is a fixed backdrop? What would change in your daily prayers if you truly believed that time is created and could cease? How will you live today as if time is a borrowed gift?

The First Objection: Impossibility of Influence
Al-Iji presents the first doubt: influence is inconceivable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection raised against the need of the contingent for an efficient cause, including its division into two possibilities and the three arguments that invalidate influence at the state of non-existence.

Core Idea
Influence at non-existence is impossible: it combines opposites, makes a negation an effect, and ignores continuity.

This lesson introduces the first doubt (shubha) from the deniers of causality. The objection targets the very concept of 'influence' (ta'thir), arguing that it is rationally inconceivable. By examining the two scenarios (influence at existence vs. non-existence) and the three arguments against the latter, we set the stage for understanding the refutations that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possibilities
The objection splits influence into two cases: at the state of existence of the effect, or at its state of non-existence. The first is immediately rejected.
Note that the 'influence' here means bringing into existence (ijad).
2
Understand the rejection of influence at existence
If influence occurs when the effect already exists, it would be 'bringing the existent into existence' (tahsil al-hasil), which is impossible.
3
Analyze the three arguments against influence at non-existence
The text provides three distinct reasons why influence cannot occur at the state of non-existence. Read each carefully.
These are: (1) collection of opposites, (2) non-existence as pure negation unfitted for effect, (3) non-existence as continuous state.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The objection concludes that influence is rationally impossible, and therefore the need for a cause is not established. However, this is a doubt to be refuted later.
Notice the phrase 'فقد بطل كون التأثير في الموجود حال العدم بوجوه ثلاثة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشبه المنكرين لكون الممكن محتاجاً إلى المؤثر عدة أي متعددة كثيرة : الشبهة الأولى : أن احتياجه إلى مؤثر سواء كان ذلك الاحتياج لإمكانه أو لغيره إنما يتحقق إذا أمكن تأثير شيء في شيء لكنه غير معقول ; إذ التأثير في الوجود مثلا إما حال الوجود أي وجود الأثر ، وهو محال ; لأنه إيجاد الموجود وتحصيل الحاصل، وإما حال العدم وهو باطل أيضاً ; لأنه جمع للنقيضين، وذلك لأن وجود الأثر مع التأثير لا يتخلف عنه أصلا، كالانكسار مع الكسر؛ والوجود مع الإيجاد ولما فرض أن التأثير في الوجود أعني الإيجاد إنما هو حال العدم كان وجود الأثر أيضا في تلك الحال فيجتمع وجود الأثر وعدمه معا ولأنه أي الأثر حال عدمه نفي محض، فلا يصلح هو في هذه الحالة أن يكون أثرا للموجد، وإذ لا أثر له فلا تأثير ولا إيجاد منه حينئذ، ولأنه أعني الأثر حال عدمه مستمر على ما كان عليه قبل أن يتعلق به تأثير وإيجاد، فلا يستند هو مع كونه مستمرا على حالته السابقة على الإيجاد إلى مؤثر الوجود، فقد بطل كون التأثير في الموجود حال العدم بوجوه ثلاثة، وإن شئت نفي يجوزه العقل لا أنه يتصوره، وإلا لما أمكن إبطاله، وإذا لم يجوز العقل التأثير لا يمكن الاحتياج إلى المؤثر من حيث إنه مؤثر. قوله : في الوجود والقرينة على هذا التخصيص قوله : لأنه إيجاد الموجود، وقوله : لأنه للنقيضين فإنه إذاكان التأثير في العدم كان الأمر بالعكس. قوله: أي الأثر يعني أن الضمير راجع إلى الأثر المفهوم مما تقدم، دون العدم، لأن الكلام في التأثير في الوجود حال عدم الأثر، وكون العدم غير صالح لكونه أثرا لا يقدح في ذلك فلا يتم التقريب . قوله : نفي محض لا تميز له أصلاً . قوله : فلا يصلح إذ الصلاحية فرع الامتياز، لا لأنه يلزم جمع النقيضين، لأن ذلك في كونه أثرا لا في صلاحيته، فلا يكون هذا الوجه راجعا إلى الأول كما وهم . قوله : ولأنه أعني الأثر حال عدمه إرجاع الضمير إلى الأثر المفهوم من التأثير لا إلى المذكور صريحاً، دفع لاعتراض شارح المقاصد بأن الكلام في التأثير بمعنى الإيجاد، وإلا لما صح أن التأثير حال الوجود إيجاد للوجود ، وحال العدم جمع للنقيضين، فالقول بأن العدم نفي صرف لا يصلح أثرا ليس كما ينبغي، لكن لا يخفى أن هذا الوجه حينئذ كما نقل من الشارح راجع إلى الوجه السابق عليه إذ ماله إلى اجتماع النقيضين ولو ذكر هذا الوجه في نفي التأثير في العدم حال العدم لكان وجها مستقلا، إذ العدم نفي محض لا يصلح لتأثر الموثر مطلقا . قوله : على ما كان عليه قبل أن يتعلق به تأثير وإيجاد في هذا التقرير دفع لاعتراض شارح المقاصد بأن الوجه الثالث ليس بتام؛ لأن العدم ربما كان حادثا لا مستمرا. ووجه الدفع أن العدم الحادث يصدق عليه أنه مستمر على ما كان عليه قبل أن يتعلق بالأثر إيجاد، وإن لم يصدق أنه مستمر بمعنى أنه غير مسبوق بالوجود ، وليس المراد بالاستمرار المعنى الثاني، كما توهم المعترض.
And the doubts of the deniers of the contingent being in need of the efficient cause are many: the first doubt: that its need for an efficient cause, whether that need is due to its possibility or something else, is only realized if an influence of a thing in a thing is possible, but it is inconceivable; for influence upon existence, for example, is either at the state of existence—i.e., the existence of the effect—and that is impossible because it would be bringing the existent into existence and obtaining what is already obtained; or at the state of non-existence, and that is also invalid because it is a collection of opposites. And that is because the existence of the effect with influence does not lag behind it at all, like breaking with breaking, and existence with bringing into existence. And when it is assumed that influence upon existence, i.e., bringing into existence, is only at the state of non-existence, then the existence of the effect would also be in that state, so the existence and non-existence of the effect would be combined together. And because it—i.e., the effect in its state of non-existence—is pure negation, so it is not fit in this state to be an effect of the agent. And since there is no effect for him, then there is no influence and no bringing into existence from him then. And because it—i.e., the effect in its state of non-existence—is continuous upon what it was before influence and bringing into existence were attached to it, so it is not attributed, while being continuous upon its previous state before bringing into existence, to an efficient cause of existence. So the notion of influence upon the existent at the state of non-existence is invalidated by three aspects, and if you wish, [you may say] negation: the intellect deems it possible, not that it conceives it; otherwise it could not be invalidated. And if the intellect does not deem influence possible, then the need for the efficient cause insofar as it is an efficient cause is not possible. His saying: 'in existence' and the evidence for this specification is his saying: 'because it would be bringing the existent into existence', and his saying: 'because it is a collection of opposites', for if the influence were in non-existence, the matter would be the opposite. His saying: 'i.e., the effect' means that the pronoun refers to the effect understood from what preceded, not to non-existence, because the discussion is about influence upon existence at the state of non-existence of the effect. And the fact that non-existence is not fit to be an effect does not harm that, so the approximation is not complete. His saying: 'pure negation' with no distinction at all. His saying: 'so it is not fit' since fitness is a branch of distinction, not because it necessitates the collection of opposites, for that is in its being an effect, not in its fitness, so this aspect is not reducible to the first as was imagined. His saying: 'and because it, i.e., the effect in its state of non-existence'—returning the pronoun to the effect understood from the term 'influence', not to the explicit mention—is a rebuttal of the objection of the author of al-Maqasid that the discussion is about influence in the sense of bringing into existence; otherwise, it would not be correct that influence at the state of existence is bringing the existent into existence, and at the state of non-existence is a collection of opposites. So the statement that non-existence is pure negation and not fit to be an effect is not as it should be. But it is not hidden that this aspect then, as transmitted from the commentator, is reducible to the preceding aspect, for it leads to the collection of opposites. And if this aspect were mentioned in negating influence on non-existence at the state of non-existence, it would be an independent aspect, for non-existence is pure negation, absolutely not fit to be affected by the agent. His saying: 'upon what it was before influence and bringing into existence were attached to it' in this formulation is a rebuttal of the objection of the author of al-Maqasid that the third aspect is not complete, because non-existence might be originated, not continuous. And the manner of rebuttal is that originated non-existence is truthfully described as continuous upon what it was before an act of bringing into existence was attached to the effect, even if it is not truthfully described as continuous in the sense of not being preceded by existence. And what is meant by continuity is not the second meaning, as the objector imagined.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection raised by deniers of the contingent's need for an efficient cause. The objection begins by asserting that the need for a cause is only relevant if influence (تأثير) of one thing upon another is possible; but influence, they argue, is rationally inconceivable. The author then explains their reasoning: influence upon an effect's existence must occur either when the effect exists or when it does not. The first case is impossible because it would mean bringing the already existent into existence (تحصيل الحاصل). The second case is also impossible because it would entail a collection of opposites (جمع النقيضين): if influence occurs at the state of non-existence, then the effect must exist (since the influence is bringing it into existence) and also not exist at that same moment. The objection then adds two more arguments: (1) non-existence is pure negation (نفي محض) and thus cannot serve as an effect, and (2) non-existence is continuous (مستمر) in its state, so it does not receive any new attribution to a cause. The passage concludes that influence on existence at the state of non-existence is invalidated by three aspects. The following commentary (قوله) clarifies various points: it explains the pronoun references, distinguishes between intellectual possibility and conceivability, argues that fitness requires distinction (امتياز), and responds to potential objections from the author of al-Maqasid regarding the independence of the third aspect and the meaning of continuity. The commentator insists that the third aspect does not reduce to the first, and that originated non-existence still counts as continuous in the relevant sense.

نفي محض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection is a final proof against causality.
Correction: It is presented as a doubt (shubha) to be addressed by later refutation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first objection argues that influence (ta'thir) is impossible because it would either be obtaining the already obtained or involve a contradiction when applied to non-existence.

?

How does this lesson's proof that influence is rationally inconceivable shake that assumption, and what does it demand of your trust in Allah as the ultimate agent?
?

Reflect on a specific moment when you had to choose between rational doubt and faith. What did that choice reveal about your heart's posture toward Allah?
?

Do you truly believe that Allah's creative act does not fall under this rational objection? Or do you secretly harbor doubt? Confront that doubt now and commit to asking Allah for certainty in His absolute power.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Three Proofs Against Affecting Nonexistent Things
Enumeration of three proofs against influence on a nonexistent thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational proofs that demonstrate the impossibility of an agent influencing a thing while that thing is non-existent.

Core Idea
An agent cannot create what already exists nor create while the thing is absent.

This lesson presents three arguments from Islamic theology that establish a fundamental principle of causality: influence (ta'thīr) cannot occur on a thing in its state of non-existence. The commentator explains why each scenario leads to an absurdity, such as creating what already exists or combining opposites. These proofs are essential for understanding the relationship between the Creator and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three proofs
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that the author enumerates three distinct arguments against influence in a state of non-existence.
The proofs are marked by 'إما' and 'وإما' and later 'ولأنه'.
2
Understand the first proof: creating the existent
The first proof states that if influence occurs while the effect already exists, it would be 'ījād al-mawjūd' – creating what already exists – which is impossible (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil).
3
Understand the second proof: combination of opposites
The second proof argues that if influence occurs while the effect is non-existent, the effect would both exist (due to influence) and be non-existent, which is a contradiction (jamʿ al-naqīḍayn).
4
Understand the third proof: the effect is pure negation
The third proof holds that the effect in its state of non-existence is pure negation (nafy maḥḍ), so it cannot serve as an effect; also it is continuous and thus not attributable to an agent.
The commentary notes this proof reduces to the second.

أثراً لا يقدح في ذلك فلا يتم التقريب . قوله : ( نفي (محض) لا تميز له أصلاً . قوله : ( فلا يصلح الخ) إذ الصلاحية فرع الامتياز، لا لأنه يلزم جمع النقيضين، لأن ذلك : في كونه أثراً لا في صلاحيته، فلا يكون الإيجاد، وإلا لما صح أن التأثير حال الوجود إيجاد للوجود ، وحال العدم جمع للنقيضين، فالقول بأن العدم نفي صرف لا يصلح أثراً ليس كما ينبغي، لكن لا يخفى أن هذا الوجه حينئذ كما نقل من الشارح راجع إلى الوجه Something in something but it is not intelligible; for influence (al-ta'thīr) in existence, for example, is either in the state of existence, i.e., the existence of the effect, and it is impossible because it is the creation of the existent (ījād al-mawjūd) and obtaining what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), or in the state of non-existence, and it is also false because it is a combination of opposites (jamʿ al-naqīḍayn). This is because the existence of the effect together with influence does not lag behind it at all, like breaking with breaking, and existence with creation. When it is assumed that influence in existence, namely creation, is only in the state of non-existence, then the existence of the effect is also in that state, so the existence of the effect and its non-existence come together. And because it (i.e., the effect in the state of its non-existence) is pure negation, it is not suitable in this state to be an effect for the agent, and since there is no effect for him, there is no influence and no creation from him then. And because it (i.e., the effect in the state of its non-existence) is continuous upon what it was before being connected to influence and creation, it is not attributed, while being continuous upon its prior state before creation, to an agent of existence. Thus the claim of influence in existence in the state of non-existence is refuted in three ways. If you wish, it is a negation that reason allows, not that it conceives it; otherwise it would not be possible to refute it. And if reason does not allow influence, the need for an agent insofar as it is an agent cannot be established. His statement: 'in existence' and the evidence for this specification is his statement: 'because it is the creation of the existent' and his statement: 'because of the two opposites' – for if influence were in non-existence, the matter would be the opposite. His statement: 'i.e., the effect etc.' means that the pronoun refers to the effect understood from what preceded, not to non-existence, because the discussion is about influence in existence in the state of non-existence of the effect, and the fact that non-existence is not suitable to be an effect does not harm that, so the approximation is not complete. His statement: 'pure negation' has no distinction at all. His statement: 'so it is not suitable etc.' because suitability is a branch of distinction, not because it entails combination of opposites, for that is in its being an effect, not in its suitability, so this aspect is not reducible to the first as was imagined. His statement: 'and because it, I mean the effect in the state of its non-existence etc.' – returning the pronoun to the effect understood from influence, not to what is explicitly mentioned, is a refutation of the objection of the commentator of al-Maqāṣid that the discussion is about influence in the sense of creation; otherwise it would not be correct that influence in the state of existence is creation of existence, and in the state of non-existence is combination of opposites. So the statement that non-existence is pure negation not suitable to be an effect is not as it should be. However, it is not hidden that this aspect then, as narrated from the commentator, is reducible to the previous aspect, for it leads to combination of opposites. And if this aspect were mentioned in denying influence in non-existence in the state of non-existence, it would be an independent aspect, for non-existence is pure negation not suitable for being affected by an agent at all. His statement: 'upon what it was before being connected to influence and creation' – in this exposition is a refutation of the objection of the commentator of al-Maqāṣid that the third aspect is not complete because non-existence might be transient, not continuous. The refutation is that transient non-existence is described as continuous upon what it was before being connected to the effect by creation, even if it is not described as continuous in the sense of being un preceded by existence; and the intended meaning of continuity is not the second meaning, as the objector imagined. Influence in non-existence: I say: influence is either in it in the state of the effect being non-existent, and it is obtaining what is already obtained, or in the state of it being existent, and it is combination of opposites, and also it is in the state of existence not suitable as an effect for the non-existent, and also it is then continuous upon what it was before being connected to non-existence, so it is not attributed to an agent of non-existence.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary presenting and explaining three arguments (wujūh) that demonstrate the impossibility of influence (ta'thīr) on something that exists at the time when it is non-existent. The author begins by stating that the notion of 'something in something' is unintelligible. He then lays out the three arguments: First, if the influence (here meaning creation, ījād) occurs while the effect already exists, it is impossible because it would be creating what already exists (ījād al-mawjūd), i.e., obtaining what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). Second, if the influence occurs while the effect is non-existent, it would combine existence and non-existence simultaneously, since the effect would exist by virtue of the influence and yet be non-existent. Third, the effect in its state of non-existence is pure negation (nafy maḥḍ), so it cannot serve as an effect; furthermore, it is continuous in its state and thus cannot be attributed to an agent of existence. The commentator then provides detailed glosses on each part, clarifying pronoun references, defending against objections from other commentators (especially the commentator of al-Maqāṣid), and explaining why certain interpretations are misguided. He concludes by summarizing the three ways and noting that the non-existence of the effect is not suitable for being affected.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three proofs are completely independent and distinct.
Correction: The third proof is actually reducible to the second (combination of opposites), as the commentary indicates.

Distinguishing Prior and Concurrent Existence in Creation
Answer distinguishing two types of existence in creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between prior and concurrent existence and why only creation with prior existence is impossible.

Core Idea
Creation is impossible only when existence precedes the act; concurrent existence is perfectly possible.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that seemed to make all creation impossible. He clarifies that the impossibility is limited to a specific case: creating something that already exists with a prior existence. He then shows that if the objection were valid, it would negate all observable changes in the world, which is absurd. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the main answer
Read the Arabic passage once. Focus on the two cases: prior existence (impossible) and concurrent existence (possible).
Notice the phrase 'تحصيل الحاصل' (obtaining what is obtained).
2
Understand the impossibility of prior existence
The author explains that creating something with a prior existence is impossible because it would be obtaining what is already obtained, which is self-evidently absurd.
3
See the concurrent existence case
The author provides an analogy: the movement of the hand (cause) and the movement of the key (effect) occur simultaneously, so obtaining the effect at the same time as the cause is not impossible.
4
Follow the refutation
If the opponent's generalization were true, no accidental attribute (like heat or sound) could ever be generated, because its generation would face the same dilemma. But we observe such attributes, so the argument fails.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Note the intuitive proof
The occurrence of attributes and their dependence on a cause is intuitive (badīhī), so the opponent's argument is definitively refuted.
Even if the exact cause is disputed, the fact of occurrence is undeniable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن المحال إيجاد ما هو موجود بوجود قبل) أي قبل الإيجاد فإنه تحصيل لما كان حاصلا قبل هذا التحصيل، وهو محال بديهة. ( وإلا فالإيجاد للموجود ) بوجود مقارن للإيجاد ؛ لأن حصول الأثر مع التأثير زماناً، وذلك تحصيل للحاصل بهذا التحصيل ولا استحالة فيه. ( ولو صح ما ذكرتم لزم أن لا يحدث (صفة) في نفسها ( أصلا ، كهذه السخونة وهذا الصوت )؛ لأن حدوثها إما حال أو عدمها وهو اجتماع النقيضين أعني الوجود والعدم وإما حال وجودها وهو حصول الحاصل. نقول : لزم أن لا يحدث صفة في شيء من مؤثر يحدثها؛ لأن إحداثها وإيجادها إما حال الوجود أو العدم، وكلاهما باطل، لكن حدوث هذه الصفات واستنادها إلى أمر يحدثها أمر بديهي فانتقض دليلكم قطعاً. والحل : أن قوله : ( لا يصلح أثراً للمعدوم لأنه موجود وأثر المعدوم يكون معدوماً. قوله : ( حينئذ) ظرف لنفي الأثر والتأثير على التنازع . قوله : (مستمر على ما كان (عليه لأن المفروض أن التأثير في الوجود حال العدم السابق على الوجود وليس المراد بالمستمر العدم الذي لا ابتداء له، إذ لا يتعلق غرضنا بكونه أزلياً، ولا يتوقف نفي الإيجاد حال العدم عليه . قوله : ( أن المحال (الخ أي المحال مقصور على هذا الإيجاد. قوله : ( وهو محال بديهة إذ لا يكون التحصيل حينئذ تحصيلاً. قوله : ( وألا) (الخ) أي لا يكون المحال مقصوراً على هذا الإيجاد لم يصح القول باستحالة إيجاد الموجود بوجود مقارن للإيجاد، لا استحالة فيه بناء على أن حصول الأثر مع التأثير زماناً، كما يشاهد ذلك في حركة اليد وحركة المفتاح. وإذا تقرر ذلك فنقول : إن أراد المستدل من إيجاد الموجود النوع الأول منعنا الملازمة، لكونه إيجاداً للموجود بهذا الوجود، وإن أراد الثاني أو الأعم نمنع بطلان التالي لأن المحال هو النوع الأول. ولما كان سند المنعين المذكورين مستفاداً من تلك المقدمة تعرض المجيب لبيانها واكتفى بها لانسياق الذهن إلى المنعين المذكورين منها بلا كلفة، فتدبر، فإنه قد تحير في حل هذه العبارة الناظرون . قوله : ( بديهي) وإن اختلف في تعيين ذلك المؤثر المحدث . قوله : ( فانتقض الخ لاستلزامه المحال وهو الحكم بخلاف ما تشهد به البديهة. قوله : ( أما حال كون الأثر معدوماً) المراد من الأثر هاهنا هو الماهية الممكنة باعتبار العدم لا العدم نفسه، كما أن المراد بالأثر سابقاً هو تلك الماهية باعتبار الوجود لا الوجود نفسه، فلا يرد أن معدومية الأثر الذي هو العدم يستلزم الوجود، فلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل كما ظن .
The answer: That which is impossible is the creation of what is existent with a prior existence (bi-wujūd qabl), i.e., before creation, for it is obtaining what was already obtained before this obtaining, and it is impossible by intuition. Otherwise, the creation of the existent with an existence concurrent with creation (bi-wujūd muqārin li-l-ījād) is not impossible, because the occurrence of the effect together with influence is temporal, and that is obtaining what is obtained by this obtaining, and there is no impossibility in it. And if what you mentioned were correct, then it would follow that no attribute would ever occur in itself, like this heat and this sound; because their occurrence is either in the state of its non-existence, which is the combination of opposites – namely existence and non-existence – or in the state of its existence, which is obtaining what is already obtained. We say: It would follow that no attribute would occur in anything from an agent that causes it, because its causing and creation are either in the state of existence or non-existence, and both are false; but the occurrence of these attributes and their attribution to something that causes them is intuitive, so your argument is definitively refuted. The solution: That his statement: 'it is not suitable as an effect for the non-existent because it is existent, and the effect of the non-existent is non-existent.' His statement: 'then' is an adverb for negating the effect and influence in a way that they compete. His statement: 'continuous upon what it was' because it is assumed that influence in existence is in the state of non-existence prior to existence, and by 'continuous' is not meant the non-existence that has no beginning, for our purpose does not depend on its being eternal, nor does the negation of creation in the state of non-existence depend on it. His statement: 'that which is impossible etc.' i.e., the impossible is limited to this creation. His statement: 'and it is impossible by intuition' because the obtaining then is not an obtaining. His statement: 'otherwise etc.' i.e., if the impossible were not limited to this creation, then the statement that it is impossible to create the existent with an existence concurrent with creation would not be valid; there is no impossibility in it based on the fact that the effect occurs with influence temporally, as is seen in the movement of the hand and the movement of the key. And when this is established, we say: if the arguer means by 'creation of the existent' the first type, we deny the implication, because it is creation of the existent with this existence; and if he means the second type or the general, we deny the falsehood of the consequent, because the impossible is only the first type. And since the basis of the two mentioned denials is derived from that premise, the respondent undertook to clarify it and sufficed with it for the mind to be led to the two mentioned denials from it without difficulty. So ponder, for the observers have been perplexed in solving this expression. His statement: 'intuitive' – even if they differ in specifying that causing agent. His statement: 'so it is refuted etc.' because it entails what is impossible, namely judging contrary to what intuition testifies to. His statement: 'As for the state of the effect being non-existent' – what is meant by 'effect' here is the possible quiddity considered as non-existent, not non-existence itself, just as what was meant by 'effect' previously was that quiddity considered as existent, not existence itself. So it does not follow that the non-existence of the effect which is non-existence entails existence, so obtaining what is already obtained does not necessarily follow, as was imagined.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is the answer (jawāb) to the objection raised by the earlier arguments. The author begins by clarifying that the impossibility (muḥāl) is specifically limited to the creation of something that already exists with a prior existence (wujūd qabl), because that would be obtaining what is already obtained. However, creating something with an existence that is concurrent with the act of creation (wujūd muqārin) is not impossible, because the effect and the influence occur simultaneously in time, like the movement of the hand causing the movement of a key. The author then argues that if the opponent's reasoning were sound, it would entail that no accidental attributes (like heat or sound) could ever be generated, since their emergence would be subject to the same dilemma. But these attributes do occur, and their dependence on a cause is intuitive (badīhī), so the argument is self-refuting. The rest of the commentary explains the nuances of the phrasing, clarifies the referents of pronouns, and defends the answer against potential misunderstandings. A key distinction is drawn between the effect considered as a possible quiddity (māhiyya mumkina) under the aspect of non-existence versus under the aspect of existence, thereby avoiding the charge of obtaining what is already obtained.

بوجود قبل
بوجود مقارن
تحصيل الحاصل
بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that creating any existent is impossible.
Correction: The answer shows it is only impossible when the existence precedes the act of creation; concurrent existence is perfectly possible.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, when is creating something that exists impossible?
A) When the existence is concurrent with the creation.
B) When the existence precedes the creation.
C) When the existence is after the creation.
D) When the thing is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of creating an existent is limited to prior existence; concurrent existence is possible and observed in all causal generation.

Influence and the Two Types of Existence
Objection and answer regarding causal influence in non-existence or existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against causal influence and the answer that distinguishes between preceding and concomitant existence.

Core Idea
Causal influence is impossible only if the effect's existence precedes the act.

This passage presents a critical objection to the possibility of causal influence: if the effect is non-existent, influence would be obtaining the obtained; if it is existent, influence would combine opposites. The answer resolves this by distinguishing between existence that precedes the act (impossible) and existence that is simultaneous (possible).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the two cases: effect non-existent and effect existent. Understand why each is problematic according to the objector.
The term 'tahsil al-hasil' is key.
2
Examine the additional arguments
The objector adds that in existence, the effect is not suitable for a non-existent cause and it continues as before.
3
Study the answer's distinction
The answer clarifies that the impossibility applies only to existence that precedes the act, not to simultaneous existence.
Look for 'wujud qabl' and 'wujud muqarin'.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how the answer avoids the objection and why this distinction is important for establishing causal agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت : التأثير إما فيه حال كون الأثر معدوماً، وهو تحصيل الحاصل وإما حال كونه موجوداً وأنه جمع للنقيضين، وأيضاً هو حال الوجود لا يصلح أثراً للمعدوم، وأيضاً هو حينئذ مستمر على ما كان عليه قبل أن يتعلق به الإعدام فلا يستند إلى مؤثر العدم. ( والجواب : أن المحال إيجاد ما هو موجود بوجود قبل) أي قبل الإيجاد فإنه تحصيل لما كان حاصلا قبل هذا التحصيل، وهو محال بديهة. ( وإلا فالإيجاد للموجود ) بوجود مقارن للإيجاد ؛ لأن حصول الأثر مع التأثير زماناً، وذلك تحصيل للحاصل بهذا التحصيل ولا استحالة فيه.
I said: Influence is either in the state of the effect being non-existent – which is obtaining the obtained – or in the state of it being existent – and that is combining the two opposites. Also, in the state of existence it is not suitable as an effect for the non-existent, and also it is then continuing as it was before being connected to non-existence, so it is not attributed to the agent of non-existence. (The answer: That the impossible is bringing into existence what is existent with an existence that is before) i.e., before the bringing into existence, for it is obtaining what was already obtained before this obtaining, and that is self-evidently impossible. (Otherwise, bringing into existence is for the existent) with an existence concomitant with the bringing into existence, because obtaining the effect with the influence is simultaneous, and that is obtaining the obtained by this obtaining, and there is no impossibility in it.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the possibility of causal influence (ta'thīr) on a contingent being, followed by its answer. The objector argues that influence cannot occur in the state of non-existence of the effect because that would be 'obtaining the obtained' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), nor in the state of existence because that would combine two opposites (jam' al-naqīḍayn). Additionally, in the state of existence the effect is already existent and thus not suitable as an effect for a non-existent cause, and it would be continuing as before, so it cannot be attributed to an agent of non-existence. The answer distinguishes between two types of existence: existence that precedes the act of bringing into existence (ījād) – which would indeed be obtaining the obtained and is impossible – and existence that is concomitant with the act (wujūd muqārin li-l-ījād), i.e., simultaneous, which does not entail impossibility because the effect comes into being together with the influence.

وجود مقارن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: It is only impossible if the existence precedes the act; simultaneous existence is not impossible.

Checkpoint

A) Existence that precedes the act of bringing into existence
B) Existence that is simultaneous with the act
C) Both types of existence
D) Neither type of existence

Key Takeaway
Causal influence is impossible only if the effect's existence precedes the act; simultaneous existence with the act is not problematic.

?

The Self-Evident Arising of Attributes
Objection that attributes cannot arise, refuted by self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opponent's argument against the possibility of attribute generation is refuted by appealing to self-evident experience.

Core Idea
The arising of attributes is self-evident; no logical trick can overturn what we directly experience.

The opponent extends the earlier argument to claim that no attribute can ever arise, because generation would require a logical impossibility. The answer cuts straight to experience: we directly observe attributes like heat and sound arising, and we know they depend on a cause. This immediate self-evident knowledge refutes the abstract proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the objection and the answer.
Notice the structure: the opponent's premise, the dilemma, the conclusion, then the refutation.
2
Analyze the opponent's dilemma
The opponent argues that an attribute cannot arise because its arising would either be in a state of non-existence (contradiction) or in a state of existence (redundancy). Understand why both horns are considered impossible.
3
See the answer's appeal to self-evidence
The response does not counter the logic directly but says the arising of attributes is self-evident (badīhī). Reflect on what 'self-evident' means in this context.
In kalam, self-evident truths are foundational and need no proof.
4
Recognize the method of refutation
The refutation works by showing that the opponent's conclusion contradicts direct observation, which is a stronger epistemic authority. This is a common strategy in Islamic theology.
The phrase 'فانتقض دليلكم قطعاً' indicates a definitive refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ولو صح ما ذكرتم لزم أن لا يحدث (صفة) في نفسها ( أصلا ، كهذه السخونة وهذا الصوت ) ؛ لأن حدوثها إما حال أو عدمها وهو اجتماع النقيضين أعني الوجود والعدم وإما حال وجودها وهو حصول الحاصل. نقول : لزم أن لا يحدث صفة في شيء من مؤثر يحدثها؛ لأن إحداثها وإيجادها إما حال الوجود أو العدم، وكلاهما باطل، لكن حدوث هذه الصفات واستنادها إلى أمر يحدثها أمر بديهي فانتقض دليلكم قطعاً.
(And if what you mentioned were correct, it would entail that no attribute arises in itself at all, such as this heat and this sound; because their arising is either in the state of its non-existence – which is combining the two opposites, namely existence and non-existence – or in the state of its existence – which is obtaining the obtained. We say: It would entail that no attribute arises in anything from any agent that brings it about, because its bringing about and bringing into existence is either in the state of existence or non-existence, and both are invalid. But the arising of these attributes and their being attributed to something that brings them about is self-evident, so your proof is refuted definitively.)
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection extending the first argument to the case of attributes (ṣifāt) such as heat and sound. The opponent argues that if the previous reasoning were correct, then no attribute could ever arise, because its arising would either be in the state of its non-existence (combining opposites) or in the state of its existence (obtaining the obtained). The answer is a simple refutation: the arising of attributes and their dependence on a cause is self-evident (badīhī), so the opponent's proof is contradicted by manifest experience. The commentator notes that this refutation is valid because the opponent's argument leads to an absurd conclusion that contradicts self-evident truth.

Misconception: The opponent's argument is logically airtight and must be refuted by equally logical reasoning.
Correction: The refutation appeals to self-evident experience (badīhī), which is a legitimate and stronger epistemic ground in kalam. Direct observation overrides abstract logical dilemmas.

Necessity Conditional on the Predicate: The Final Solution to the Causality Objection
Sanusi provides the final solution: the impossibility of influence is conditional, not absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of 'necessity conditional on the predicate' (ḍarūra bi-sharṭ al-maḥmūl) and how it resolves the objection that a cause cannot influence an effect in either its existence or non-existence.

Core Idea
Impossibility conditional on a state does not negate intrinsic possibility.

The objection that a cause cannot influence an effect because influence is impossible in both the state of existence and non-existence seems to challenge causality. The final solution presented here introduces a key distinction: the impossibility is conditional on the predicate (existence or non-existence), not an absolute impossibility. This lesson unpacks that solution, showing how it preserves the intrinsic possibility of the contingent being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the technical term 'ḍarūra bi-sharṭ al-maḥmūl' (necessity conditional on the predicate). This is the core concept.
Pay attention to how the passage distinguishes conditional necessity from absolute necessity.
2
Understand conditional necessity vs. intrinsic possibility
The passage explains that the impossibility of influence under the condition of existence or non-existence is a conditional necessity. This does not negate intrinsic possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī), which considers the essence alone without any additional attributes.
Intrinsic possibility is about the essence itself, not under any condition.
3
Analyze the reformulation of the objection
The passage reinterprets the opponent's premise: if 'influence is either in existence or non-existence' means conditional on those states, the exclusive disjunction is false. If it means in the time of existence or non-existence, it is possible. This shows the solution's scope.
The reformulation clarifies that the objection only works if you assume a condition, which is not required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحل : أن ذلك الذي ذكرتموه من استحالة التأثير حال الوجود أو حال العدم، ضرورة بشرط المحمول، فإن التأثير في وجود الأثر بشرط الوجود أو بشرط العدم محال، فسلب التأثير في الوجود مثلا ضروري بشرط اتصاف الأثر بالوجود أو العدم، ومثل ذلك يسمى ضرورة بشرط المحمول، (وهو) أي هذا المذكور أعني الضرورة المشروطة بالمحمول ( لا ينافي الإمكان (الذاتي ) ؛ لأن الملاحظ فيه الذات دون مالها من الصفات، فامتناع التأثير بشرط إحدى هاتين الصفتين لا ينافي إمكانه بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن في زمان كل واحدة منهما وتحريره أن يقال قولك التأثير إما حال الوجود أو حال العدم وكلاهما باطل إن أردت به التأثير إما بشرط الوجود أو بشرط العدم فالحصر ممنوع فإن التأثير في ذات الممكن من حيث هو لا بشرط الوجود، ولا بشرط العدم، وإن أردت به أنه في زمان الوجود أو زمان العدم اخترنا أنه في زمان
And the solution: That what you mentioned – the impossibility of influence in the state of existence or the state of non-existence – is a necessity conditional on the predicate (ḍarūra bi-sharṭ al-maḥmūl). For influence in the existence of the effect conditional on existence or conditional on non-existence is impossible. So the negation of influence in existence, for example, is necessary conditional on the effect being characterized by existence or non-existence. And that like is called 'necessity conditional on the predicate'. (And it) i.e., this mentioned, namely the necessity conditioned by the predicate, (does not negate intrinsic possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī)), because what is considered therein is the essence without its attributes. So the impossibility of influence conditional on one of these two attributes does not negate its possibility with respect to the essence of the contingent in the time of each of them. And its formulation is that your saying 'influence is either in the state of existence or in the state of non-existence, and both are invalid' – if you mean by it influence either conditional on existence or conditional on non-existence, then the exclusive disjunction is denied, because influence is in the essence of the contingent as such, not conditional on existence and not conditional on non-existence. And if you mean by it that it is in the time of existence or the time of non-existence, we choose that it is in the time...
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage provides the final, complete solution to the original objection. It explains that the impossibility of influence in the state of existence or non-existence is a 'necessity conditional on the predicate' (ḍarūra bi-sharṭ al-maḥmūl). This means that the impossibility is only when the effect is considered under the condition of being existent or non-existent. Such a conditional necessity does not negate the intrinsic possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī) of the contingent being, because intrinsic possibility considers the essence (dhāt) alone, without any additional attributes. Therefore, the exclusive disjunction (al-ḥaṣr) between influence in existence or non-existence is false if taken with the condition. The correct view is that influence occurs in the essence of the contingent as such, without any condition, and can be in the time of existence or non-existence. The commentator adds that this solution cuts off the root of the doubt.

إمكان ذاتي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of influence in existence or non-existence is absolute, negating causality entirely.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the impossibility is conditional on the predicate (existence or non-existence), not an intrinsic impossibility of the essence. Intrinsic possibility remains, so causality is not negated.

Checkpoint

B) The impossibility of influence when the effect is considered under the condition of existence or non-existence
C) The self-evident arising of attributes
D) The simultaneous existence of cause and effect

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of influence under the condition of existence or non-existence does not undermine the intrinsic possibility of the contingent, because intrinsic possibility considers the essence alone.

Second Doubt: The Impossibility of Influence and Its Answer
States and refutes the second doubt that influence is impossible in three aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second doubt against the need for an influencer for the possible, its threefold argument, and the correct answer that influence is on particular existence.

Core Idea
Influence is on particular existence, not essence or absolute existence.

The second doubt attempts to prove that the possible does not need an influencer by arguing that influence is impossible in all respects. This passage presents that argument and then offers the crucial answer: influence is on the particular existence of each thing, which is the contingent aspect that needs a cause. Let's unpack the three aspects and see why the answer resolves the doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the structure: the doubt states three impossible cases, then the answer.
The doubt uses 'إما في الماهية أو الوجود أو الموصوفية به'.
2
Identify the three aspects of influence
Find the three categories: essence (mahiyya), existence (wujud), and being qualified (mawsufiyya). Note why each is deemed impossible for influence.
Essence cannot be made itself; existence is a state; being qualified is non-existential.
3
Understand the logic of the doubt
The doubt argues that if influence is impossible in all three, then the possible is independent. See how each impossibility is argued.
This relies on earlier discussion about making the essence.
4
Contrast with the answer
The answer redirects influence to 'particular existence' (al-wujud al-khas). Understand why this avoids the earlier impossibilities.
Reference to earlier discussion that al-maj'ul is al-wujud al-khas.
5
Connect to the broader doctrine
Reflect on how this answer preserves the need for an influencer for contingent things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثانية) : وهي أيضاً دالة على أن الممكن غير محتاج إلى مؤثر، لا لإمكانه ولا لغيره؛ إذ ذلك فرع إمكان التأثير، وهو محال إذ ( التأثير إما في الماهية أو الوجود أو الموصوفية به؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن التأثير في شيء من هذه الثلاثة كانت الماهية الموجودة مستغنية عما فرض مؤثراً بالقياس إليها ، ( وقد بطلت ) هذه الأقسام كلها فيما مر؛ لأن جعل الماهية تلك الماهية محال، وكذا جعل الوجود وجوداً ، وأيضاً . حال فلا يقبل تأثيراً ، والموصوفية عدمية فلا تكون أثراً ) والجواب : أنه ) أي التأثير (في الوجود الخاص، أي) في الهويات كما مر من أن المجعول هو الوجود الخاص
(The second shubha): And it also indicates that the possible is not in need of an influencer, neither for its possibility nor for anything else, because that is a branch of the possibility of influence, and it is impossible, since influence is either in the essence (mahiyya) or existence (wujud) or being qualified (mawsufiyya) by it; because if influence is not in any of these three, then the existing essence would be independent of what is assumed to be an influencer with respect to it. And all these divisions have been invalidated in what has passed, because making the essence that essence is impossible, and likewise making existence existence, and also because it (existence) is a state (hal) and thus does not accept influence, and being qualified is non-existential (adamiyya) and thus is not an effect. And the answer: that (influence) is in the particular existence (al-wujud al-khas), i.e. in the identities (al-hawiyyat), as has passed that the made thing (al-maj'ul) is the particular existence.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the second shubha (doubt) against the need for an influencer (mu'aththir) for the possible (al-mumkin). The shubha argues that the possible is not in need of any influencer because influence would have to be in one of three aspects: the essence (mahiyya), existence (wujud), or being qualified (mawsufiyya) by existence. All three are claimed to be impossible: making the essence itself is impossible (as it would be making something itself), making existence is also impossible (since existence is a state (hal) and cannot receive influence), and being qualified is a non-existential (adami) attribute and thus cannot be an effect. Therefore, influence is impossible, so the possible does not require an influencer. The answer (al-jawab) to this shubha is that influence is in the particular existence (al-wujud al-khas), i.e., the individual identities (al-hawiyyat). This aligns with the earlier discussion that the made thing (al-maj'ul) is the particular existence, not the essence or absolute existence. Thus, influence is not in the essence (which is not made) nor in the absolute existence (which is a state), but in the concrete, particular existence that distinguishes each individual entity.

Key Terms
الماهية (mahiyya)
الوجود (wujud)
الموصوفية (mawsufiyya)
الوجود الخاص (al-wujud al-khas)
الهويات (al-hawiyyat)
المجعول (al-maj'ul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that influence is on the essence itself.
Correction: The answer clarifies that influence is on the particular existence, which is the contingent aspect, not the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the second shubha, why is influence in the essence (mahiyya) impossible?
A) Because the essence is a non-existential attribute.
B) Because making the essence that essence would be a tautology and impossible.
C) Because the essence does not exist.
D) Because influence only occurs in existence.

Key Takeaway
Influence is on particular existence, not on essence or absolute existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your very existence, in its particularity, is a direct act of Allah's influence, or do you take it for granted as a natural given? How does the concept of 'particular existence' as the object of divine influence change how you view your own identity and purpose?
?
When you reflect on your own essence and existence, do you realize that neither your essence nor your existence in general is directly made by you, but your particular being is constantly sustained by Allah? How does this make you feel about your dependency on Him, and what will you do today to live in awareness of that dependency?
?
The shubha argued that influence is impossible because it cannot affect essence or existence. But Allah's influence is on your particular existence. Do you live as if this particular you is a direct result of Allah's will, or do you behave as if your individuality is self-sufficient? What step can you take this week to acknowledge that your very identity is a gift from Him?

The Second Shubha: Where Does the Effecter Act?
Al-Jurjani presents a shubha on the impossibility of effect and answers by specifying effect in specific existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second shubha attempts to deny the need for a cause, and how the Ash‘arī answer locates the effecter's action in specific existence.

Core Idea
The effecter acts not on essence or absolute existence, but on specific existence.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection: if effect is impossible in essence, existence, or being-described, then the possible needs no cause. Al-Jurjani then offers the Ash‘arī response: effect is in specific existence, the individual identity of the thing. Understanding this distinction is key to the Ash‘arī theory of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice that the passage has two parts: the objection (shubha) and the answer.
The shubha is structured as a trilemma.
2
Identify the three impossible loci
The shubha claims effect must be in essence, existence, or being-described. For each, the passage gives a reason why it's impossible. List these reasons.
Essence: making it itself is circular; Existence: a state (hāl) cannot receive effect; Being-described: non-existent.
3
Understand the answer
The answer specifies that effect is in specific existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ), not the absolute existence or essence. The commentary clarifies that this means consequential bringing-into-existence (istitbā‘) of the individual identity.
This avoids the objections because specific existence is not a mere state, but the concrete particularized existence of a thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثانية) : وهي أيضاً دالة على أن الممكن غير محتاج إلى مؤثر، لا لإمكانه ولا لغيره؛ إذ ذلك فرع إمكان التأثير، وهو محال إذ ( التأثير إما في الماهية أو الوجود أو الموصوفية به؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن التأثير في شيء من هذه الثلاثة كانت الماهية الموجودة مستغنية عما فرض مؤثراً بالقياس إليها ، ( وقد بطلت ) هذه الأقسام كلها فيما مر؛ لأن جعل الماهية تلك الماهية محال، وكذا جعل الوجود وجوداً ، وأيضاً . حال فلا يقبل تأثيراً ، والموصوفية عدمية فلا تكون أثراً ) والجواب : أنه ) أي التأثير (في الوجود الخاص، أي) في الهويات كما مر من أن المجعول هو الوجود الخاص
. قوله : ( أي التأثير في الوجود الخاص) أي التأثير في الوجودات الخاصة التي هي الهويات، أعني الماهيات الشخصية بناء على رأي الشيخ الأشعري. ومعنى التأثير الاستتباع لا في جعل الماهية ماهية حتى يقال أنه لا يمكن توسط الجعل بين الشيء ونفسه ولا في الموصوفية التي اعتبارية، ولا في الوجود المطلق الذي زعمتموه أنه حال .
(The second [shubha]): And it also indicates that the possible is not in need of an effecter, neither for its possibility nor for anything else; because that [need] is a branch of the possibility of effect, and it is impossible since (effect is either in the essence, or existence, or being described by it; because if effect is not in any of these three, then the existing essence would be independent of what is assumed to be an effecter in relation to it, and these divisions are all invalid as has passed; because making the essence that essence is impossible, and likewise making existence existence, and also [because] a state (hāl) does not receive effect, and being described is non-existent so it cannot be an effect). And the answer: that [effect] is in specific existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ), i.e., in the identities (al-hawiyyāt) as has passed that what is made is specific existence. His statement: (i.e., effect in specific existence) i.e., effect in the particular existences which are the identities, meaning the individual essences according to the view of Shaykh al-Ash‘arī. And the meaning of effect is consequential bringing-into-existence (al-istitbā‘), not in making the essence an essence such that it be said that making cannot mediate between a thing and itself, nor in the being-described which is mental consideration (i‘tibāriyya), nor in the absolute existence which you claimed is a state (hāl).
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the second shubha (sophistical objection) regarding the need of the possible for a cause, specifically the impossibility of effect (ta’thīr). The shubha argues that the possible does not need an effecter because effect is impossible: it would have to be either in the essence (making it that essence, impossible), or in existence (making it existent, but existence is a state (hāl) that does not receive effect), or in being described (which is non-existent and thus cannot be an effect). Since all are impossible, the possible is independent of any effecter. The answer given is that effect is in the specific existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ)—the particularized existence that is the identity of the thing (hawiyya)—not in the essence or absolute existence. The commentary clarifies that, according to Ash‘arī, the effect is consequential bringing-into-existence (istitbā‘) of the specific existence, not making the essence an essence (which would be circular) nor in the mental attribute of being-described nor in the abstract existence (which is a state, hāl). Thus the objection is deflected by specifying the locus of effect.

وجود

وجود خاص
هويات
استتباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer denies the reality of effect altogether.
Correction: The answer does not deny effect; it relocates it to specific existence, affirming that the effecter brings the thing into existence as a particular individual, not by acting on the essence or absolute existence.

Checkpoint

B) In existence (wujūd) absolutely
C) In specific existence (al-wujūd al-khāṣṣ)
D) In being described (al-mawsūfiyya)

Key Takeaway
The effecter's effect is in the specific existence of a thing, not its essence or absolute existence, understood as consequential bringing-into-existence (istitbā‘).

Does your heart truly believe that your individual identity and every detail of your life is a direct act of God, not a product of impersonal forces? What would it mean to accept that the most specific aspects of your existence—your talents, your struggles, your circumstances—are intentionally caused by God?
?
The shubha argued that effect is impossible, leading to the claim that you need no effecter. In your own life, do you ever live as if you are self-sufficient, as if your being does not require constant divine support? Where today do you need to repent of that independence and acknowledge that your very existence is a continuous gift?
