Objection from Hadath of Sensible Attributes and Its Answer
Al-Jurjani presents an objection that the previous argument would negate hadath of attributes and answers by noting the extra nature of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani responds to the objection that his earlier argument would negate the temporal origination of sensible attributes by clarifying that the argument applies only to existence, not the essences.

Core Idea
The objection fails because the proof targets existence, not quiddity.

Al-Jurjani considers a potent counterexample: sensible attributes like heat and cold clearly arise from their causes. If his earlier reasoning—that effect in essence, existence, or description is impossible—were taken universally, it would deny this obvious fact. He then provides the answer by referencing the earlier investigation (taḥqīq) that existence is superadded to essence. This restricts the proof to the existence of attributes, leaving their essences untouched.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the counterexample: sensible attributes are originated, so the earlier argument would incorrectly deny their hadath.
2
Identify the three possible loci of effect
The objection says effect must be either in essence, existence, or being described by it, and all are impossible per the earlier proof. Understand why each is considered impossible.
3
Understand the answer's key distinction
The answer points out that the earlier proof assumed existence is extra to essence. Thus it only applies to the existence of attributes, not their essences. The objection fails because it tries to apply the proof to essences.
Essences are not 'made' in the sense of causation.
4
Grasp the importance of the restriction
Al-Jurjani explicitly includes the phrase 'from the one who originates them' to limit the argument's scope to the act of granting existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فينفي ما ذكرتموه ( الحدوث ) أي حدوث الصفات المحسوسة عمن يحدثها؛ لأن تأثيره إما في ماهيتها أو وجودها أو موصوفيتها به والكل باطل لما ذكرتم بعينه.
قوله : ( وقد سبق منا إلخ) التحقيق السابق مبني على كون الوجود زائداً على الماهية كما مر . قوله : ( عمن يحدثها اعتبر هذا التقييد ليكون الدليل المذكور جارياً في صورة النقض، ولولا اعتباره لا يمكن إجراؤه ، إذ لو قيل بحدوث ماهياتها لا يمكن إبطاله بأن جعل الماهية محال إذ لا جعل .
And also it negates what you mentioned (al-ḥudūth), i.e., the temporal origination of sensible attributes from the one who originates them; because His effect is either in their essence, or existence, or being described by it, and all are invalid according to what you mentioned exactly. His statement: (And there has preceded from us [the investigation]) the preceding investigation is based on existence being superadded to essence (zā’id ‘alā al-māhiyya) as has passed. His statement: (from the one who originates them) consider this restriction in order for the mentioned proof to apply in the case of the counterexample; and were it not for its consideration, it could not be applied, because if it were said that their essences are originated, it cannot be refuted on the grounds that making the essence [into that essence] is impossible, since there is no making.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (naqḍ) based on the undeniable fact that sensible attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maḥsūsa) come into existence from an originator (e.g., heat from fire). If the previous reasoning—that effect in essence/existence/description is impossible—were correct, it would also negate this obvious hadath (temporal origination). The answer, provided in the commentary, states that the earlier investigation (taḥqīq) about effect being in specific existence presupposes that existence is superadded (zā’id) to essence (i.e., an accident). Thus, when the objector speaks of the hadath of the quiddities themselves (not their existence), the refutation does not apply because no 'making' of essence is involved—essences are not made. The restriction 'from the one who originates them' ensures the argument targets the act of causing existence, not the essence per se.

صفات محسوسة
نقض
وجود زائد على الماهية
جعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The earlier argument implies that sensible attributes cannot originate at all.
Correction: The argument only covers the case where existence is superadded to essence; when we speak of the hadath of attributes, we are referring to their existence, not their quiddity. So the argument does not deny the obvious hadath of sensible attributes.

Checkpoint
Why does the objection from hadath of sensible attributes fail according to the answer?
A) Because hadath is impossible in itself
B) Because sensible attributes are eternal
C) Because the earlier argument assumed existence is superadded to essence, so it only concerns existence, not essence
D) Because the objector misquoted the earlier argument

Key Takeaway
The argument about effect in essence/existence/description does not negate the hadath of sensible attributes because it concerns only their existence (which is superadded to essence), not their essences.

The objector confused the scope of the proof. Have you ever misunderstood a theological truth because you applied it beyond its intended scope? Where in your understanding of Allah's attributes might you be overextending a concept? Commit to revisiting that area with the correct distinction.
?

Your bad habits have an essence (what they are) but their existence in your life is renewed each time you choose them. The Third Shubha: Infinite Regress of Need and Effectivity
Al-Jurjani presents the third shubha on infinite regress of need and effectivity and the answer denying their real existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third shubha attempts to prove that possible beings do not need a cause, and why the answer denies that need and effectivity are real external attributes.

Core Idea
Need and effectivity are not real entities; they are relational descriptions.

Al-Jurjani now presents the third shubha, which targets the very notions of need and effectivity. The opponent argues that if these were real attributes, they would cause an infinite regress; if they are not, then possible beings cannot truly need a cause. The answer, however, distinguishes between real existents and mere relative descriptions. This lesson unpacks that argument and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the shubha carefully
Read the Arabic passage of the shubha and its translation. Notice the two-pronged argument: either need/effectivity exist as real attributes (leading to regress) or they don't (so possible beings have no real need).
Focus on the logical structure: if real → regress; if not real → no need.
2
Identify the answer's key move
The answer denies the initial assumption that need and effectivity are real existents. Instead, they are merely relational. This blocks the infinite regress because only real attributes require further attributes.
The commentary clarifies that need itself would need an effecter if it were real, but since it's not, the argument dissolves.
3
Distinguish between 'no need' and 'need is non-existent'
The answer explains that the opponent conflates 'he has no need' with 'his need is non-existent.' The former negates the attribute, the latter is a positive claim about non-existence. The correct reading is that possible beings are not described as needing a cause in the sense of a real need.
This distinction is crucial for avoiding the opponent's conclusion.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this shubha is important: it challenges the causal dependency of the possible on the Necessary. By showing that need and effectivity are not real, the argument preserves the possibility of causal dependence without infinite regress.
This ties back to the overall proof for a necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : الحاجة والمؤثرية لو وجدتا في الخارج ( تسلسل ) ) أي لزم التسلسل، وذلك لأن الحاجة لو وجدت لاحتاجت إلى الموصوف بها، إذ لا يتصور قيامها بذاتها، فللحاجة حاجة أخرى فينقل الكلام إلى حاجة الحاجة، وكذا المؤثرية لو وجدت لاحتاجت إلى مؤثرية أخرى ؛ إذ يستحيل كونها واجبة بذاتها، وإذا لم تكونا موجودتين لم يكن الممكن متصفاً بالحاجة إلى سبب، لا لإمكانه ولا لغيره، ولم يكن شيء متصفاً بالمؤثرية في الممكن أصلاً ، وهو المطلوب . ( والجواب :
قوله : ( لاحتاجت إلخ) ولك أن تقول : لاحتاجت إلى مؤثر إذ يستحيل كونها واجبة لذاتها، إلا أن الطريق المذكور لما كان أظهر اكتفى به . قوله : ( إذ يستحيل إلخ لامتناع تعدد الواجب ولقيامها بالغير. قوله : ( وإذا لم تكونا إلخ) بناء على عدم الفرق بين قولنا : لا حاجة له، وحاجته لا، وكذا بين قولنا : لا مؤثرية له ومؤثريته لا، كما مر .
The third shubha: Need (al-ḥāja) and effectivity (al-mu’aththiriyya), if they existed externally, (lead to infinite regress), i.e., infinite regress is necessary, because if need existed it would need that which is described by it, since it is inconceivable that it subsists by itself; so need has another need, and the discourse is transferred to the need of need; and likewise effectivity, if it existed, would need another effectivity, since it is impossible that it be necessary by itself; and if they do not exist, then the possible is not described as needing a cause, neither due to its possibility nor to anything else, and nothing is described as having effectivity in the possible at all—and that is what is desired. (And the answer: his statement: (it would need etc.) And you may say: it would need an effecter since it is impossible that it be necessary by itself, except that the mentioned path, since it is more apparent, sufficed. His statement: (since it is impossible etc.) because of the impossibility of multiplicity of the Necessary [Being] and because they subsist by another. His statement: (and if they do not exist etc.) based on the lack of distinction between our saying: he has no need, and: his need is non-existent, and likewise between our saying: he has no effectivity, and: his effectivity is non-existent, as has passed.)
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents the third shubha concerning the possibility of need (ḥāja) and effectivity (mu’aththiriyya) as real attributes that would cause infinite regress. The shubha argues: If these were external realities, they would themselves need a substrate (or a cause), leading to an infinite chain; if they do not exist, then the possible cannot be described as needing a cause—which is what the opponent wants (i.e., to deny the need for a creator). The answer (jawāb) clarifies that need and effectivity are not positive existents; they are relative or mental considerations. The commentary explains that one could say need itself would need an effecter (since it cannot be self-necessary), but the clearer approach is to deny their real existence. The commentary also notes that inability to be described with need is not the same as having no need—the opponent conflates them. Thus, the infinite regress is avoided because these are not real attributes that require further attributes.

Key Terms
شبهة
حاجة
مؤثرية
تسلسل
ممكن
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that need and effectivity are real attributes that exist in the external world like other accidental qualities.
Correction: The answer shows that need and effectivity are not real existents; they are merely relational descriptions. If they were real, they would themselves require a cause, leading to infinite regress. By denying their reality, the regress is avoided.

Checkpoint
What is the key flaw in the third shubha according to the answer?
A) It assumes need and effectivity are real existents when they are not
B) It confuses possible and necessary beings
C) It ignores the fact that infinite regress is possible
D) It relies on a weak definition of causality

Key Takeaway
The third shubha fails because need and effectivity are not real attributes—they are mere relational concepts—thus no infinite regress arises, and the possible's dependence on a cause remains intact.

The shubha attempts to deny the need for a cause by dismissing need as a real attribute. Have you ever, even subtly, acted as if you are self-sufficient—perhaps by neglecting prayer, relying on your own plans, or feeling secure in your wealth? What specific step will you take this week to physically and mentally acknowledge your total dependence on Allah?
?
If need is not a 'thing' that exists independently, then your lack of awareness of your need (e.g., in moments of pride) does not mean you actually don't need Him. What habits or attitudes have you built on the false assumption that you are less needy than others? How will you uproot that assumption starting today?

Fourth Objection: Contingency and Need in Existence
Fourth objection and its refutation regarding contingency and causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against contingency necessitating a cause and how the answer refutes it.

Core Idea
Contingency does not necessitate a cause for non-existence, so the objection fails.

This lesson examines a specific objection to the kalam argument that contingency requires a cause. The objector claims that if contingency makes existence need a cause, the same should apply to non-existence. However, since non-existence cannot be an effect, the objection collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the objection: contingency would necessitate a cause for both existence and non-existence.
Notice the equal relation argument.
2
Identify the two cases in the answer
The answer distinguishes two scenarios: if non-existence can be an effect, the proof fails; if not, the implication is denied.
This is a key logical move.
3
Reflect on why the answer is effective
Consider how the answer preserves the need for a cause only for existence, blocking the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة ( الرابعة : وهي مخصوصة بنفي كون الإمكان محوجاً أن يقال : (لو أحوج ) الإمكان ( في الوجود) إلى المؤثر ( لأحوج في العدم) أيضاً إلى المؤثر، (لاستواء نسبتهما إِليه أي نسبة الوجود والعدم إلى الإمكان لأنه رفع الضرورة الذاتية عنهما معاً فكما أن الوجود ممكن كذلك العدم ممكن، (لكن العدم نفي محض لا يصلح أثراً لشيء سواء كان عدماً أصلياً أو طارئاً، وفي الأصلي مانع آخر، وهو أنه مستمر فالتأثير فيه تحصيل للحاصل فوجب أن لا يكون الوجود أيضاً أثراً لشيء، ( والجواب : أن العدم إن صلح أثراً بطل دليلكم لبطلان انتفاء اللازم حينئذ ، ( وإلا ) وإن لم يصلح (منعنا الملازمة، أي لا نسلم أنه لو أحوج في
The fourth doubt: and it is specific to negating that contingency necessitates need. It is said: If contingency necessitated a cause in existence, it would also necessitate a cause in non-existence, because of the equal relation of both to it, i.e., the relation of existence and non-existence to contingency, since it removes essential necessity from both. So just as existence is possible, so too non-existence is possible. But non-existence is pure negation, not fit to be an effect of anything, whether original or accidental non-existence. In original non-existence, there is another obstacle: it is continuous, so effecting it would be obtaining what is already obtained. Hence existence must also not be an effect of anything. The answer: That non-existence, if it is fit to be an effect, then your proof fails due to the falsity of the necessary not occurring then. And if not, we deny the implication, i.e., we do not accept that it necessitates a cause in non-existence.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection (shubha rabi'a) specifically targeting the claim that contingency (imkan) necessitates a cause. The objector argues: if contingency makes existence need a cause, then the same should apply to non-existence because contingency is equally related to both (it removes essential necessity from both). But non-existence is pure negation and cannot be an effect, so existence should not be an effect either. The answer distinguishes two cases: if non-existence can be an effect, then the objector's proof fails because the necessary consequence (that existence also has no cause) does not hold; if non-existence cannot be an effect, then we deny the implication (that contingency necessitates a cause in non-existence). Thus, the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
الإمكان
المحوج
الوجود
العدم
الأثر
الملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is valid because non-existence cannot be an effect, so existence also should not be an effect.
Correction: The answer denies the implication when non-existence cannot be an effect, so the objection does not force the conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the fourth objection?
A) Non-existence can be an effect, so the objection fails.
B) If non-existence cannot be an effect, the implication is denied.
C) Both existence and non-existence require a cause.
D) Contingency does not necessitate a cause.

Key Takeaway
The answer to the fourth objection shows that contingency does not necessitate a cause for non-existence, preserving the need for a cause only for existence.

?

?
The objector assumed that God's causality must follow human logic of equal treatment. Have you ever questioned God's wisdom because you thought He should have acted differently? How does the answer's denial of implication teach you to submit to His unique causality?

Objection and Response on Non-Existence as an Effect
Al-Jurjani defends the claim that non-existence can be an effect of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the claim that non-existence can be an effect of non-existence, and how he uses rational necessity to refute the objection that this would lead to allowing existence from non-existence.

Core Idea
Rational necessity distinguishes non-existence from non-existence and existence from non-existence.

Al-Jurjani presents a sophisticated argument in defense of the idea that non-existence can be an effect (maʿlūl). The objector claims that if non-existence can be attributed to non-existence, then existence could also be attributed to non-existence, which would undermine the proof for God's existence. Al-Jurjani counters by appealing to rational necessity (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya) as the decisive factor that permits one and prohibits the other. In this lesson, we will examine this exchange and see how the author upholds the logical integrity of his position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that this is an objection-response argument within a larger discussion on contingency and causality. The issue is whether non-existence can be an effect.
Recall that in kalam, effects depend on causes.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector argues that if non-existence can be an effect, then existence could also come from non-existence, which would negate the need for a creator and close the door to proving God's existence.
3
Examine al-Jurjani's response
He denies the implication, stating that rational necessity (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya) judges the permissibility of non-existence from non-existence and the impossibility of existence from non-existence.
Rational necessity is a self-evident truth.
4
Reflect on the role of rational necessity
Consider why the author appeals to innate rational judgment rather than further logical deduction. It serves as a foundational principle that stops the objection.
5
Apply the lesson
See how this argument fits into the broader proof for God's existence. The ability to distinguish between types of causal dependence is crucial.

Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response in a kalam argument about whether non-existence (ʿadam) can be an effect (athar). The author (likely al-Jurjani) begins by stating the clear difference: existence can be an effect, non-existence cannot. From this, he argues that possibility (imkān) necessitates need only in the aspect that can be an effect. The objector then argues: if we grant the implication (mulāzama) that non-existence cannot be an effect, the opponent might claim that non-existence of an effect is due to non-existence of the cause. But the objector retorts: if that were allowed, then existence could also be attributed to non-existence, which would eliminate the need for an efficient cause (muʾaththir) and thus destroy the proof for God's existence. The response is that this objection is mistaken because rational necessity (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya) distinguishes between the two cases: non-existence can be an effect of non-existence, but existence cannot be an effect of non-existence. The implication is denied. The commentary (sharh) then clarifies each part of the text, explaining that the author's statement 'we deny the implication' does not negate the truth of the consequent, and that the author's phrasing 'from the start without hesitation' is meant to avoid an apparent contradiction in the text.

عدم (ʿadam, non-existence)
إمكان (imkān, possibility)
علة (ʿilla, cause)
معلول (maʿlūl, effect)
ملازمة (mulāzama, implication/necessary connection)
ضرورة عقلية (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya, rational necessity)
مؤثر (muʾaththir, efficient cause)

Misconception: If non-existence can be an effect, then existence could also come from non-existence.
Correction: Rational necessity (ḍarūra ʿaqliyya) judges the permissibility of non-existence from non-existence but the impossibility of existence from non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Rational necessity is the deciding factor that distinguishes between the permissibility of non-existence from non-existence and the impossibility of existence from non-existence, preserving the proof for God's existence.

The Fifth Doubt: Does Possibility Require a Cause for Survival?
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the doubt that possibility necessitates need for a cause during survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that possibility would necessitate need for a cause during survival and why this leads to absurdities such as obtaining the already obtained.

Core Idea
Possibility alone cannot necessitate a cause during survival, or else it would require obtaining the already obtained.

In this lesson, al-Jurjani presents the fifth doubt concerning whether possibility (imkān) necessitates need for an efficient cause during the state of survival. The doubt argues that if possibility is the real ground for need, then since it remains with the contingent after origination, need should persist. But this leads to absurd consequences. We will explore the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the doubt and its refutation. Note the structure: doubt, then response showing two absurd outcomes.
The passage uses a conditional and two cases in the refutation.
2
Identify the two branches of the refutation
The refutation shows that the cause in survival either produces the same existence (obtaining the obtained) or produces a renewed thing (making it not a cause of the remaining entity). Compare these two.
These two outcomes are both impossible, so the original claim must be false.
3
Understand why possibility is said to remain
The doubt asserts that possibility is a necessary concomitant of the essence (lāzim al-māhiyya) and never leaves it, so it is present in survival as well.
This is a key premise: possibility is not an accidental attribute but intrinsic.
4
Reflect on the concept of 'obtaining the already obtained'
If the cause in survival brings existence itself, but existence already exists, that is a logical absurdity (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). Consider why this is impossible.
This is a standard principle in kalam and philosophy.

الإمكان محوجاً ، ( لو كان المحوج ) إلى المؤثر ( هو الإمكان لأحوج إليه أيضاً (حال البقاء، لثبوته حينئذ أي ثبوت الإمكان للممكن في حال البقاء : ( فإنه لازم للماهية ) الممكنة تقتضيه ذاتها من حيث هي هي. ، فلا ينفك عنها أصلاً كالوجوب والامتناع الذاتيين، وإذا كان الإمكان ثابتاً حال البقاء كان معلوله الذي هو الاحتياج إلى المؤثر ثابتاً أيضاً . ) والثاني باطل لأن الحاصل به أي بتأثير المؤثر حال البقاء ( إن كان نفس الوجود وأنه حاصل قبله ) أي قبل البقاء ( لزم تحصيل الحاصل وإن كان الحاصل به ( أمراً متجدداً لم يكن ) ذلك المؤثر بتأثيره ( موجباً للباقي ) الذي هو المتصف بذلك الوجود الحاصل قبل البقاء، (بل) موجباً ( لأمر آخر)، فلا يكون مؤثراً في الباقي،
قوله: (وهي أيضاً مخصوصة إلخ) هذا مبني على ما سيجيء من أن القائلين بعلية الحدوث يقولون بأن الماهية إذا حدثت أي خرجت إلى الوجود لم يبق لها الحاجة، وأما بالنظر إلى التحقيق من أن الاتصاف بالحدوث حال البقاء أيضاً لأنه عبارة عن المسبوقية فالشبهة تنفي علية الحدوث للحاجة أيضاً، كما لا يخفى .

کلامه
The third observation - the fourth purpose: On investigations of the contingent per se.
Possibility necessitating need: [The argument is that] if the necessitator of need for the efficient cause were possibility, it would necessitate need also in the state of survival, because it is established then, i.e., the establishment of possibility for the contingent in the state of survival. For it is necessary for the possible essence; its essence requires it qua itself, so it is never separate from it, like intrinsic necessity and impossibility. And if possibility is established in the state of survival, then its effect, which is need for the efficient cause, is also established. And the second [consequent] is false because what is obtained by it, i.e., by the effect of the efficient cause in the state of survival, if it is existence itself and it is obtained before it (i.e., before survival), then it would be obtaining the obtained; if what is obtained by it is a renewed thing, then that efficient cause by its effect would not be the cause of the remaining thing (which is qualified by that existence obtained before survival), but rather a cause of something else, so it would not be an effect in the remaining thing.
[Commentary begins]
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth doubt (al-shubha al-khāmisah) concerning whether possibility (imkān) necessitates need for an efficient cause (muʾaththir) during the state of survival (ḥāl al-baqāʾ) after creation. The argument (attributed to the opponent) is: if possibility is what necessitates need, then since possibility remains with the contingent in survival (because it is a necessary concomitant of the essence, like necessity and impossibility), the need for a cause should persist as well. The response is that this leads to absurdity: either the cause in survival produces the same existence already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) or produces a renewed thing, in which case it is not a cause of the remaining existent. The commentary clarifies nuances: the claim that possibility is a necessary concomitant of the essence is based on the fact that possibility is one of the second intelligibles (al-maʿqūlāt al-thāniya) that are abstracted by the mind; the relation of needing a cause in survival may be affected by other conditions. The passage sets up the discussion for the refutation of the doubt.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkān)
بقاء (baqāʾ)
مؤثر (muʾaththir)
معلول (maʿlūl)
لازم الماهية (lāzim al-māhiyya)
تحصيل الحاصل (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility must always necessitate need for a cause, even during survival.
Correction: The need for a cause is only for origination; during survival the cause's effect would be either redundant or unrelated, as the passage shows.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth doubt, why does possibility necessitate need for a cause even during survival?
A) Because survival is a new attribute requiring a cause
B) Because possibility is a necessary concomitant of the essence and remains with it
C) Because the cause must continuously create existence
D) Because the contingent is always in need of preservation

Key Takeaway
Possibility does not necessitate need for a cause during survival, because that would lead to absurdities such as obtaining the already obtained or making the cause irrelevant to the surviving entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your continued existence at this very moment does not automatically require a cause because possibility alone doesn't necessitate it, then why do you still feel the need for God's constant support? Does your heart truly depend on Him for each breath, or do you take survival for granted?
?
The argument shows that a cause cannot produce the same existence twice—it would be obtaining the obtained. How does this challenge your understanding of God's ongoing creation? Do you recognize that every moment you exist is a fresh act of divine will, not a mere continuation?
?
If need for a cause is only for origination, not survival, then what does that say about your gratitude for being brought into existence? Are you living as if the initial act of creation is enough, or does each moment call for a renewed sense of thankfulness and reliance?

The Persistence of Possibility and the Need for a Cause
The objection that possibility necessitates need even in persistence and the initial dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if possibility necessitates need, then need persists, and the initial dilemma that the cause's effect in persistence cannot be the same existence without absurdity.

Core Idea
Possibility remains in persistence, so need for a cause remains.

This passage presents a key objection against the view that possibility alone necessitates need. The objection argues that since possibility persists, the need must persist, leading to a dilemma: either the cause's effect in persistence is the existence itself (which is already acquired) or a new thing (which would not affect the original entity). Let's examine this foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the structure of the objection: possibility is a necessary concomitant of the essence, so it persists in the state of persistence, therefore need persists.
Notice the parallel with essential necessity and impossibility.
2
Identify the two consequences
The passage presents two possibilities for the cause's effect in persistence: either it is the existence itself (leading to acquisition of the acquired) or a renewed thing (leading to the cause not affecting the remaining entity). Identify each and understand why each is problematic.
3
Understand the dilemma
Ask yourself: why does the commentator consider both options impossible? The first leads to a logical absurdity (obtaining what is already obtained), the second severs the cause's connection to the remaining entity.
This dilemma sets up the need for a more refined answer later.

Source Text

المرصد الثالث - المقصد الرابع: في أبحاث الممكن لذاته
الإمكان محوجاً ، ( لو كان المحوج ) إلى المؤثر ( هو الإمكان لأحوج إليه أيضاً (حال البقاء، لثبوته حينئذ أي ثبوت الإمكان للممكن في حال البقاء : ( فإنه لازم للماهية ) الممكنة تقتضيه ذاتها من حيث هي هي. ، فلا ينفك عنها أصلاً كالوجوب والامتناع الذاتيين، وإذا كان الإمكان ثابتاً حال البقاء كان معلوله الذي هو الاحتياج إلى المؤثر ثابتاً أيضاً . ) والثاني باطل لأن الحاصل به أي بتأثير المؤثر حال البقاء ( إن كان نفس الوجود وأنه حاصل قبله ) أي قبل البقاء ( لزم تحصيل الحاصل وإن كان الحاصل به ( أمراً متجدداً لم يكن ) ذلك المؤثر بتأثيره ( موجباً للباقي ) الذي هو المتصف بذلك الوجود الحاصل قبل البقاء، (بل) موجباً ( لأمر آخر)، فلا يكون مؤثراً في الباقي،
قوله: (وهي أيضاً مخصوصة إلخ) هذا مبني على ما سيجيء من أن القائلين بعلية الحدوث يقولون بأن الماهية إذا حدثت أي خرجت إلى الوجود لم يبق لها الحاجة، وأما بالنظر إلى التحقيق من أن الاتصاف بالحدوث حال البقاء أيضاً لأنه عبارة عن المسبوقية فالشبهة تنفي علية الحدوث للحاجة أيضاً، كما لا يخفى .
قوله: (فلا ينفك عنها أصلاً وإلا لزم الانقلاب فإن قلت قد صرح في التجريد بأن الجهات الثلاثة من المعقولات الثانية قلت الاتصاف بها بالفعل إنما هو في الذهن، فإن العقل بعد ملاحظة الماهية بالقياس إلى الوجود والعدم يصفها بإحداهما، فبهذا الاعتبار من المعقولات الثانية. وأما الاتصاف الانتزاعي بها أعني كون الماهية بحيث إذا لاحظها العقل مقيساً لها إلى الوجود والعدم انتزع عنها إحداهما فهو لازم للماهية من حيث هي هي، وعلية الإمكان للحاجة إنما هو بهذا الاعتبار كما لا يخفى
قوله : (لأحوج حال البقاء يمكن أن يقال على قياس ما ذكره في الوجود والعدم، الاحتياج حال البقاء والتأثير فيه إما أن يكون ممكناً أو لا، فإن كان ممكناً فبطلان التالي ممنوع وإلا فالملازمة ممنوعة، وإنما يلزم لو لم يكن هناك مانع .
قوله : (تقتضي من ذاتها حيث هي هي فيه بحيث إذ قد سبق أن الإمكان من المعقولات الثانية، الذي يقتضيها ذات المعقول الأول بحسب الوجود ،والذهن وقوله : من حيث هي هي يدل على أنه من لوازم الماهية بالمعنى المتعارف ثم إنه مما لا يحتاج إلى إلزامه أجزاء الشبهة إذ يكفي أن يقال : المجموع على القول بأن الإمكان هو كون الشيء بحيث لو وجه في الذهن كان متصفا بمساواة الوجود والعدم بالنظر إلى ذاته وهذه الحيثية ثابتة له حال البقاء. اللهم إلا أن يقال : هذا سبق كلام صاحب الشبهة على المشهور، وإن لم يكن مختاراً، كما يفهم من في حواشي حكمة العين أيضاً . قوله : ( كان معلوله الذي هو الاحتياج إلى المؤثر ثابتاً أيضاً قد يقال: إن لم لا يجوز أن يكون عدم البقاء شرطاً لوجود المعلول الذي هو الاحتياج بأن لا يكون الإمكان علة تامة له . وفيه

( والجواب : أنه ) أي التأثير في الممكن الباقي ليس تحصيلاً للحاصل، ولا تحصيلاً للمتجدد (بل) تأثيره فيه هو أن يكون دوامه لدوامه كما كان وجوده أولى من وجوده ) فإن سمي الدوام (متجدداً ) لأنه لم یکن حاصلاً في أول زمان الوجود ) صار ) النزاع (لفظياً ) ؛ لأنا نقول : التأثير في دوام الوجود الحاصل أولاً لا في أمر متجدد هو وجود ابتدائي، وأنتم تقولون : لا تأثير في الوجود الحاصل أولاً ، بل في أمر متجدد هو دوامه، فالمعنى واحد والاختلاف في أن
قوله : ( والمقدر خلافه لأن المراد من قولنا : الأحوج حال البقاء أن يكون الباقي في بقائه محتاجاً إلى المؤثر ويكون له التأثير في بقائه، فلا يرد أن التأثير حال البقاء لا يقتضي أن يكون التأثير في البقاء، والمقدر هو الأول دون الثاني.
قوله : ( تأثيره في بقائه إلخ) فيكون التأثير في الباقي، فلا يلزم خلاف المقدر.
قوله : (لا) في ذاته إلخ فلا يلزم تحصيل الحاصل، فلا يلزم شيء من المحذورين.
قوله : (الذات ممكنة إلخ يعني أن الذات متصفة بالإمكان بحسب أصل الوجود حال البقاء، إذ الممكن لا يصير واجباً، ولا تأثير فيها بحسب أصل الوجود فبقي الذات بلا مؤثر بحسب أصل الوجود، مع ثبوت إمكانها بهذا الاعتبار. هكذا ينبغي أن يقرر الكلام ليتضح المرام ويندفع الشكوك والأوهام.
قوله : (ليس تحصيلاً للحاصل بأن يكون بحسب أصل الوجود. قوله : ( ولا تحصيلاً للمتجدد بأن يكون التأثير باعتبار أمر متجدد لم يكن حاصلاً ابتداء،
حتى لا يكون التأثير في الباقي . قوله : ( أن يكون دوامه (لدوامه فالتأثير في الاتصاف بالوجود في الزمان الثاني، كما كان في الزمان الأول، فلا يلزم شيء من المحذورين.
قوله : ( والاختلاف إلخ) حيث أردنا به الوجود الابتدائي لو أردتم به الدوام.
نظر، إذ يلزم أن لا يحتاج القديم إلى المؤثر أصلاً، إذ كل زمان يفرض هو فيه زمان بقاء له، والقائلون بأن الإمكان علة الاحتياج لا يلتزمونه قطعاً على أن مال هذا إلى اعتبار الحدوث مع الإمكان وحده فتأمل.
قوله : ( بحسب أصل الوجود لو سكت عن هذ القيد ترويجاً للجواب، لكان أحسن وأوفق بقوله : ولا تأثير فيها، كما اعترفتم به فيبقى الذات بلا مؤثر إلا أنه قيده به إشعاراً لضعف
الجواب ابتداء .
Third Observing Station - Fourth Objective: On Investigations of the Contingent in Itself
Possibility is a necessitator: (If the necessitator) to the effect (is possibility, then it would necessitate it also) (in the state of persistence, due to its subsistence then, i.e., the subsistence of possibility for the contingent in the state of persistence: (for it is necessary for the essence) of the contingent, its essence necessitates it from the aspect of itself, and it is not separate from it at all, like essential necessity and impossibility. And if possibility is established in the state of persistence, then its effect, which is the need for the effect, is also established.) And the second [consequence] is false because what is obtained by it, i.e., by the effect of the cause in the state of persistence, (if it is the existence itself and it is obtained before it) i.e., before persistence, (then the acquisition of the acquired would be necessary, and if what is obtained by it is a renewed thing, then) that cause by its effect (would not be a necessitator of the remaining) which is the thing qualified by that existence obtained before persistence, (but) a necessitator (of something else), so it would not be an effect in the remaining.
His statement: (And it is also specific, etc.) This is based on what will come, that those who affirm causality of origination say that the essence, when it originates, i.e., comes into existence, no longer has need. However, according to investigation, the characterization by origination is also in the state of persistence because it is an expression of precedence, so the doubt refutes the causality of origination for need as well, as is not hidden.
His statement: (So it is not separate from it at all, otherwise reversal would be necessary. For if you say: It has been stated in al-Tajrid that the three modes are among the secondary intelligibles. I say: Actual characterization by them is only in the mind, for the intellect, after considering the essence in relation to existence and non-existence, characterizes it by one of them. In this respect, it is among the secondary intelligibles. As for the attributive extraction of them, i.e., the essence being such that if the intellect considers it measured against existence and non-existence, it extracts one of them from it, it is necessary for the essence from the aspect of itself. And the causality of possibility for need is only in this respect, as is not hidden.)
His statement: (It would necessitate in the state of persistence) It can be said analogously to what he mentioned concerning existence and non-existence: The need in the state of persistence and the effect in it is either possible or not. If it is possible, then the falsity of the consequent is prevented; otherwise, the implication is prevented. It would only be necessary if there were no obstacle.
His statement: (It necessitates from its essence from the aspect of itself) In it, because it has already preceded that possibility is among the secondary intelligibles, which the essence of the first intelligible necessitates according to existence and the mind. And his statement 'from the aspect of itself' indicates that it is among the necessary concomitants of the essence in the conventional sense. Then it does not require imposing the parts of the doubt, for it suffices to say: The sum, on the view that possibility is the thing being such that if it is directed in the mind, it is characterized by equality of existence and non-existence with respect to its essence, and this aspect is established for it in the state of persistence. Unless it is said: This is a precedence of the objectioner's statement according to the well-known [view], even if it is not chosen, as is understood from the glosses of Hikmat al-'Ayn as well.
His statement: (So its effect, which is the need for the cause, is also established) It can be said: Why is it not permissible that the absence of persistence is a condition for the existence of the effect, which is the need, such that possibility is not a complete cause for it? And therein is his statement.
And the intended is contrary to it. (It is not said: His effect in its persistence) which is a renewed thing, (not in its essence) with respect to the original existence that was obtained (because) we say: (The essence is possible in the state of persistence) and there is no effect in it as you admitted, (so the essence remains) without a cause in it, thus it would be independent of it despite the subsistence of its possibility that necessitates it to him, by assumption. This is a contradiction. (And the answer: That is) i.e., the effect in the contingent remaining is not an acquisition of the acquired, nor an acquisition of the renewed, (but) its effect in it is that its permanence is for its permanence just as its existence was from its existence.) If permanence is called (renewed) because it was not obtained in the first moment of existence, then the dispute becomes (verbal); for we say: The effect is in the permanence of the existence obtained first, not in a renewed thing that is an initial existence, and you say: There is no effect in the existence obtained first, but rather in a renewed thing which is its permanence. So the meaning is one, and the difference is in that
His statement: (And the intended is contrary to it) Because what is meant by our statement 'the necessitator in the state of persistence' is that the remaining in its persistence needs the cause, and it has the effect in its persistence, so it is not objected that the effect in the state of persistence does not require that the effect be in persistence, and the intended is the first, not the second.
His statement: (His effect in its persistence, etc.) So the effect is in the remaining, so no violation of the intended occurs.
His statement: (Not) in its essence, etc. So no acquisition of the acquired is necessary, so none of the two problematic consequences occur.
His statement: (The essence is possible, etc.) Means that the essence is characterized by possibility with respect to the original existence in the state of persistence, since the contingent does not become necessary, and there is no effect in it with respect to the original existence, so the essence remains without a cause with respect to the original existence, while its possibility is established in this respect. Thus should the speech be formulated to clarify the goal and repel doubts and illusions.
His statement: (It is not an acquisition of the acquired) by being with respect to the original existence. His statement: (Nor an acquisition of the renewed) by the effect being in terms of a renewed thing that was not obtained initially, so that the effect is not in the remaining. His statement: (That its permanence is) (for its permanence) so the effect is in the characterization by existence in the second moment, as it was in the first moment, so none of the two problematic consequences occur.
His statement: (And the difference, etc.) Where we meant by it the initial existence, if you meant by it permanence.
Consideration: For it would require that the eternal not need the cause at all, since every moment that is supposed is a moment of its persistence, and those who say that possibility is the cause of need definitely do not commit to that. Moreover, this tends to considering origination along with possibility alone, so reflect.
His statement: (With respect to the original existence) If he had omitted this qualifier to promote the answer, it would have been better and more consonant with his statement: 'And there is no effect in it, as you admitted, so the essence remains without a cause.' But he restricted it to indicate the weakness of the answer initially.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the argument about whether possibility (imkan) is the cause of the contingent's need for a cause, especially in the state of persistence (baqa'). The commentator presents an objection: if possibility is what necessitates the need, then even in persistence the contingent would still need a cause because possibility remains (since it is a necessary concomitant of the essence, like essential necessity and impossibility). Then he examines the response: in the state of persistence, the effect of the cause is either the existence itself (which would be 'acquisition of the acquired') or a renewed thing (which would mean the cause does not affect the remaining entity). The commentator proceeds to discuss several points: (1) The notion that origination (huduth) as a cause of need is also refuted because origination is a relation of precedence that remains in persistence. (2) The three modes (wujub, imkan, imtina') are secondary intelligibles; the actual attribution is mental, but the attributive extraction (intiza'i) is a necessary concomitant of the essence. The causality of possibility for need is in this latter sense. (3) A further objection about whether the absence of persistence is a condition for the need, to which the answer is given. (4) The crux of the defense: the cause's effect in persistence is the permanence (dawam) of the existence, not a new existence. If one calls permanence 'renewed,' the dispute becomes verbal. The commentator explains each gloss (qawluhu) on the matn, clarifying that the effect is on the remaining entity in its persistence, not on the essence regarding original existence. He also notes a final doubt: if the cause's effect is only on permanence, then an eternal being would not need a cause at any moment (since it always persists), but those who hold possibility as the cause do not accept that. The passage concludes by suggesting that the qualifier 'with respect to original existence' might be omitted for a better answer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That need for a cause ceases after origination because possibility is only relevant at the moment of coming into existence.
Correction: The commentator shows that possibility remains as an essential concomitant, so need persists unless a response is given.

Checkpoint
What is the first possible consequence of the cause's effect in persistence according to the objection?
A) Acquisition of the acquired (tahsil al-hasil)
B) Creation of a new essence
C) Removal of possibility
D) Independence of the contingent

Key Takeaway
The objection that possibility necessitates need in persistence forces a dilemma: either absurd repetition or loss of causal relevance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time you felt self-sufficient after achieving a milestone. This passage challenges that feeling by showing that even in persistence, you are not independent. In what specific area of your life do you most need to acknowledge that your continued existence and well-being depend on a higher power? What concrete action will you take today to remind yourself of this dependence?
?
The dilemma shows that if a cause gave the same existence twice, it would be absurd. Have you ever treated God's previous blessings as a one-time deposit that requires no further renewal? How does this passage call you to reorient your understanding of your ongoing need for divine sustenance? Write a short prayer expressing your reliance on God for each moment.

Causality and Created Things
advanced
6 min
Possibility as a Secondary Intelligible and Its Causality
Discussion of origination, secondary intelligibles, and the precise sense in which possibility necessitates need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between actual mental attribution and attributive extraction, and how the causality of possibility for need is based on the latter.

Core Idea
Possibility's causality stems from its attributive extraction, not its mental attribution.

The commentator now addresses a deeper philosophical point: the nature of the three modes (wujub, imkan, imtina'). They are secondary intelligibles, but their causality for need depends on a specific sense—attributive extraction (intiza'). This clarification is crucial to understanding how possibility can be a persistent necessitator without leading to the earlier dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on huduth
Notice how the commentator refutes the alternative view that origination (huduth) is the cause of need, showing that origination also persists in a sense (as precedence).
This supports the main argument that possibility is the real necessitator.
2
Understand the secondary intelligibles
The passage explains that the actual characterization by the three modes occurs only in the mind. But there is another sense—attributive extraction—which is a necessary concomitant of the essence itself.
The causality of possibility for need is only in this latter sense.
3
Grasp the alternative formulation
The commentator offers an alternative way to state the objection without imposing the parts of the doubt: possibility can be defined as equality of existence and non-existence with respect to the essence, and this aspect persists.
This shows the objection can be framed in a simpler manner.

Third Observing Station - Fourth Objective: On Investigations of the Contingent in Itself
Possibility is a necessitator: (If the necessitator) to the effect (is possibility, then it would necessitate it also) (in the state of persistence, due to its subsistence then, i.e., the subsistence of possibility for the contingent in the state of persistence: (for it is necessary for the essence) of the contingent, its essence necessitates it from the aspect of itself, and it is not separate from it at all, like essential necessity and impossibility. And if possibility is established in the state of persistence, then its effect, which is the need for the effect, is also established.) And the second [consequence] is false because what is obtained by it, i.e., by the effect of the cause in the state of persistence, (if it is the existence itself and it is obtained before it) i.e., before persistence, (then the acquisition of the acquired would be necessary, and if what is obtained by it is a renewed thing, then) that cause by its effect (would not be a necessitator of the remaining) which is the thing qualified by that existence obtained before persistence, (but) a necessitator (of something else), so it would not be an effect in the remaining.
His statement: (And it is also specific, etc.) This is based on what will come, that those who affirm causality of origination say that the essence, when it originates, i.e., comes into existence, no longer has need. However, according to investigation, the characterization by origination is also in the state of persistence because it is an expression of precedence, so the doubt refutes the causality of origination for need as well, as is not hidden.
His statement: (So it is not separate from it at all, otherwise reversal would be necessary. For if you say: It has been stated in al-Tajrid that the three modes are among the secondary intelligibles. I say: Actual characterization by them is only in the mind, for the intellect, after considering the essence in relation to existence and non-existence, characterizes it by one of them. In this respect, it is among the secondary intelligibles. As for the attributive extraction of them, i.e., the essence being such that if the intellect considers it measured against existence and non-existence, it extracts one of them from it, it is necessary for the essence from the aspect of itself. And the causality of possibility for need is only in this respect, as is not hidden.)
His statement: (It would necessitate in the state of persistence) It can be said analogously to what he mentioned concerning existence and non-existence: The need in the state of persistence and the effect in it is either possible or not. If it is possible, then the falsity of the consequent is prevented; otherwise, the implication is prevented. It would only be necessary if there were no obstacle.
His statement: (It necessitates from its essence from the aspect of itself) In it, because it has already preceded that possibility is among the secondary intelligibles, which the essence of the first intelligible necessitates according to existence and the mind. And his statement 'from the aspect of itself' indicates that it is among the necessary concomitants of the essence in the conventional sense. Then it does not require imposing the parts of the doubt, for it suffices to say: The sum, on the view that possibility is the thing being such that if it is directed in the mind, it is characterized by equality of existence and non-existence with respect to its essence, and this aspect is established for it in the state of persistence. Unless it is said: This is a precedence of the objectioner's statement according to the well-known [view], even if it is not chosen, as is understood from the glosses of Hikmat al-'Ayn as well.
His statement: (So its effect, which is the need for the cause, is also established) It can be said: Why is it not permissible that the absence of persistence is a condition for the existence of the effect, which is the need, such that possibility is not a complete cause for it? And therein is his statement.
And the intended is contrary to it. (It is not said: His effect in its persistence) which is a renewed thing, (not in its essence) with respect to the original existence that was obtained (because) we say: (The essence is possible in the state of persistence) and there is no effect in it as you admitted, (so the essence remains) without a cause in it, thus it would be independent of it despite the subsistence of its possibility that necessitates it to him, by assumption. This is a contradiction. (And the answer: That is) i.e., the effect in the contingent remaining is not an acquisition of the acquired, nor an acquisition of the renewed, (but) its effect in it is that its permanence is for its permanence just as its existence was from its existence.) If permanence is called (renewed) because it was not obtained in the first moment of existence, then the dispute becomes (verbal); for we say: The effect is in the permanence of the existence obtained first, not in a renewed thing that is an initial existence, and you say: There is no effect in the existence obtained first, but rather in a renewed thing which is its permanence. So the meaning is one, and the difference is in that
His statement: (And the intended is contrary to it) Because what is meant by our statement 'the necessitator in the state of persistence' is that the remaining in its persistence needs the cause, and it has the effect in its persistence, so it is not objected that the effect in the state of persistence does not require that the effect be in persistence, and the intended is the first, not the second.
His statement: (His effect in its persistence, etc.) So the effect is in the remaining, so no violation of the intended occurs.
His statement: (Not) in its essence, etc. So no acquisition of the acquired is necessary, so none of the two problematic consequences occur.
His statement: (The essence is possible, etc.) Means that the essence is characterized by possibility with respect to the original existence in the state of persistence, since the contingent does not become necessary, and there is no effect in it with respect to the original existence, so the essence remains without a cause with respect to the original existence, while its possibility is established in this respect. Thus should the speech be formulated to clarify the goal and repel doubts and illusions.
His statement: (It is not an acquisition of the acquired) by being with respect to the original existence. His statement: (Nor an acquisition of the renewed) by the effect being in terms of a renewed thing that was not obtained initially, so that the effect is not in the remaining. His statement: (That its permanence is) (for its permanence) so the effect is in the characterization by existence in the second moment, as it was in the first moment, so none of the two problematic consequences occur.
His statement: (And the difference, etc.) Where we meant by it the initial existence, if you meant by it permanence.
Consideration: For it would require that the eternal not need the cause at all, since every moment that is supposed is a moment of its persistence, and those who say that possibility is the cause of need definitely do not commit to that. Moreover, this tends to considering origination along with possibility alone, so reflect.
His statement: (With respect to the original existence) If he had omitted this qualifier to promote the answer, it would have been better and more consonant with his statement: 'And there is no effect in it, as you admitted, so the essence remains without a cause.' But he restricted it to indicate the weakness of the answer initially.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the argument about whether possibility (imkan) is the cause of the contingent's need for a cause, especially in the state of persistence (baqa'). The commentator presents an objection: if possibility is what necessitates the need, then even in persistence the contingent would still need a cause because possibility remains (since it is a necessary concomitant of the essence, like essential necessity and impossibility). Then he examines the response: in the state of persistence, the effect of the cause is either the existence itself (which would be 'acquisition of the acquired') or a renewed thing (which would mean the cause does not affect the remaining entity). The commentator proceeds to discuss several points: (1) The notion that origination (huduth) as a cause of need is also refuted because origination is a relation of precedence that remains in persistence. (2) The three modes (wujub, imkan, imtina') are secondary intelligibles; the actual attribution is mental, but the attributive extraction (intiza'i) is a necessary concomitant of the essence. The causality of possibility for need is in this latter sense. (3) A further objection about whether the absence of persistence is a condition for the need, to which the answer is given. (4) The crux of the defense: the cause's effect in persistence is the permanence (dawam) of the existence, not a new existence. If one calls permanence 'renewed,' the dispute becomes verbal. The commentator explains each gloss (qawluhu) on the matn, clarifying that the effect is on the remaining entity in its persistence, not on the essence regarding original existence. He also notes a final doubt: if the cause's effect is only on permanence, then an eternal being would not need a cause at any moment (since it always persists), but those who hold possibility as the cause do not accept that. The passage concludes by suggesting that the qualifier 'with respect to original existence' might be omitted for a better answer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the three modes (wujub, imkan, imtina') are only mental constructs with no basis in the essence itself.
Correction: The commentator shows that while their actual attribution is mental, the attributive extraction is a real necessary concomitant of the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, in what sense is possibility a cause of need?
A) In the sense of actual mental attribution
B) In the sense of attributive extraction (intiza')
C) In the sense of being a secondary intelligible
D) In the sense of being a primary intelligible

Key Takeaway
Possibility necessitates need through its attributive extraction, which is a persistent concomitant of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very essence is nothing but pure possibility—neither necessary nor impossible—then what does that say about your constant need for the Divine? How aware are you of your own contingency in every single moment of your existence?
?
The lesson distinguishes between mental attribution (what you think about yourself) and attributive extraction (how you truly are before Allah). In your daily life, do you live as if your existence is self-sufficient, or do you truly acknowledge that your essence is always in need of the Creator? What would change if you lived with the latter awareness?
?
Consider that even after you came into existence, your need persists because possibility never leaves your essence. How does this ongoing dependence on Allah affect the way you pray, trust, and repent? Are you living as if you needed Him once, or as if you need Him every moment?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
6 min
The Verbal Dispute over Permanence as the Effect
The answer that the effect is permanence, making the dispute verbal, and a final doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final answer to the objection: that the cause's effect in persistence is the permanence (dawam) of the existence, not a new existence, and that the dispute ultimately becomes verbal.

Core Idea
The cause's effect is the permanence of existence, not a new existence.

Having established that possibility persists, the commentator now provides the definitive answer to the dilemma. He argues that the cause's effect in persistence is not a new existence but rather the permanence of the already obtained existence. If one calls permanence 'renewed', the dispute becomes merely verbal. This resolves the earlier absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
The passage begins with an objection: why cannot the absence of persistence be a condition for need? Then the commentator gives the main answer: the effect is permanence, not a new existence.
Notice the phrase 'its permanence is for its permanence'.
2
Understand the verbal dispute
If permanence is called 'renewed' because it was not present at the first moment, then the disagreement is merely about terminology.
The commentator notes that both sides agree on the underlying reality.
3
Examine the final doubt
A doubt is raised: if the cause only affects permanence, then an eternal being would never need a cause. The commentator hints at a response involving origination.
This shows the complexity of the issue.

I say: Actual characterization by them is only in the mind, for the intellect, after considering the essence in relation to existence and non-existence, characterizes it by one of them. In this respect, it is among the secondary intelligibles. As for the attributive extraction of them, i.e., the essence being such that if the intellect considers it measured against existence and non-existence, it extracts one of them from it, it is necessary for the essence from the aspect of itself. And the causality of possibility for need is only in this respect, as is not hidden.)
His statement: (It would necessitate in the state of persistence) It can be said analogously to what he mentioned concerning existence and non-existence: The need in the state of persistence and the effect in it is either possible or not. If it is possible, then the falsity of the consequent is prevented; otherwise, the implication is prevented. It would only be necessary if there were no obstacle.
His statement: (It necessitates from its essence from the aspect of itself) In it, because it has already preceded that possibility is among the secondary intelligibles, which the essence of the first intelligible necessitates according to existence and the mind. And his statement 'from the aspect of itself' indicates that it is among the necessary concomitants of the essence in the conventional sense. Then it does not require imposing the parts of the doubt, for it suffices to say: The sum, on the view that possibility is the thing being such that if it is directed in the mind, it is characterized by equality of existence and non-existence with respect to its essence, and this aspect is established for it in the state of persistence. Unless it is said: This is a precedence of the objectioner's statement according to the well-known [view], even if it is not chosen, as is understood from the glosses of Hikmat al-'Ayn as well.
His statement: (So its effect, which is the need for the cause, is also established) It can be said: Why is it not permissible that the absence of persistence is a condition for the existence of the effect, which is the need, such that possibility is not a complete cause for it? And therein is his statement.
And the intended is contrary to it. (It is not said: His effect in its persistence) which is a renewed thing, (not in its essence) with respect to the original existence that was obtained (because) we say: (The essence is possible in the state of persistence) and there is no effect in it as you admitted, (so the essence remains) without a cause in it, thus it would be independent of it despite the subsistence of its possibility that necessitates it to him, by assumption. This is a contradiction. (And the answer: That is) i.e., the effect in the contingent remaining is not an acquisition of the acquired, nor an acquisition of the renewed, (but) its effect in it is that its permanence is for its permanence just as its existence was from its existence.) If permanence is called (renewed) because it was not obtained in the first moment of existence, then the dispute becomes (verbal); for we say: The effect is in the permanence of the existence obtained first, not in a renewed thing that is an initial existence, and you say: There is no effect in the existence obtained first, but rather in a renewed thing which is its permanence. So the meaning is one, and the difference is in that
His statement: (And the intended is contrary to it) Because what is meant by our statement 'the necessitator in the state of persistence' is that the remaining in its persistence needs the cause, and it has the effect in its persistence, so it is not objected that the effect in the state of persistence does not require that the effect be in persistence, and the intended is the first, not the second.
His statement: (His effect in its persistence, etc.) So the effect is in the remaining, so no violation of the intended occurs.
His statement: (Not) in its essence, etc. So no acquisition of the acquired is necessary, so none of the two problematic consequences occur.
His statement: (The essence is possible, etc.) Means that the essence is characterized by possibility with respect to the original existence in the state of persistence, since the contingent does not become necessary, and there is no effect in it with respect to the original existence, so the essence remains without a cause with respect to the original existence, while its possibility is established in this respect. Thus should the speech be formulated to clarify the goal and repel doubts and illusions.
His statement: (It is not an acquisition of the acquired) by being with respect to the original existence. His statement: (Nor an acquisition of the renewed) by the effect being in terms of a renewed thing that was not obtained initially, so that the effect is not in the remaining. His statement: (That its permanence is) (for its permanence) so the effect is in the characterization by existence in the second moment, as it was in the first moment, so none of the two problematic consequences occur.
His statement: (And the difference, etc.) Where we meant by it the initial existence, if you meant by it permanence.
Consideration: For it would require that the eternal not need the cause at all, since every moment that is supposed is a moment of its persistence, and those who say that possibility is the cause of need definitely do not commit to that. Moreover, this tends to considering origination along with possibility alone, so reflect.
His statement: (With respect to the original existence) If he had omitted this qualifier to promote the answer, it would have been better and more consonant with his statement: 'And there is no effect in it, as you admitted, so the essence remains without a cause.' But he restricted it to indicate the weakness of the answer initially.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the argument about whether possibility (imkan) is the cause of the contingent's need for a cause, especially in the state of persistence (baqa'). The commentator presents an objection: if possibility is what necessitates the need, then even in persistence the contingent would still need a cause because possibility remains (since it is a necessary concomitant of the essence, like essential necessity and impossibility). Then he examines the response: in the state of persistence, the effect of the cause is either the existence itself (which would be 'acquisition of the acquired') or a renewed thing (which would mean the cause does not affect the remaining entity). The commentator proceeds to discuss several points: (1) The notion that origination (huduth) as a cause of need is also refuted because origination is a relation of precedence that remains in persistence. (2) The three modes (wujub, imkan, imtina') are secondary intelligibles; the actual attribution is mental, but the attributive extraction (intiza'i) is a necessary concomitant of the essence. The causality of possibility for need is in this latter sense. (3) A further objection about whether the absence of persistence is a condition for the need, to which the answer is given. (4) The crux of the defense: the cause's effect in persistence is the permanence (dawam) of the existence, not a new existence. If one calls permanence 'renewed,' the dispute becomes verbal. The commentator explains each gloss (qawluhu) on the matn, clarifying that the effect is on the remaining entity in its persistence, not on the essence regarding original existence. He also notes a final doubt: if the cause's effect is only on permanence, then an eternal being would not need a cause at any moment (since it always persists), but those who hold possibility as the cause do not accept that. The passage concludes by suggesting that the qualifier 'with respect to original existence' might be omitted for a better answer.

Correction: The effect is the permanence of the existence, which is a middle ground.

The Objection and Answer on the Effect of the Agent on the Possible's Persistence
Presents the objection and answer on the agent's effect, reducing to a verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that the agent's effect on the persistent possible is on its duration, not a renewed existence, and that the dispute reduces to a verbal disagreement.

Core Idea
The agent's effect on persistence is continuation, not new creation.

This passage from al-Mawaqif presents a classic objection and response concerning how an agent affects a possible entity after its initial creation. The objection claims the effect is on a renewed persistence, while the answer shows that this leads to a contradiction unless we recognize the disagreement as merely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: the claim is that the agent's influence is on the persistence (baqā') of the possible, which is a renewed thing, not on its essence (dhāt) with respect to the original existence.
Note the key term 'mutajaddid' (renewed).
2
See the counter-argument
The response argues that if the agent only affects persistence, then the essence remains possible without an effect at the moment of persistence, leading to a contradiction (khalaf).
This exposes the flaw in the objection.
3
Grasp the answer
The answer clarifies that the agent's effect on the persistent possible is neither a repetition of what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) nor a production of a renewed thing; rather, it is that the duration (dawām) of the possible is due to the agent's continued causation.
The effect is on the continuation of the initial existence.
4
Recognize the verbal dispute
The passage concludes that if one calls this duration 'renewed' because it was not present at the first moment, the dispute becomes merely verbal (lafẓī). Both sides agree on the reality, differing only on terminology.
Understanding this resolves the apparent conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الرابع: في أبحاث الممكن لذاته والمقدر خلافه . ( لا يقال : تأثيره في بقائه الذي هو أمر متجدد، لا في ذاته بحسب أصل الوجود الذي كان حاصلا) لأنا) نقول : الذات ممكنة حال البقاء ولا تأثير فيها كما اعترفتم به فتبقى الذات بلا مؤثر فيها فتكون مستغنية عنه مع ثبوت إمكانها المحوج إياها إليه فرضا هذا خلف. ( والجواب : أنه أي التأثير في الممكن الباقي ليس تحصيلاً للحاصل، ولا تحصيلاً للمتجدد بل تأثيره فيه هو أن يكون دوامه لدوامه كما كان وجوده أولى من وجوده فإن سمي الدوام متجدداً لأنه لم یکن حاصلاً في أول زمان الوجود صار النزاع لفظياً ; لأنا نقول : التأثير في دوام الوجود الحاصل أولاً لا في أمر متجدد هو وجود ابتدائي، وأنتم تقولون : لا تأثير في الوجود الحاصل أولاً ، بل في أمر متجدد هو دوامه، فالمعنى واحد والاختلاف في أن المراد بلفظ المتجدد ماذا؟
The Third Observation - The Fourth Objective: On the investigations of the possible in itself. And what is presupposed is otherwise. (It is not said: His effect is on its persistence) which is a renewed thing, (not on its essence) according to the original existence that had been obtained. (For we) say: The essence is possible in the state of persistence, and there is no effect in it as you have admitted, so the essence remains without any effect in it, thus it becomes independent of Him despite the proof of its possibility that necessitates it to Him, hypothetically; this is a contradiction. (And the answer: That it) i.e., the effect on the persistent possible is neither a fulfillment of the already obtained, nor a production of the renewed, but rather His effect in it is that its duration be through His duration just as its existence first was from His existence. For if duration is called 'renewed' because it was not obtained in the first moment of existence, the dispute becomes verbal; for we say: The effect is on the duration of the existence obtained first, not on a renewed thing that is an initial existence, and you say: There is no effect on the existence obtained first, but rather on a renewed thing which is its duration. So the meaning is one, and the disagreement is about what is meant by the term 'renewed'.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning the effect of the agent (al-mu'aththir) on the possible entity (al-mumkin) during its state of persistence (baqā'). The objection claims that the agent's effect is on the persistence of the possible, which is a renewed thing (amr mutajaddid), and not on its essence (dhāt) with respect to the original existence already obtained. The response (al-jawāb) counter-argues that this leads to a contradiction: the essence remains without any effect from the agent at the time of persistence, yet it is still possible and thus in need of an agent. The answer then clarifies that the effect on the persistent possible is neither a repetition of what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) nor a production of a renewed thing; rather, the agent's effect is that the continued existence of the possible is due to the agent's continued causation, just as its initial existence was due to the agent. If one calls this duration 'renewed' because it was not present at the first moment, the dispute becomes merely verbal (lafẓī). The conclusion is that both sides agree on the underlying reality: the agent's effect is on the continuation of the initial existence, not on a new initial existence; the difference is only over the terminology of 'renewed'.

Key Takeaway
The agent's effect on the possible's persistence is a continuation of the initial existence, and any disagreement over calling it 'renewed' is merely verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that your continued existence at every moment depends on God just as your initial creation did. When you wake up each morning, do you truly feel that your being is sustained by Him at that very moment, or do you act as if your existence is self-powered? Commit today to a specific moment—perhaps the first breath of the day—where you consciously acknowledge that your persistence is from God alone.
?
The dispute in this lesson was about words, but the reality remained that creatures are utterly dependent on their Creator. In your daily life, is your recognition of God's ongoing sustenance merely a verbal profession, or does it change how you live? Identify one area where you tend to feel independent (e.g., health, provision, ability) and make a concrete commitment to acknowledge God's continuous creation in that area this week.
