Critique and Clarification of the First Answer on Persistence
Critiques the first answer from al-Muḥaṣṣal and provides a clear clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether persistence (baqā') is a new attribute requiring a distinct act of God or whether it is the continuation of original existence, and how the author's formulation resolves the issue.

Core Idea
Persistence is not a new thing; it is the continuation of the same existence, requiring the agent's ongoing causation.

This passage examines a key objection to the standard Ash'ari view on persistence. The critic argues that persistence is a distinct new attribute, while the author insists it is merely the duration of the original existence. The discussion clarifies how the possible remains dependent on the agent in both origination and persistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage includes both the main argument and glosses. Focus on the main thread first.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice the critic's position from Naqd al-Muḥaṣṣal (persistence is a new thing) and the author's objection. See how the author reformulates the effect on original existence by virtue of its duration.
The key difference is whether the effect is on a new persistence or on the continuation of the same existence.
3
Understand the resolution
The author's formulation avoids the problem of 'talṣīl al-ḥāṣil' (fulfilling the already fulfilled) by not treating persistence as a separate attribute conferred upon an already persistent essence.
The critic's view leads to the objection that possibility remains with respect to original existence.
4
Grasp the final clarification
The summary (khulāṣa) states that both original existence and its continuation are not entailed by the possible's essence, so each requires the agent. This is the core insight.
This clarifies why the possible needs the agent at every moment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الجواب الأول مذكور في نقد المحصل، وليس فيه أنه لا تأثير في ذات الممكن حتى يتجه عليه ما أورده المصنف، بل فيه أن تأثير المؤثر في أمر جديد هو البقاء، فإنه غير الإحداث فهو مؤثر في أمر جديد صار به باقياً، لا في الذات الذي كان باقياً، ومعناه أنه إذا أخذ الذات مع البقاء موصوفاً به لم أن يفيده المؤثر البقاء بهذا الاعتبار، وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل. وإذا أخذ وحده كان بقاؤه مستفاداً منه ولا شك أن البقاء هو دوام الوجود، فيكون الذات باعتبار دوام وجوده مستنداً إلى المؤثر. وهذا بعينه ما آثره ولا فرق، إلا في تسمية البقاء أي الدوام متجدداً وتوضيح المقام بما لا مزيد عليه في تحقيق المرام أن يقال : كما أن اتصاف الممكن بالوجود في زمان حدوثه لم يكن مقتضى ذاته؛ لاستواء
قوله : ( أن تأثير المؤثر إلخ) لا يخفى أن عبارته تدل دلالة ظاهرة على أن التأثير في البقاء، وأنه أمر جديد لم يكن حال الإحداث، وأنه سبب لصيرورته باقياً، فهو باق بهذا البقاء لا ببقاء سابق عليه حتى لزم تحصيل الحاصل. وحينئذ يرد عليه ما أورده المصنف من أن الإمكان بالنظر إلى أصل الوجود باق ولا حاجة فيه إلى المؤثر بل في البقاء الذي هو أمر جديد نعم لو قيل كما ذكره المصنف من أن التأثير في أصل الوجود باعتبار دوامه واستمراره في الأزمنة الآتية، كما كان فى الزمان الأول، تم الجواب وانقلعت الشبهة وشتان بين العبارتين والتأويل بمجرد حسن الظن تكلف .
قوله : ( ولا شك أن البقاء إلخ إن أريد استفادة هذه المقدمة من عبارته فممنوع، بل المستفاد منها أنه أمر جديد لم يكن وقت الإحداث والتأثير واقع فيه وإن أراد صدقها في الواقع فلا يجدي في تطبيق عبارته كما لا يخفى .
قوله: (ولا) فرق إلا في تسميته إلخ) الحصر ممنوع ; لتحقق الفرق باعتبار أن الناقد اعتبر التأثير في البقاء الذي هو متجدد، والمصنف اعتبر التأثير في أصل الوجود يكون دوامه لدوامه .
قوله : ( حتى يتجه عليه ما أورده المصنف) أي حتى يتجه عليه ظاهراً، والافتاويله بما أشار إليه الشارح من أن المراد نفي التأثير في الذات بحسب أصل الوجود ممكن، ويحتمل أن يريد أنه ليس فيه هذا الحكم محمولاً على ظاهره حتى يتجه ما أورده المصنف .
قوله : ( وتوضيح المقام بما لا مزيد عليه إلخ خلاصته أن هاهنا أمرين أصل الوجود واستمراره وشيء منهما ليس مقتضى ذات الممكن، فيحتاج في كل منهما إلى الفاعل فإن قلت : معلول الإمكان هو الاحتياج إلى المؤثر في الوجود الابتدائي، وقد تخلف في حالة البقاء، قلت : بعد تسليم تخلف نفس الاحتياج فيه معلول الإمكان هو الاحتياج في الاتصاف بنفس الوجود، فإن كان عقيب العدم يفيد العلة الاتصاف بالوجود الابتدائي، أي الوجود في زمان الحدوث، وإن كان حالة البقاء يفيد الاتصاف به فيما بعده، كما صرح به
And know that the first answer is mentioned in Naqd al-Muḥaṣṣal, and it does not contain that there is no effect in the essence of the possible such that what the author (al-Muṣannif) adduced would apply to it. Rather it contains that the effect of the agent is on a new thing, which is persistence (al-baqā'), for it is other than origination (al-iḥdāth). So He is an effect on a new thing by which it becomes persistent, not on the essence that was persistent. And its meaning is that if the essence is taken together with persistence as described by it, then the agent does not confer persistence upon it in this respect; otherwise the fulfillment of the already obtained would follow. And if it is taken alone, its persistence is derived from Him, and there is no doubt that persistence is the duration of existence, so the essence, by virtue of the duration of its existence, is dependent on the agent. And this is exactly what he (al-Muṣannif) presented, and there is no difference except in calling persistence — i.e., duration — 'renewed'. And the clarification of the position, beyond which there is no more, in achieving the objective is to say: Just as the description of the possible with existence at the time of its origination was not a requirement of its essence because of equality...
His saying: (That the effect of the agent, etc.) It is not hidden that his statement indicates a clear indication that the effect is on persistence, and that it is a new thing that did not exist at the time of origination, and that it is the cause of its becoming persistent. So it is persistent by this persistence, not by a preceding persistence, such that the fulfillment of the already obtained would follow. And then the objection raised by al-Muṣannif applies to him, namely that possibility with respect to original existence remains and there is no need for the agent in it, but rather in the persistence which is a new thing. Yes, if it were said as al-Muṣannif mentioned — that the effect is on the original existence by virtue of its duration and continuation in the coming times, just as it was in the first time — then the answer would be complete and the doubt would be removed, and there is a vast difference between the two statements, and interpretation by mere good opinion is a strain.
His saying: (And there is no doubt that persistence, etc.) If one intends to derive this premise from his statement, it is prevented; rather what is derived from it is that it is a new thing that did not exist at the time of origination and the effect occurs in it. And if he intends its truth in reality, it does not avail in applying his statement, as is not hidden.
His saying: (And no) difference except in its naming, etc.) The exclusivity is prevented, because the difference is realized in that the critic (al-Nāqid) considered the effect on persistence which is renewed, while al-Muṣannif considered the effect on the original existence such that its duration is through His duration.
His saying: (So that what al-Muṣannif adduced applies to him) i.e., so that it applies to him apparently, and it is possible to interpret it by what the commentator (al-Shāriḥ) indicated — that what is meant is the negation of effect in the essence according to original existence. And it is probable that he intends that this judgment is not present in it when taken at face value, such that what al-Muṣannif adduced would apply to it.
His saying: (And the clarification of the position beyond which there is no more, etc.) Its conclusion is that here there are two things: the original existence and its continuation, and neither of them is a requirement of the essence of the possible, so each of them requires the agent. For if you say: The effect of possibility is the need for the agent in the initial existence, and it has failed to occur in the state of persistence, I say: After conceding the failure of the very need, the effect of possibility is the need in the description with existence itself. For if it is after non-existence, the cause brings about the description with the initial existence, i.e., existence in the time of origination; and if it is in the state of persistence, it brings about the description with it in what follows after it, as he explicitly stated.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis and clarification of the first answer regarding the agent's effect on the persistent possible. It begins by noting that the answer in Naqd al-Muḥaṣṣal does not deny effect on the essence; rather it states that the effect is on a new thing, namely persistence (baqā'), which is distinct from origination (iḥdāth). The passage then explains the meaning: if the essence is considered already qualified by persistence, the agent does not confer persistence again (otherwise it would be a repetition), but if the essence is considered alone, its persistence is derived from the agent. It concludes that this is exactly what al-Muṣannif (the author) presented, and the only difference is terminological — calling persistence 'renewed' (mutajaddid).

Next, the passage defends al-Muṣannif's formulation against the critique by showing that the critic's statement (from al-Muḥaṣṣal) actually leads to a confusion: if persistence is a new thing, then the possibility remains with respect to original existence without need for the agent, which invites the objection. Al-Muṣannif's formulation — that the effect is on the original existence in terms of its continuation — avoids this. The passage then refutes attempts to harmonize the two by interpretation, insisting on the difference.

Finally, a clear summary (khulāṣa) is given: there are two aspects — original existence (aṣl al-wujūd) and its continuation (istimrāruh) — neither of which is entailed by the possible's essence, so each requires an agent. The agent's causation in the state of persistence is the same as in origination: it brings about the description of existence in the subsequent moment.

Key Takeaway
The possible's persistence is not a separate creation but the continuation of its existence, which requires the agent's continuous support.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you reflect on your past achievements or even your current state, do you attribute their continuity and stability to God's ongoing support or to your own power? How does this lesson challenge your sense of ownership over your own existence?
?
If God were to withdraw His sustaining power for a single instant, you would cease to exist. How does this reality affect the way you prioritize your relationship with Him over your daily concerns? What is one concrete step you can take today to remind yourself of this dependence?

The First Answer on Influence and Persistence
Critique of the first answer regarding influence on persistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first answer from Naqd al-Muhassal regarding the agent's influence on persistence and the objection that it leads to tahsil al-hasil.

Core Idea
Persistence must be a new act of influence, but that leaves the original existence untouched.

The commentator examines the first answer that claims the agent's influence is on persistence as a new matter. He clarifies that this answer avoids influencing the essence but raises a question: if influence is only on persistence, what about the original existence? The objection from the author is that this implies the possible's existence is independent of the agent in its first moment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first answer's claim
Read the passage and identify what the first answer says: the agent's influence is on persistence (al-baqā') as a new matter, not on the essence. Note that the answer is from Naqd al-Muhassal.
2
Identify the objection (tahsil al-hasil)
See how taking the essence with persistence would lead to obtaining what is already obtained. The commentator explains the reasoning: if the essence already has persistence, influence on it would be redundant.
3
See the commentator's defense of the author's alternative
The commentator shows that the first answer essentially matches what the author prefers, except for naming. Recognize that the author's formulation avoids the objection by focusing on continuance of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد بلفظ المتجدد ماذا؟ . واعلم أن الجواب الأول مذكور في نقد المحصل، وليس فيه أنه لا تأثير في ذات الممكن حتى يتجه عليه ما أورده المصنف، بل فيه أن تأثير المؤثر في أمر جديد هو البقاء، فإنه غير الإحداث فهو مؤثر في أمر جديد صار به باقياً، لا في الذات الذي كان باقياً، ومعناه أنه إذا أخذ الذات مع البقاء موصوفاً به لم أن يفيده المؤثر البقاء بهذا الاعتبار، وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل. وإذا أخذ وحده كان بقاؤه مستفاداً منه ولا شك أن البقاء هو دوام الوجود، فيكون الذات باعتبار دوام وجوده مستنداً إلى المؤثر. وهذا بعينه ما آثره ولا فرق، إلا في تسمية البقاء أي الدوام متجدداً وتوضيح المقام بما لا مزيد عليه في تحقيق المرام أن يقال : كما أن اتصاف الممكن بالوجود في زمان حدوثه لم يكن مقتضى ذاته؛ لاستواء يتصور قوله : ( أن تأثير المؤثر إلخ) لا يخفى أن عبارته تدل دلالة ظاهرة على أن التأثير في البقاء، وأنه أمر جديد لم يكن حال الإحداث، وأنه سبب لصيرورته باقياً، فهو باق بهذا البقاء لا ببقاء سابق عليه حتى لزم تحصيل الحاصل. وحينئذ يرد عليه ما أورده المصنف من أن الإمكان بالنظر إلى أصل الوجود باق ولا حاجة فيه إلى المؤثر بل في البقاء الذي هو أمر جديد نعم لو قيل كما ذكره المصنف من أن التأثير في أصل الوجود باعتبار دوامه واستمراره في الأزمنة الآتية، كما كان فى الزمان الأول، تم الجواب وانقلعت الشبهة وشتان بين العبارتين والتأويل بمجرد حسن الظن تكلف . قوله : ( ولا شك أن البقاء إلخ إن أريد استفادة هذه المقدمة من عبارته فممنوع، بل المستفاد منها أنه أمر جديد لم يكن وقت الإحداث والتأثير واقع فيه وإن أراد صدقها في الواقع فلا يجدي في تطبيق عبارته كما لا يخفى . قوله: (ولا) فرق إلا في تسميته إلخ) الحصر ممنوع ؛ لتحقق الفرق باعتبار أن الناقد اعتبر التأثير في البقاء الذي هو متجدد، والمصنف اعتبر التأثير في أصل الوجود يكون دوامه لدوامه . قوله : ( حتى يتجه عليه ما أورده المصنف) أي حتى يتجه عليه ظاهراً، والافتاويله بما أشار إليه الشارح من أن المراد نفي التأثير في الذات بحسب أصل الوجود ممكن، ويحتمل أن يريد أنه ليس فيه هذا الحكم محمولاً على ظاهره حتى يتجه ما أورده المصنف .
What is meant by the term 'the renewing'? Know that the first answer is mentioned in Naqd al-Muhassal, and it does not contain that there is no influence in the essence of the possible such that the author's objection would apply against it. Rather, it contains that the influence of the agent is upon a new matter, which is persistence (al-baqā'), for it is other than origination (al-iḥdāth). So He is influential in a new matter by which it becomes enduring, not in the essence that was enduring. Its meaning is that if the essence is taken together with persistence as described by it, the agent does not benefit it persistence in this respect, otherwise there would be the acquisition of what is already obtained. And if it is taken alone, its persistence is derived from Him, and there is no doubt that persistence is the continuance of existence. So the essence, by virtue of the continuance of its existence, is dependent upon the agent. And this is precisely what he [the author] preferred, and there is no difference except in naming persistence—i.e., continuance—as 'renewing'. And clarifying the matter in a way with nothing further possible in achieving the goal is to say: Just as the attribution of the possible with existence at the time of its temporal origination was not necessitated by its essence—because of the equality [of its relation to existence and non-existence]—imagine. His saying: (That the influence of the agent, etc.) It is not hidden that his expression clearly indicates that the influence is upon persistence, and that it is a new matter that was not present at the time of origination, and that it is the cause of its becoming enduring. So it is enduring by this persistence, not by a prior persistence such that the acquisition of what is already obtained would follow. Then the author's objection applies against it: that possibility with respect to the original existence remains and has no need for the agent, but rather [the need is] for persistence, which is a new matter. Yes, if it were said as the author mentioned—that the influence is upon the original existence by virtue of its continuance and persistence in the times to come, just as it was in the first time—the answer would be complete and the doubt would be removed. And how far apart are the two expressions! And interpretation based merely on good opinion is contrived. His saying: (And there is no doubt that persistence, etc.) If it is intended that this premise is derived from his expression, it is prevented. Rather, what is derived from it is that it is a new matter that was not present at the time of origination and influence. And if he intends its truth in reality, it does not benefit in applying his expression, as is not hidden. His saying: (And there is) no difference except in naming it, etc.) The exclusivity is prevented because a difference is realized by virtue of the fact that the critic considered influence upon persistence, which is renewing, while the author considered influence upon the original existence such that its continuance is due to its continuity. His saying: (Until what the author mentioned applies to it) meaning apparently it applies to it. And its interpretation by what the commentator indicated—that what is meant is the negation of influence upon the essence in terms of original existence—is possible. And it is probable that he intends that this judgment is not present in it taken at its face value such that what the author mentioned applies to it.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the first answer (al-jawāb al-awwal) concerning how the agent influences the possible entity (al-mumkin) in its persistence (al-baqā'). The commentator begins by clarifying what is meant by 'the renewing' (al-mutajaddid). He explains that the first answer, found in Naqd al-Muḥaṣṣal, does not claim that there is no influence on the essence; rather it claims that the influence is on a new matter, namely persistence, which is distinct from the original origination. The agent makes the entity endure by this persistence, not by a persistence already present—otherwise it would be tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil (obtaining what is already obtained). The commentator then notes that this is essentially what the author (al-Muṣannif) preferred, except for the naming of persistence as 'renewing'. He then quotes the author's objection: if the influence is on persistence, then the possible's original existence remains without need for the agent, contradicting the need for an agent at every moment. The commentator suggests that the author's own formulation—influence on original existence in terms of its continuance in future times—avoids the objection. The rest of the passage defends this view against the critic's expression, arguing that there is a real difference between the two formulations, not merely a terminological one. The commentator also addresses the interpretation of the original expression, noting that it might not be intended literally.

Key Terms
المتجدد (al-mutajaddid)
الجواب الأول (al-jawāb al-awwal)
البقاء (al-baqā')
تحصيل الحاصل (tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil)
الذات (al-dhāt)
الممكن (al-mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the first answer denies any influence on the possible at all.
Correction: The first answer actually affirms influence on persistence as a new matter, not on the essence itself.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, what does the agent influence?
A) The essence of the possible
B) Persistence as a new matter
C) Non-existence
D) The accident of time

Key Takeaway
The first answer attempts to avoid influencing the essence by making the agent's influence on persistence, but this raises the objection that the original existence remains unaffected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is renewed at every moment by Allah, why do you still act as if your next breath is guaranteed by your own strength? What would it mean to truly live in awareness of your constant dependence?
?
The first answer argued that persistence is a new act of influence. Do you experience your daily life as a series of new gifts from Allah, or do you take your continued existence for granted? How can you cultivate gratitude for each moment's persistence?
?
The principle of tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil warns against assuming something is already obtained. In your spiritual life, do you ever assume that spiritual states (like repentance or certainty) persist automatically without renewing them? What must you actively seek from Allah every day?

The Real Difference: Influence on Persistence vs. Influence on Continuance
Distinguishing two formulations of influence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the real difference between influence on persistence (as a new matter) and influence on original existence with continuance.

Core Idea
Influence on continuance is not the same as influence on a new thing called persistence.

The commentator insists that the difference between the two formulations is not merely terminological. He shows that the critic's expression leads to the objection, while the author's formulation avoids it. This lesson explores the exact point of divergence.

Guided Path
1
Examine the two formulations
Read the passage and identify the two formulations: the critic's (influence on persistence as a new matter) and the author's (influence on original existence with continuance).
2
Understand why they are different
Analyze how the critic's formulation implies that original existence is independent of the agent, while the author's directly links continuance to the agent's influence.
3
See the commentator's rejection of the terminological interpretation
The commentator refutes the claim that the only difference is in naming. He provides evidence from the expressions themselves that there is a substantive difference.

Source Text

?

?

Causality and Created Things

8 min
Two Aspects of Existence: Original and Continued
The commentator provides a definitive clarification of the two aspects—original existence and its continuation—and refutes misunderstandings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a contingent being requires an agent for both its initial existence and its continued existence, and how the commentator refutes common misunderstandings regarding influence on persistence.

Core Idea
A contingent being never outruns its need for the Creator—every moment of existence is a gift.

The commentator now provides a definitive clarification that resolves a key point in the causation debate. He distinguishes two aspects of existence—its beginning and its continuation—and shows that the contingent stands in equal need of the agent for both. He also addresses and refutes two common misunderstandings that have confused many minds. This passage offers a precise, nuanced understanding of how divine agency relates to the ongoing existence of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the full translation. Let the commentator's own argument flow through you before you interpret.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two aspects
The commentator says there are two things (amrān): original existence (aṣl al-wujūd) and its continuation (istimrāruh). Notice that neither is necessitated by the essence of the possible, so both require the agent.
This is the core thesis.
3
Trace the objection and reply
An objection asks: Doesn't the need for an influencer cease after initial existence? The reply: The need is for the attribution of existence itself, at every moment.
The objection is based on a misunderstanding of tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil.
4
Understand the refutations
The commentator refutes two false views: (1) that influence on the persistent is obtaining what is already obtained, and (2) that influence on a renewing matter is not influence on the persistent. He clarifies the true meaning of influence.
These refutations protect the correct understanding of divine agency.
5
Reflect on the final advice
The commentator ends: 'Be insightful in your matter, lest the situation become ambiguous due to changing expressions.' This reminds us that clarity comes from grasping underlying principles, not just words.

قوله : ( وتوضيح المقام بما لا مزيد عليه إلخ خلاصته أن هاهنا أمرين أصل الوجود واستمراره وشيء منهما ليس مقتضى ذات الممكن، فيحتاج في كل منهما إلى الفاعل فإن قلت : معلول الإمكان هو الاحتياج إلى المؤثر في الوجود الابتدائي، وقد تخلف في حالة البقاء، قلت : بعد تسليم تخلف نفس الاحتياج فيه معلول الإمكان هو الاحتياج في الاتصاف بنفس الوجود، فإن كان عقيب العدم يفيد العلة الاتصاف بالوجود الابتدائي، أي الوجود في زمان الحدوث، وإن كان حالة البقاء يفيد الاتصاف به فيما بعده، كما صرح به نسبته إلى وجوده وعدمه، كذلك انضمام ذلك الوجود إليه . وبقاء اتصافه به في الزمان الثاني وما بعده ليس مقتضى ذاته؛ لأن استواء نسبته إلى طرفيه أمر لازم له في حد ذاته فكما استحال اقتضاؤه الوجود في الزمان الأول استحال اقتضاؤه إياه في الزمان الثاني. وكما أن اتصافه بالوجود في زمان الحدوث مستند إلى المؤثر، كذلك اتصافه به فيما بعد من الأزمنة مستند إليه أيضاً. والأول هو اتصافه بأصل الوجود، والثاني هو اتصافه ببقاء الوجود، فهو في وجوده ابتداء وفي استمراره محتاج إلى المؤثر الذي يفيده الوجود ويديمه له على معنى أنه يجعله متصفاً بالوجود، ويديم له ذلك الاتصاف لا على معنى أنه يوجد اتصافه بالوجود ويوجد دوام اتصافه به؛ لأن الاتصاف ودوامه أمران اعتباريان لا وجود لهما في الخارج، وقد نبهت على معنى التأثير والإيجاب فيما سبق ومن قال : إن التأثير في الباقي تحصيل للحاصل فقد وهم أن المؤثر يحصل في الزمان الثاني أصل الوجود الذي كان حاصلا أو وهم أنه يفيد البقاء ويحصله للممكن المأخوذ مع بقائه وكلاهما باطل ومن قال : إن التأثير إذا كان في أمر متجدد لا يكون تأثيراً في الباقي البتة فقد توهم أن ذلك المتجدد وجود ابتدائي وهو أيضاً باطل لأن التأثير في ذلك الوجود الحاصل لا في أصله بل في بقائه ودوامه الذي هو متجدد وما يقال من أن المعنى بالتأثير هو استتباع وجود المؤثر وجود الأثر، وذلك حاصل حال البقاء فراجع إلى ما ذكرناه من أن وجوده لوجوده و دوامه ،لدوامه فكن من أمرك على بصيرة كيلا يشتبه عليك الحال بتغير العبارات .
His saying: (And clarifying the matter in a way with nothing further possible, etc.) Its summary is that there are here two things: original existence (aṣl al-wujūd) and its continuation (istimrāruh). Neither of them is necessitated by the essence of the possible, so in each of them it needs the agent. If you say: The effect of possibility is the need for the influencer in the initial existence, and that [need] has ceased in the state of persistence, I say: After conceding the cessation of the need itself, the effect of possibility is the need in the attribution (al-ittiṣāf) with existence itself. For if [existence] is subsequent to non-existence, the cause confers the attribution with initial existence—i.e., existence at the time of temporal origination. And if it is in the state of persistence, it confers the attribution with it thereafter, as he explicitly stated: 'its relation to its existence and non-existence is the same, likewise the addition of that existence to it.' And the persistence of its attribution with it in the second time and thereafter is not necessitated by its essence, because the equality of its relation to its two sides is a necessary concomitant of it in itself. Just as it is impossible for it to necessitate existence in the first time, so too it is impossible for it to necessitate it in the second time. And just as its attribution with existence at the time of temporal origination is dependent on the agent, likewise its attribution with it in the times afterwards is also dependent on Him. The first is its attribution with original existence, and the second is its attribution with the persistence of existence. So in its existence initially and in its continuation, it needs the agent who confers existence upon it and perpetuates it for it, in the sense that He makes it attributed with existence and perpetuates for it that attribution—not in the sense that He creates its attribution with existence and creates the continuance of its attribution with it, because the attribution and its continuance are two relational matters (amrān iʿtibāriyān) that have no existence in the external. And I have already alerted to the meaning of influence (al-taʾthīr) and necessitation (al-ījāb) in what preceded. And whoever says that influence on the persistent is the acquisition of what is already obtained has wrongly imagined that the agent obtains in the second time the original existence that was already obtained, or wrongly imagined that He bestows persistence and obtains it for the possible taken together with its persistence—both are false. And whoever says that if influence is upon a renewing matter, it is not influence on the persistent at all, has wrongly imagined that that renewing is initial existence—that too is false, because the influence is upon that obtained existence, not upon its origin, but upon its persistence and continuance, which is renewing. And what is said that the meaning of influence is the entailment (istitbāʿ) of the existence of the agent for the existence of the effect—and that obtains in the state of persistence—reverts to what we have mentioned: that its existence is due to His existence, and its continuance is due to His continuance. So be insightful in your matter, lest the situation become ambiguous to you due to the changing of expressions.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a definitive clarification (tawḍīḥ al-maqām) of the central issue: the possible entity (al-mumkin) is dependent on the agent for both its original existence (aṣl al-wujūd) and its continuation (istimrār) or persistence (baqā'). The commentator summarizes that there are two things: the initial existence and its continuation; neither is necessitated by the essence of the possible, because the possible has an equal relation to existence and non-existence at all times. Therefore, at the moment of origination, the agent confers existence; at later moments, the agent confers the continuation of existence—not by creating a new existence, but by perpetuating the attribution (ittiṣāf) of the essence with existence. The commentator refutes two misunderstandings: (1) that influence on the persistent would be tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil (obtaining what is already obtained)—this assumes the agent re-creates the same existence, which is false; (2) that influence on a renewing matter implies it is not influence on the persistent—this confuses continuation with a new origination. The correct view is that influence is on the obtained existence in terms of its persistence, which is a renewing aspect. Finally, the commentator notes that influence is the entailment of the effect's existence by the agent's existence, and this holds in both origination and persistence.

Key Terms
أصل الوجود (aṣl al-wujūd)
استمراره (istimrāruh)
الاتصاف (al-ittiṣāf)
البقاء (al-baqā')
تحصيل الحاصل (tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil)
التأثير (al-ta'thīr)
الإيجاب (al-ījāb)
أمر اعتباري (amr iʿtibārī)
استتباع (istitbāʿ)
دوام (dawām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that persistence requires a new act of creation each moment, i.e., that the agent re-creates the same existence.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the agent does not re-create the original existence but perpetuates the same attribution of existence. The agent's influence is on the continuation of the same existence, not on a new origination.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the claim that influence on the persistent is tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil?
A) Because influence is on the original existence, not on persistence
B) Because the agent obtains persistence without re-creating the original existence
C) Because persistence is not an attribute of the possible
D) Because tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil is only a fallacy in mathematics

Key Takeaway
A contingent being depends on the Creator for every moment of its existence—both its beginning and its continuation—and this dependence is understood as the perpetuation of the same attribution of existence, not a new creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does knowing that your existence at this very moment is not from yourself but from Allah's direct action change the way you view your independence and accomplishments? What would it look like to live each moment acknowledging that dependency?
?
Think of a time when you felt self-sufficient or forgetful of Allah. How does the teaching that your continued existence requires His continuous perpetuation challenge that feeling? What concrete step can you take today to remind yourself of this reliance?
?
If every moment of your life is a direct gift from Allah, not just a one-time creation, then what is the purpose of each moment? How should this affect your use of time and your relationship with your Creator?

The Sixth Doubt: The Dilemma of the Agent
The doubt enumerates two alternatives for the agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth doubt in kalam: the dilemma that if the agent is eternal, there is no cause for specific times, and if originated, it leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
The sixth doubt traps the opponent: the agent is either eternal or originated, both lead to problems.

Sanusi presents the sixth doubt, which challenges the principle that the possible needs an agent. He formulates a dilemma: the agent of accidents must be either eternal or originated. If eternal, no cause for temporal specificity; if originated, infinite regress follows. Let's examine this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Notice the structure: a conditional followed by two possibilities.
The passage ends with 'وهو محال'.
2
Identify the two horns
Find the two alternatives: the agent is eternal or originated. Under each, see the consequence.
3
Understand why each horn is problematic
If eternal, no specification of times. If originated, infinite regress. Ask: why are these problematic?
The doubt aims to show that the principle of needing an agent leads to absurdity.
4
Reflect on the dilemma
Consider how a defender of kalam might respond. This prepares you for the next lesson on the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة ( السادسة : لو كان ( الإمكان أو الحدوث محوجاً إلى المؤثر كان ( للحوادث) التي نشاهدها (مؤثر) إما لحدوثها وإما لإمكانها، (فإما أن يقال ذلك المؤثر ( قديم فيلزم (حدوثها أي حدوث تلك الحوادث في أوقاتها المخصوصة ( بلا سبب) مخصص لتلك الأوقات بالحدوث من الأوقات السابقة عليها مع كونها متساوية في أن ذلك المؤثر القديم موجود فيها، (وإما ) أن يقال ذلك المؤثر (حادث)، فيكون محتاجاً إلى مؤثر آخر حادث أيضاً، (فيتسلسل) وهو محال. (قلنا): المؤثر في قوله : ( فإما أن يقال ذلك المؤثر) أي الفاعل المستجمع لجميع شرائط التأثير. قوله : ( حادث) إما بذاته أو بشرط من شرائط تأثيره . قوله: (وهو محال) فيه بحث، لجواز أن يكون شرط تأثيره أمراً اعتبارياً متجدداً يقتضي ذاته التجدد والتقضي، فإنه كما في الوجود أمر غير قار الذات لا ينقبض العقل من أن يكون المعدوم كذلك، أو يكون تجدده بسبب تجدد أمر آخر وهكذا فيلزم التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية .
The sixth doubt: If possibility or temporal origination necessitates the agent, then the observed accidents (al-ḥawādith) have an agent either due to their temporal origination or due to their possibility. Then either it is said that that agent is eternal, and then it follows that they occur—i.e., those accidents occur at their specific times—without a cause (sabab) specifying those times for occurrence from among the times preceding them, despite their equality in that that eternal agent exists in them; or it is said that that agent is originated (ḥādith), and then it is in need of another originated agent, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. (We said): The agent in… His saying: (Then either it is said that that agent) i.e., the agent possessing all conditions of influence. His saying: (originated) either in itself or by a condition of its influence. His saying: (and it is impossible) is debatable, because it is possible that the condition of its influence is a relational matter (amr iʿtibārī) that is renewing, whose essence necessitates renewal and passing away. For just as in existence there is a non-stable essence, the mind does not shrink from the non-existent being like that. Or its renewal is due to the renewal of another matter, and so on, leading to infinite regress in relational matters.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth doubt (al-shubha al-sādisa) concerning the necessity of an agent for the possible or originated. The doubt argues: if possibility or temporal origination (ḥudūth) necessitates an agent, then for every observed accident (ḥādith) there must be an agent. Now, that agent is either eternal (qadīm) or originated (ḥādith). If eternal, then since the eternal agent is equally present at all times, there would be no cause (sabab) to specify the occurrence of accidents at their particular times rather than others. If originated, then that agent itself needs another originated agent, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is absurd. Thus, the doubt attempts to show that the principle that the possible needs an agent leads to a dilemma. The commentator then begins to answer, but the response is cut short in this window. He quotes the original text's three statements and comments on each. Regarding the impossibility of infinite regress, he notes there is room for discussion (fīhi baḥth), because it is possible that the condition of the agent's influence is a relational matter (amr iʿtibārī) that is renewing, and such relational matters can be posited without infinite regress in actual existents. The text ends with a suggestion that such regress in relational matters might be acceptable.

Key Terms
الشبهة السادسة
الحدوث
الإمكان
المؤثر
القديم
الحادث
السبب
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sixth doubt shows that the concept of a creator is logically impossible.
Correction: The doubt only presents a dilemma; it does not prove impossibility. The answer will show that the dilemma can be resolved by considering relational conditions.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth doubt, if the agent is eternal, what problem arises?
A) The agent must be originated
B) There is no cause for specific times
C) Infinite regress
D) The agent is multiple

Key Takeaway
The sixth doubt presents a dilemma: an eternal agent leaves times unspecified, an originated agent leads to regress—thus challenging the need for an agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see events happening in your life at specific times, do you assume they are merely random or caused by your own choices? How does the dilemma of an eternal agent challenge you to see a deeper Cause behind every moment?
?
This doubt suggests that if the agent is eternal, there is no reason for things to happen when they do. Does your life feel like a series of accidents, or can you sincerely attribute each moment's timing to a wise, eternal Will? What would change if you truly believed that nothing happens without His specification?
?
The impossibility of infinite regress forces you to stop at a First Agent. In your daily reliance on secondary causes—people, systems, your own efforts—do you ever pause to acknowledge that they are not ultimate? How can you make the remembrance of the First Agent a living reality in your next decision?

Answering the Sixth Doubt: Relational Matters and Regress
The answer comments on three statements from the original doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the beginning of the answer to the sixth doubt, focusing on the possibility that the condition of the agent's influence is a renewing relational matter, which permits infinite regress in such matters.

Core Idea
The answer weakens the regress objection: infinite regress in relational matters is not impossible.

Having presented the sixth doubt's dilemma, the commentator now begins to respond. He first clarifies the terms and then questions the impossibility claimed. He suggests that the condition of the agent's influence could be a non-stable relational matter, and that infinite regress in such matters may be acceptable.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic from 'قلنا' onward. Notice it comments on three parts of the original text.
2
Identify the three comments
Find each quote and its explanation: on 'that agent', on 'originated', on 'impossible'.
3
Understand the challenge to impossibility
Focus on the phrase 'فيه بحث'. The commentator argues that infinite regress in relational matters (أمور اعتبارية) is not impossible.
This is a key move in the response.
4
Grasp the concept of 'amr iʿtibārī'
A relational matter is non-stable and renewing. The mind can accept such regress.
This distinguishes between regress in actual existents and relational matters.

The Possible's Need for an Agent in Origination and Continuance
Al-Isfahani argues that the possible needs an agent for both origination and continuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that the possible-in-itself requires an agent not only for its initial existence but also for its continuance, and you will be able to distinguish the agent's influence on the original existence from its influence on continuance.

Core Idea
The possible's neutral essence makes it perpetually dependent on Allah for existence and continuance.

In this passage, Al-Isfahani extends the classic Ash'ari principle of contingency from the moment of origination to every subsequent moment. Just as a possible thing cannot bring itself into existence, it cannot sustain itself in existence. This argument is central to the Islamic theological understanding of continual divine sustenance (baqa'). Let's examine how the author builds this case and addresses objections.

Guided Path
1
Understand the parity argument
Al-Isfahani states that the possible's relation to existence and non-existence is equal in its essence. This remains true at every moment, so it cannot necessitate existence at any time, whether first or second.
The key premise is the essence does not change its neutral relation over time.
2
Note the conclusion: need for agent in both
Since the possible cannot necessitate existence at any moment, it needs an agent to give it existence initially and to perpetuate that existence later. The agent 'makes it characterized by existence' and 'perpetuates that characterization'.
The agent's role is not to create a new existence each moment but to sustain the attribute.
3
Distinguish the two aspects of influence
Al-Isfahani clarifies that the agent's influence is on the characterization (ittiṣāf) and its continuation, not on bringing into existence the characterization itself, which is a mental consideration (iʿtibārī).
This avoids the pitfall of positing separate external entities for the attribute.
4
Examine the refutations
Two objections are refuted: (1) the claim that influence on the enduring is 'obtaining what is already obtained' – Al-Isfahani shows this confuses sustaining with re-creating; (2) the claim that influence on a renewed thing cannot be on the enduring – this mistakes the renewed continuance for a new initial existence.
Both objections rest on misunderstandings of the agent's actual role.
5
Reflect on the closing warning
Al-Isfahani warns against being confused by changing terminology, emphasizing that the meaning of influence remains consistent: the agent's existence entails the effect's existence, both in origination and continuance.
This reinforces the need for precise conceptual understanding.

المرصد الثالث - المقصد الرابع: في أبحاث الممكن لذاته. نسبته إلى وجوده وعدمه، كذلك انضمام ذلك الوجود إليه. وبقاء اتصافه به في الزمان الثاني وما بعده ليس مقتضى ذاته؛ لأن استواء نسبته إلى طرفيه أمر لازم له في حد ذاته فكما استحال اقتضاؤه الوجود في الزمان الأول استحال اقتضاؤه إياه في الزمان الثاني. وكما أن اتصافه بالوجود في زمان الحدوث مستند إلى المؤثر، كذلك اتصافه به فيما بعد من الأزمنة مستند إليه أيضاً. والأول هو اتصافه بأصل الوجود، والثاني هو اتصافه ببقاء الوجود، فهو في وجوده ابتداء وفي استمراره محتاج إلى المؤثر الذي يفيده الوجود ويديمه له على معنى أنه يجعله متصفاً بالوجود، ويديم له ذلك الاتصاف لا على معنى أنه يوجد اتصافه بالوجود ويوجد دوام اتصافه به؛ لأن الاتصاف ودوامه أمران اعتباريان لا وجود لهما في الخارج. وقد نبهت على معنى التأثير والإيجاب فيما سبق. ومن قال: إن التأثير في الباقي تحصيل للحاصل فقد وهم أن المؤثر يحصل في الزمان الثاني أصل الوجود الذي كان حاصلاً أو وهم أنه يفيد البقاء ويحصله للممكن المأخوذ مع بقائه، وكلاهما باطل. ومن قال: إن التأثير إذا كان في أمر متجدد لا يكون تأثيراً في الباقي البتة فقد توهم أن ذلك المتجدد وجود ابتدائي وهو أيضاً باطل لأن التأثير في ذلك الوجود الحاصل لا في أصله بل في بقائه ودوامه الذي هو متجدد. وما يقال من أن المعنى بالتأثير هو استتباع وجود المؤثر وجود الأثر، وذلك حاصل حال البقاء فراجع إلى ما ذكرناه من أن وجوده لوجوده ودوامه لدوامه. فكن من أمرك على بصيرة كيلا يشتبه عليك الحال بتغير العبارات.
The Third Observatory - The Fourth Objective: On the investigations of the possible in itself. Its relation to its existence and non-existence is equal, likewise the attachment of that existence to it. And the continuance of its being characterized by it in the second moment and thereafter is not a requirement of its essence; because the equality of its relation to its two sides is a necessary property of it in its very essence, so just as its requiring existence in the first moment is impossible, so its requiring it in the second moment is impossible. And just as its being characterized by existence at the time of origination is dependent on the agent, so its being characterized by it in subsequent times is also dependent on it. The first is its being characterized by the original existence, and the second is its being characterized by the continuance of existence. So it is, in its existence at the beginning and in its continuance, in need of the agent which gives it existence and perpetuates it for it, in the sense that it makes it characterized by existence and perpetuates for it that characterization, not in the sense that it brings into existence its characterization by existence and brings into existence the duration of its characterization by it; because the characterization and its duration are two mental considerations having no existence in the external world. And I have already drawn attention to the meaning of influence and necessitation previously. Whoever says that influence on the enduring is the acquisition of what is already obtained has mistakenly thought that the agent produces in the second moment the original existence that was already present, or mistakenly thought that it gives continuance and obtains it for the possible taken together with its continuance; both are false. And whoever says that influence, if it concerns something renewed, is not influence on the enduring at all, has mistakenly thought that the renewed thing is an initial existence; this too is false, because the influence is on that obtained existence, not on its origin, but on its continuance and duration which is renewed. And what is said that the meaning of influence is the entailment of the existence of the agent for the existence of the effect, and that obtains in the state of continuance, refers back to what we mentioned: that its existence is for its existence and its duration is for its duration. So be fully aware of your matter, lest the state be confused for you by the change of expressions.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a metaphysical argument about the possible-in-itself (al-mumkin li-dhatihi) and its need for an agent (mu'aththir). The author, Al-Isfahani, begins by stating the standard Ash'ari position: the possible's relation to existence and non-existence is equal, and the attachment of existence to it is not intrinsic. He then extends this to the second moment and beyond: just as the possible cannot necessitate existence at the first moment, it cannot necessitate continuance of existence in later moments, because its essence remains equally related to both sides ad infinitum. Therefore, just as its being characterized by existence at the moment of origination is dependent on an agent, so is its being characterized by existence in subsequent times. He distinguishes two aspects: original existence (asl al-wujud) and continuance of existence (baqa' al-wujud). The possible needs the agent for both. He clarifies that the agent's influence is to make the possible characterized by existence and to perpetuate that characterization, not to create the characterization or its duration as separate entities, because those are mental considerations (i'tibariyan) without external existence. He then refutes two common misunderstandings: (1) the objection that influence on the enduring is 'obtaining what is already obtained' (tahsil al-hasil)—this mistakes the agent's act as re-creating the original existence rather than sustaining the characterization; (2) the claim that influence on a renewed thing cannot be influence on the enduring because it treats the renewed thing as a new existence, whereas the influence is on the continuance itself. He concludes by noting that the definition of influence as 'the entailment of the agent's existence for the effect's existence' applies equally to continuance, and warns against confusion arising from changing terminology.

Key Terms
ممكن (possible)
مؤثر (agent)
اتصاف (characterization)
بقاء (continuance)
اعتباري (mental consideration)
تحصيل الحاصل (obtaining what is already obtained)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a possible being, once created, can continue in existence without a sustaining cause, because the agent's influence is only needed for origination.
Correction: Al-Isfahani argues that the possible's essence remains equally related to non-existence at every moment, so it needs the agent to perpetually sustain its existence. The agent's influence is not just for the first moment but for every moment of continuance.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Isfahani, why does the possible need an agent for both origination and continuance of existence?
A) Because the possible's essence changes over time.
B) Because the possible's essence is equally related to existence and non-existence at every moment.
C) Because the possible cannot exist without a cause for its origin, but continuance is self-sufficient.
D) Because the possible's existence and continuance are two separate external entities.

Key Takeaway
The possible-in-itself is perpetually dependent on an agent for both its origination and continuance, because its essence is equally related to existence and non-existence at every moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that your very existence at this moment is not self-sustained—it is perpetually dependent on Allah. When you wake up tomorrow, will you truly feel that you are being re-created? Or do you live as if your own essence guarantees your next breath? Commit to a specific moment today to pause and say 'Allah is sustaining me right now.'
?
The passage warns that focusing on changing terminology can confuse the reality. In your own life, do you let sophisticated words or debates about faith distract you from the raw truth: that you need Allah every second? What one verbal distraction can you set aside this week to focus on the reality of your dependence?
?
Al-Isfahani says the possible's essence is equally related to existence and non-existence at every moment. If you truly believed that your existence hangs by a thread each instant, how would it change your relationship with sin, gratitude, or fear? Make a concrete change today—such as adding a dhikr that reminds you of your constant need for Allah.

The Sixth Doubt: The Dilemma of the Eternal Cause
Al-Isfahani presents the sixth doubt as a dilemma about the cause of temporal events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the sixth doubt in kalam: the dilemma that an eternal cause cannot specify temporal events without a specifier, and a temporal cause leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
The sixth doubt forces a choice between an eternal cause without specification and an infinite chain of temporal causes.

Before addressing the sixth doubt, Al-Isfahani presents a powerful dilemma: if temporal events need a cause, that cause must be either eternal or temporal. Both options seem problematic. This lesson unpacks the dilemma itself, setting the stage for the Ash'ari response.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the dilemma carefully. Note the two horns of the dilemma: eternal cause (no specifier) or temporal cause (infinite regress).
2
Identify the two alternatives
Find where the author states the cause could be eternal or temporal. Underline the consequences of each.
The eternal cause horn leads to lack of specification; the temporal cause leads to tasalsul.
3
Reflect on the problem
Why is the lack of a specifier problematic for an eternal cause? Why is infinite regress impossible in kalam? Consider the background of causal reasoning.
This is a standard argument to challenge the necessity of a cause for temporal events.

الشبهة السادسة: لو كان الإمكان أو الحدوث محوجاً إلى المؤثر، كان للحوادث التي نشاهدها مؤثر إما لحدوثها وإما لإمكانها. فإما أن يقال ذلك المؤثر قديم فيلزم حدوثها أي حدوث تلك الحوادث في أوقاتها المخصوصة بلا سبب مخصص لتلك الأوقات بالحدوث من الأوقات السابقة عليها مع كونها متساوية في أن ذلك المؤثر القديم موجود فيها. وإما أن يقال ذلك المؤثر حادث، فيكون محتاجاً إلى مؤثر آخر حادث أيضاً، فيتسلسل وهو محال. قلنا: المؤثر في الحوادث قديم مختار عندنا، وفعله تابع لإرادته. وتعلق إرادته بتخصيص الحدوث ببعض الأوقات مع تساويها لا يحتاج إلى داع بل له أن يختار أحد مقدوريه المتساويين على الآخر بلا سبب يدعوه إليه، فإن ذلك هو الكمال. والترجيح الصادر من الفاعل لأحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا لداع يدعوه إلى اختيار ذلك المقدور غير الوقوع أي وقوع أحد المتساويين بلا سبب مؤثر، والثاني هو المحال، لأنه ترجح أحد المتساويين من طرفي الممكن بلا سبب مرجح من خارج وقد عرفت بطلانه بالضرورة. وأما الأول فليس بمحال لأنه ترجيح من غير مرجح أي من غير داع يدعوه لا من غير ذات متصف بالترجيح ولا استحالة فيه؛ لأن المؤثر إذا كان مختاراً، فهو يرجح كيف يشاء. وفيه بحث وهو أن المختار وإن رجح أحد مقدوريه بإرادته، لكن إذا كانت إرادته لأحدهما مساوية لإرادته للآخر بالنظر إلى ذاته، وهذا التعلق إما أزلي فيكون المؤثر التام بجميع شرائطه قديماً، ولا يلزم قدم الحادث؛ لأنه تعلق إرادته بوقوعه في وقت مخصوص، ولا تخلف المعلول عن العلة التامة فإن التخلف فيما إذا كان المؤثر مختاراً أن يقع على خلاف ما أراده، فإذا أراد وقوعه في وقت مخصوص، فلو وقع قبل ذلك الوقت أو بعده كان تخلفاً كما إذا أراد وقوعه على كيفية مخصوصة فلو وقع على كيفية أخرى كان تخلفاً. أو تعلق إرادته متجدد فيكون المؤثر التام حادثاً، وتخصص التعلق بوقت دون آخر بذات الإرادة فإن شأنها التخصيص بلا مخصص ذكره الشارح قدس سره، أو بتعلق آخر متجدد ويلزم التسلسل في التعلقات، لكونها أموراً اعتبارية. قوله: (وفيه بحث إلخ) حاصله: أن الترجيح بلا مرجح باطل؛ لأنه يستلزم الترجح بلا مرجح أو التسلسل. قوله: The sixth doubt: If possibility or temporal origination necessitates an agent, then the temporal events we observe must have an agent, either due to their origination or due to their possibility. Then either that agent is said to be eternal, and it would necessitate their occurrence—i.e., the occurrence of those temporal events at their specific times—without a cause that specifies those times for occurrence from among the times preceding them, while they are equal in that that eternal agent exists in them. Or that agent is said to be temporal, and then it would need another temporal agent, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. We said: The agent for temporal events is eternal, free (mukhtar) according to us, and His act follows His will. And the attachment (ta'allaq) of His will to specifying occurrence at some times, despite their equality, does not need a motive (da'i); rather, He may choose one of His two equal potentialities over the other without a cause that calls Him to it, for that is perfection. And the preference (tarjih) issuing from the agent for one of His two potentialities over the other without a motive that calls Him to choose that potentiality is nothing but the occurrence—i.e., one of the two equals occurring without a causative cause—and the second [i.e., the latter] is impossible, because it is the preponderance (tarajjuh) of one of the two equals from the two sides of the possible without a preponderating cause from outside, and you have already known its impossibility necessarily. As for the first, it is not impossible, because it is a preference (tarjih) without a preponderator (murajjih), i.e., without a motive that calls Him, not without a self characterized by preference, and there is no impossibility in it; because when the agent is free, He prefers as He wills. And there is a discussion (bahth) concerning this: that the free agent, even if He prefers one of His two potentialities by His will, yet if His will for one of them is equal to His will for the other with respect to [the Mu'tazili's statement?]... And this attachment is either eternal, and then the complete agent with all its conditions is eternal, and the temporality of the effect does not necessarily follow; because His will attaches to its occurrence at a specific time, and the effect is not delayed from the complete cause, for the delay occurs only when the agent is free such that the effect could occur contrary to what He willed. So if He willed its occurrence at a specific time, if it occurred before that time or after it, that would be a delay, just as if He willed its occurrence in a specific manner and it occurred in another manner, that would be a delay. Or the attachment of His will is temporal, and then the complete agent is temporal, and the specification of the attachment to one time rather than another is by the essence of the will, for it is its nature to specify without a specifier, as the commentator (al-Sharif) may God sanctify his soul mentioned, or by another temporal attachment, and then an infinite regress in attachments follows, since they are mental considerations. His saying: ('And there is a discussion, etc.') Its summary: that preference without a preponderator is false, because it entails preponderance without a preponderator or an infinite regress. His saying: ('But if His will is equal, etc.') The mention of the will after what the respondent said about the attachment of His will to specification, etc., is to cut off the basis of the answer by showing that preference without a preponderator is impossible, whether the preponderator is the will or the attachment of the will. His saying: ('Equal to His will for the other, etc.') Otherwise necessitation would follow and the inability to do the other alternative. His saying: ('And there is a discussion: that the free agent, etc.') Some virtuous scholars said there is a discussion in the discussion (bahth), because it is said: It is in the nature of the free agent that His will attaches to one of the two potentialities even if it is equal in its attachment to both, and He does not need in the attachment of His equal will to one of them another will, for that would entail an infinite regress. Its verification: that the relation of the will to the two opposites, even if it is equal, yet the powerful agent gives preference to one of the two equals over the other without a motive; so what is entailed is preference (tarjih) without a preponderator, not preponderance (tarajjuh) without an agent, such that the door to proving the Maker would be closed. And if it is said: If the attachment of the will is an effect of the essence of the one who wills, then His influence on it is either by necessitation, and then necessitation with respect to the act would also follow, as is not hidden; or it is by will, and then an infinite regress would follow. We said: The infinite regress would follow only if it needed
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents a classic kalam objection (shubha) against the view that temporal events require an agent, and the Ash'ari response based on divine free choice. The objection is a dilemma: either the agent for temporal events is eternal or temporal. If eternal, then all events should occur simultaneously or without specific times since the eternal agent is equally related to all times, leading to events occurring without a specifier (mukhasis). If temporal, then that agent itself needs an agent, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The response states that the agent is eternal and free (mukhtar), and His act follows His will. The will's attachment to specific times does not require a motive (da'i) because God can choose one of two equal alternatives without a cause. This is presented as perfection. The author distinguishes between two types of 'preference without a preponderator': (1) the agent's act of preferring (tarjih) without a motive—this is possible for a free agent; (2) the event's occurrence (tarajjuh) without a preponderating cause—this is impossible. Then a discussion (bahth) follows: even if the free agent prefers by His will, if His will is equally related to both alternatives, then either the attachment (ta'allaq) of the will is eternal or temporal. If eternal, the complete cause is eternal and the effect's temporality does not contradict because the will specified a particular time. If temporal, then the complete cause is temporal, and the specification of the attachment to a specific time is either by the essence of the will (which inherently specifies without a specifier) or by another temporal attachment, leading to an infinite regress in attachments (which is allowed since they are mental considerations). The commentary then glosses these points, emphasizing that preference without a preponderator is rejected because it entails preponderance without a preponderator or regress. The discussion continues with a rebuttal: the free agent's will can attach to one alternative despite being equal, without needing a further will, otherwise infinite regress. The final part (incomplete) seems to address the objection that if the attachment is an effect of the essence, it either leads to necessitation or regress, and the response is that regress only follows if it needed something (text cuts off).

Key Terms
شبهة (doubt)
قديم (eternal)
حادث (temporal)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
سبب (cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the dilemma proves that temporal events do not need any cause at all.
Correction: The dilemma only shows that the standard conception of causality leads to problems; it does not deny causality but calls for a revised understanding, which the Ash'ari response provides.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the agent of temporal events is eternal, according to the sixth doubt?
A) Events would occur without a specifier for their times.
B) Events would occur at all times simultaneously.
C) The agent would need a specifier itself.
D) Infinite regress would follow.

Key Takeaway
The sixth doubt presents a dilemma: an eternal cause cannot specify times, and a temporal cause leads to infinite regress, challenging the need for a cause for temporal events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the specific timing of events in your life—why they happened when they did—do you truly believe that an eternal God has specified them, or do you subconsciously attribute them to chance? How does this dilemma challenge you to deepen your reliance on Allah's will as the only specifier? What is one situation this week where you can consciously affirm that the timing is from Him?
?
The dilemma shows that an eternal cause without a will to specify cannot explain why events occur now rather than later. In your daily decisions, do you act as if your choices are merely random occurrences, or do you recognize that every moment is specified by the Creator? How can you cultivate a constant awareness of this?
?
If you were to defend the belief that all events have a cause, how would you answer the dilemma presented here? Does your faith in Allah as the Eternal Cause move you to seek a deeper understanding of His attribute of will, or do you avoid such questions? What is one concrete step you can take this week to study the divine attributes more seriously?

The Response: Divine Free Choice and the Distinction of Tarjih and Tarajjuh
Al-Isfahani responds to the sixth doubt by affirming God as eternal free agent and distinguishing the agent's preference from the event's preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to the sixth doubt: God as eternal free agent who prefers without a motive, and the critical distinction between the agent's act of preference (tarjih) and the event's preponderance (tarajjuh).

Core Idea
God freely chooses one equal possibility over another without a motive—this is tarjih, not tarajjuh.

Al-Isfahani now presents the Ash'ari response to the sixth doubt. The key move is to affirm an eternal free agent (mukhtar) whose act follows His will. God can specify a time for an event without an external motive. The response also introduces a crucial distinction between the agent's act of preferring (tarjih) and the event's becoming preponderant (tarajjuh). This distinction is fundamental to Ash'ari occasionalism.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic and translation of Al-Isfahani's response. Notice the assertion that God is eternal and free, and His act follows His will.
2
Identify the distinction
Find where the author distinguishes between tarjih and tarajjuh. Understand why one is possible and the other impossible.
Tarjih is the agent's act; tarajjuh is the effect's occurrence. The first can be without a motive; the second requires a cause.
3
Reflect on the concept of free choice
How does this response solve the dilemma? The eternal agent can specify times by His will, without needing a specifier. The will itself is the specifier.
This is a core Ash'ari doctrine: God's will is not constrained by external motives.

بلا سبب مخصص لتلك الأوقات بالحدوث من الأوقات السابقة عليها مع كونها متساوية في أن ذلك المؤثر القديم موجود فيها. وإما أن يقال ذلك المؤثر حادث، فيكون محتاجاً إلى مؤثر آخر حادث أيضاً، فيتسلسل وهو محال. فإن ذلك هو الكمال. والترجيح الصادر من الفاعل لأحد مقدوريه على الآخر لا لداع يدعوه إلى اختيار ذلك المقدور غير الوقوع أي وقوع أحد المتساويين بلا سبب مؤثر، والثاني هو المحال، لأنه ترجح أحد المتساويين من طرفي الممكن بلا سبب مرجح من خارج وقد عرفت بطلانه بالضرورة. وأما الأول فليس بمحال لأنه ترجيح من غير مرجح أي من غير داع يدعوه لا من غير ذات متصف بالترجيح ولا استحالة فيه؛ لأن المؤثر إذا كان مختاراً، فهو يرجح كيف يشاء. وفيه بحث وهو أن المختار وإن رجح أحد مقدوريه بإرادته، لكن إذا كانت إرادته لأحدهما مساوية لإرادته للآخر بالنظر إلى ذاته، وهذا التعلق إما أزلي فيكون المؤثر التام بجميع شرائطه قديماً، ولا يلزم قدم الحادث؛ لأنه تعلق إرادته بوقوعه في وقت مخصوص، ولا تخلف المعلول عن العلة التامة فإن التخلف فيما إذا كان المؤثر مختاراً أن يقع على خلاف ما أراده، فإذا أراد وقوعه في وقت مخصوص، فلو وقع قبل ذلك الوقت أو بعده كان تخلفاً كما إذا أراد وقوعه على كيفية مخصوصة فلو وقع على كيفية أخرى كان تخلفاً. أو تعلق إرادته متجدد فيكون المؤثر التام حادثاً، وتخصص التعلق بوقت دون آخر بذات الإرادة فإن شأنها التخصيص بلا مخصص ذكره الشارح قدس سره، أو بتعلق آخر متجدد ويلزم التسلسل في التعلقات، لكونها أموراً اعتبارية. قوله: (وفيه بحث إلخ) حاصله: أن الترجيح بلا مرجح باطل؛ لأنه يستلزم الترجح بلا مرجح أو التسلسل. قوله: (لكن إذا كانت إرادته إلخ) التعرض للإرادة بعد ما قال المجيب وتعلق إرادته بتخصيص إلخ لحسم مادة الجواب ببيان أنه لا يمكن الترجيح بلا مرجح، سواء كان المرجح الإرادة أو تعلق الإرادة. قوله: ( مساوية لإرادته إلخ) وإلا لزم الإيجاب وعدم القدرة على الطرف الآخر. قوله: ( وفيه بحث وهو أن المختار إلخ) قال بعض الفضلاء في البحث بحث؛ لأنه يقال: إن من شأن المختار أن تتعلق إرادته بأحد المقدورين وإن كانت مساوية في تعلقها بهما، ولا يحتاج في تعلق إرادته المساوية بأحدهما إلى إرادة أخرى فيلزم التسلسل. وتحقيقه أن نسبة الإرادة إلى الضدين وإن كانت على السوية، إلا أن القادر يرجح أحد المتساويين على الآخر بلا داع فاللازم هو الترجيح بلا مرجح لا الترجح بلا مؤثر حتى يلزم انسداد باب إثبات الصانع. فإن قيل: تعلق الإرادة إن كان أثراً لذات المريد فتأثيره فيه إما بالإيجاب فيلزم الإيجاب بالنظر إلى الفعل أيضاً، كما لا يخفى وإن كان بالإرادة يلزم التسلسل. قلنا: إنما يلزم التسلسل لو احتاج
The sixth doubt: If possibility or temporal origination necessitates an agent, then the temporal events we observe must have an agent, either due to their origination or due to their possibility. Then either that agent is said to be eternal, and it would necessitate their occurrence—i.e., the occurrence of those temporal events at their specific times—without a cause that specifies those times for occurrence from among the times preceding them, while they are equal in that that eternal agent exists in them. Or that agent is said to be temporal, and then it would need another temporal agent, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. We said: The agent for temporal events is eternal, free (mukhtar) according to us, and His act follows His will. And the attachment (ta'allaq) of His will to specifying occurrence at some times, despite their equality, does not need a motive (da'i); rather, He may choose one of His two equal potentialities over the other without a cause that calls Him to it, for that is perfection. And the preference (tarjih) issuing from the agent for one of His two potentialities over the other without a motive that calls Him to choose that potentiality is nothing but the occurrence—i.e., one of the two equals occurring without a causative cause—and the second [i.e., the latter] is impossible, because it is the preponderance (tarajjuh) of one of the two equals from the two sides of the possible without a preponderating cause from outside, and you have already known its impossibility necessarily. As for the first, it is not impossible, because it is a preference (tarjih) without a preponderator (murajjih), i.e., without a motive that calls Him, not without a self characterized by preference, and there is no impossibility in it; because when the agent is free, He prefers as He wills. And there is a discussion (bahth) concerning this: that the free agent, even if He prefers one of His two potentialities by His will, yet if His will for one of them is equal to His will for the other with respect to [the Mu'tazili's statement?]... And this attachment is either eternal, and then the complete agent with all its conditions is eternal, and the temporality of the effect does not necessarily follow; because His will attaches to its occurrence at a specific time, and the effect is not delayed from the complete cause, for the delay occurs only when the agent is free such that the effect could occur contrary to what He willed. So if He willed its occurrence at a specific time, if it occurred before that time or after it, that would be a delay, just as if He willed its occurrence in a specific manner and it occurred in another manner, that would be a delay. Or the attachment of His will is temporal, and then the complete agent is temporal, and the specification of the attachment to one time rather than another is by the essence of the will, for it is its nature to specify without a specifier, as the commentator (al-Sharif) may God sanctify his soul mentioned, or by another temporal attachment, and then an infinite regress in attachments follows, since they are mental considerations. His saying: ('And there is a discussion, etc.') Its summary: that preference without a preponderator is false, because it entails preponderance without a preponderator or an infinite regress. His saying: ('But if His will is equal, etc.') The mention of the will after what the respondent said about the attachment of His will to specification, etc., is to cut off the basis of the answer by showing that preference without a preponderator is impossible, whether the preponderator is the will or the attachment of the will. His saying: ('Equal to His will for the other, etc.') Otherwise necessitation would follow and the inability to do the other alternative. His saying: ('And there is a discussion: that the free agent, etc.') Some virtuous scholars said there is a discussion in the discussion (bahth), because it is said: It is in the nature of the free agent that His will attaches to one of the two potentialities even if it is equal in its attachment to both, and He does not need in the attachment of His equal will to one of them another will, for that would entail an infinite regress. Its verification: that the relation of the will to the two opposites, even if it is equal, yet the powerful agent gives preference to one of the two equals over the other without a motive; so what is entailed is preference (tarjih) without a preponderator, not preponderance (tarajjuh) without an agent, such that the door to proving the Maker would be closed. And if it is said: If the attachment of the will is an effect of the essence of the one who wills, then His influence on it is either by necessitation, and then necessitation with respect to the act would also follow, as is not hidden; or it is by will, and then an infinite regress would follow. We said: The infinite regress would follow only if it needed
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents a classic kalam objection (shubha) against the view that temporal events require an agent, and the Ash'ari response based on divine free choice. The objection is a dilemma: either the agent for temporal events is eternal or temporal. If eternal, then all events should occur simultaneously or without specific times since the eternal agent is equally related to all times, leading to events occurring without a specifier (mukhasis). If temporal, then that agent itself needs an agent, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The response states that the agent is eternal and free (mukhtar), and His act follows His will. The will's attachment to specific times does not require a motive (da'i) because God can choose one of two equal alternatives without a cause. This is presented as perfection. The author distinguishes between two types of 'preference without a preponderator': (1) the agent's act of preferring (tarjih) without a motive—this is possible for a free agent; (2) the event's occurrence (tarajjuh) without a preponderating cause—this is impossible. Then a discussion (bahth) follows: even if the free agent prefers by His will, if His will is equally related to both alternatives, then either the attachment (ta'allaq) of the will is eternal or temporal. If eternal, the complete cause is eternal and the effect's temporality does not contradict because the will specified a particular time. If temporal, then the complete cause is temporal, and the specification of the attachment to a specific time is either by the essence of the will (which inherently specifies without a specifier) or by another temporal attachment, leading to an infinite regress in attachments (which is allowed since they are mental considerations). The commentary then glosses these points, emphasizing that preference without a preponderator is rejected because it entails preponderance without a preponderator or regress. The discussion continues with a rebuttal: the free agent's will can attach to one alternative despite being equal, without needing a further will, otherwise infinite regress. The final part (incomplete) seems to address the objection that if the attachment is an effect of the essence, it either leads to necessitation or regress, and the response is that regress only follows if it needed something (text cuts off).

Key Terms
مختار (free agent)
إرادة (will)
ترجيح (preference)
ترجح (preponderance)
مرجح (preponderator)
داع (motive)
مقدور (potentiality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'preference without a preponderator' is the same as 'preponderance without a cause', which is absurd.
Correction: Al-Isfahani explicitly distinguishes them: tarjih is the agent's act, tarajjuh is the event's occurrence. The former is possible for a free agent; the latter is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why is preference (tarjih) without a motive possible for God?
A) Because God is eternal and His will is unconstrained.
B) Because God is free and can choose without external cause.
C) Because temporal events do not require a cause.
D) Because the will itself is a preponderator.

Key Takeaway
God's free choice (tarjih) does not require a motive; it is the very act of specification that makes an event happen, not a preponderance without a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face two equally valid choices in life, do you trust that God's free choice in making you choose one (without you having a strong motive) is part of His perfection, or do you feel anxious without a clear reason? How can you cultivate contentment with His tarjih in your life?
?
In your understanding of God's actions, do you subconsciously require a 'cause' for why He does things? How does this lesson's distinction between tarjih and tarajjuh challenge your tendency to demand a reason from God?
?
The passage says God can choose between equals without a motive—this is perfection. Have you ever felt that God's decree was 'arbitrary' or 'unfair' because you saw no reason for it? How will you now respond to that feeling given this teaching?

Causality and Created Things
