The Discussion: Attachments of the Will and the Objection of Regress
Al-Isfahani engages in a discussion (bahth) about the nature of the will's attachment and objections to the free choice response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deeper discussion about the attachment of God's will to specific events, including the alternatives of eternal or temporal attachment and the objection that preference without a preponderator still leads to problems.

Core Idea
The will's attachment (ta'allaq) cannot be understood without addressing whether it is eternal or temporal, each with its own implications.

After the initial response, a discussion (bahth) arises. The objector argues that even if God is free, His will for one of two equals must be based on something. Al-Isfahani explores two possibilities: the will's attachment is either eternal or temporal. Each leads to further questions about specification and regress. This lesson delves into that sophisticated theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the discussion passage
Read the Arabic and translation from 'وفيه بحث' onward. Notice the objection that the free agent's will, if equal, still needs a reason.
2
Identify the two alternatives for attachment
The author considers whether the will's attachment is eternal or temporal. What are the consequences of each?
Eternal attachment: complete cause is eternal, but effect is temporal. Temporal attachment: leads to regress unless the will itself specifies without a specifier.
3
Understand the response to the objection
The author responds that preference without a preponderator is not the same as preponderance without a cause. The free agent's will itself is the specifier.
The text is incomplete, but the key point is that infinite regress in attachments is not problematic because they are mental considerations (i'tibari).
4
Reflect on the incomplete ending
Note that the text cuts off mid-sentence. The final point is about regress only following if it needed something.
This shows the argument is ongoing in the original work.

الشبهة السادسة: لو كان الإمكان أو الحدوث محوجاً إلى المؤثر، كان للحوادث التي نشاهدها مؤثر إما لحدوثها وإما لإمكانها. فإما أن يقال ذلك المؤثر قديم فيلزم حدوثها أي حدوث تلك الحوادث في أوقاتها المخصوصة الوقت أو بعده كان تخلفاً كما إذا أراد وقوعه على كيفية مخصوصة فلو وقع على كيفية أخرى كان تخلفاً. أو تعلق إرادته متجدد فيكون المؤثر التام حادثاً، وتخصص التعلق بوقت دون آخر بذات الإرادة فإن شأنها التخصيص The sixth doubt: If possibility or temporal origination necessitates an agent, then the temporal events we observe must have an agent, either due to their origination or due to their possibility. Then either that agent is said to be eternal, and it would necessitate their occurrence—i.e., the occurrence of those temporal events at their specific times—without a cause that specifies those times for occurrence from among the times preceding them, while they are equal in that that eternal agent exists in them. Or that agent is said to be temporal, and then it would need another temporal agent, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. We said: The agent for temporal events is eternal, free (mukhtar) according to us, and His act follows His will. And the attachment (ta'allaq) of His will to specifying occurrence at some times, despite their equality, does not need a motive (da'i); rather, He may choose one of His two equal potentialities over the other without a cause that calls Him to it, for that is perfection. And the preference (tarjih) issuing from the agent for one of His two potentialities over the other without a motive that calls Him to choose that potentiality is nothing but the occurrence—i.e., one of the two equals occurring without a causative cause—and the second [i.e., the latter] is impossible, because it is the preponderance (tarajjuh) of one of the two equals from the two sides of the possible without a preponderating cause from outside, and you have already known its impossibility necessarily. As for the first, it is not impossible, because it is a preference (tarjih) without a preponderator (murajjih), i.e., without a motive that calls Him, not without a self characterized by preference, and there is no impossibility in it; because when the agent is free, He prefers as He wills. So if He willed its occurrence at a specific time, if it occurred before that time or after it, that would be a delay, just as if He willed its occurrence in a specific manner and it occurred in another manner, that would be a delay. His saying: ('And there is a discussion, etc.') Its summary: that preference without a preponderator is false, because it entails preponderance without a preponderator or an infinite regress. His saying: ('But if His will is equal, etc.') The mention of the will after what the respondent said about the attachment of His will to specification, etc., is to cut off the basis of the answer by showing that preference without a preponderator is impossible, whether the preponderator is the will or the attachment of the will. His saying: ('Equal to His will for the other, etc.') Otherwise necessitation would follow and the inability to do the other alternative. And if it is said: If the attachment of the will is an effect of the essence of the one who wills, then His influence on it is either by necessitation, and then necessitation with respect to the act would also follow, as is not hidden; or it is by will, and then an infinite regress would follow. Commentary
This passage presents a classic kalam objection (shubha) against the view that temporal events require an agent, and the Ash'ari response based on divine free choice. The objection is a dilemma: either the agent for temporal events is eternal or temporal. If eternal, then all events should occur simultaneously or without specific times since the eternal agent is equally related to all times, leading to events occurring without a specifier (mukhasis). If temporal, then that agent itself needs an agent, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The response states that the agent is eternal and free (mukhtar), and His act follows His will. The will's attachment to specific times does not require a motive (da'i) because God can choose one of two equal alternatives without a cause. This is presented as perfection. The author distinguishes between two types of 'preference without a preponderator': (1) the agent's act of preferring (tarjih) without a motive—this is possible for a free agent; (2) the event's occurrence (tarajjuh) without a preponderating cause—this is impossible. Then a discussion (bahth) follows: even if the free agent prefers by His will, if His will is equally related to both alternatives, then either the attachment (ta'allaq) of the will is eternal or temporal. If eternal, the complete cause is eternal and the effect's temporality does not contradict because the will specified a particular time. If temporal, then the complete cause is temporal, and the specification of the attachment to a specific time is either by the essence of the will (which inherently specifies without a specifier) or by another temporal attachment, leading to an infinite regress in attachments (which is allowed since they are mental considerations). The commentary then glosses these points, emphasizing that preference without a preponderator is rejected because it entails preponderance without a preponderator or regress. The discussion continues with a rebuttal: the free agent's will can attach to one alternative despite being equal, without needing a further will, otherwise infinite regress. The final part (incomplete) seems to address the objection that if the attachment is an effect of the essence, it either leads to necessitation or regress, and the response is that regress only follows if it needed something (text cuts off).

Key Terms
تعلق (attachment)
اعتباري (mental consideration)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
إرادة (will)
داع (motive)
مخصص (specifier)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Ash'ari position leads to an infinite regress of wills or attachments that is impossible.
Correction: The Ash'ari response holds that if these attachments are mental considerations (i'tibariyyat), infinite regress is not ontologically problematic.

Checkpoint
In the discussion, what is one way to avoid infinite regress if the will's attachment is temporal?
A) By claiming the attachment is eternal.
B) By stating that the will's essence specifies without a specifier.
C) By denying that temporal events need a cause.
D) By accepting regress as impossible.

Key Takeaway
The discussion reveals sophisticated arguments about the nature of divine will, with the Ash'ari defense that the will itself is the specifier and infinite regress in mental considerations is not harmful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own will, do you ever assume it must have an external reason or motive? How does the Ash'ari assertion that God's will is self-sufficient and needs no specifier challenge your own tendencies to rationalize or second-guess God's decrees? Will you accept that His will is the ultimate explanation, even when you cannot see the reason?
?
The discussion highlights that attachments of the will are mental considerations, not real attributes. Have you ever treated your own mental projections about God (e.g., expectations of how He should act) as if they were real constraints on Him? How can you surrender those projections and let God be God, even when His will seems arbitrary to your mind?
?
If infinite regress in mental considerations is not a problem, then what does that say about your own need to trace every cause back to a beginning? Are you willing to rest in the fact that God's will is the first cause, beyond which no further explanation is needed? What would it look like for you to stop demanding a 'why' and simply trust that His will is good?

Equality of Will and Infinite Regress of Wills
Objection on equality of will leading to regress of wills or attachments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if the will for one object equals the will for another, it leads to either infinite regress of wills or an unjustified preference, and how a reply about multiple attachments also leads to regress.

Core Idea
Equal wills force a choice: infinite regress or uncaused preference.

Al-Sayyid al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī presents a subtle objection against the Ash‘arī account of divine will. If God’s will for two objects is equal, why does He choose one? The objection forces a dilemma: either an infinite regress of wills or a preference without cause. A proposed solution—that the will is one with multiple attachments—also leads to an infinite regress of attachments. This passage sharpens a classic Mu‘tazilī line of argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the dilemma: if the will for one action is equal to the will for another, why is one actualized?
2
Examine the first horn
If the preference is attributed to another will, that will in turn requires another, leading to infinite regress of wills.
3
Examine the second horn
If the preference is uncaused, then one equal is preferred without a cause, which violates the principle of sufficient reason.
4
Consider the reply and its consequence
A possible reply says the will is one but its attachments multiply. The objection retorts that this leads to infinite regress in attachments.
Note that this regress is of mental considerations, not real beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه بحث وهو أن المختار وإن رجح أحد مقدوريه بإرادته، لكن إذا كانت إرادته لأحدهما مساوية لإرادته للآخر بالنظر إلى ذاته توجه أن يقال : لم اتصف بإحدى الإرادتين دون الأخرى؟ فإن أسند هذه ترجیح الإرادة إلى إرادة أخرى نقلنا الكلام إليها، ولزم تسلسل الإرادات . وإن لم يسند إلى شيء فقد ترجح أحد المتساويين على الآخر بلا سبب . فإن قيل: الإرادة واحدة لكن يتعدد تعلقها بحسب المرادات :قلنا فيلزم حينئذ التسلسل في التعلقات . الشبهة السابعة : جملة الحوادث التي وجدت إلى الآن من حيث هي جملة لا شك أنها حادثة . وقد عرفت اندفاعه إما باختيار أن التعلق أزلي ولا تخلف أو باختيار أنه متجدد ومخصص وقوعه نفس الإرادة أو أنه واقع بلا سبب ولا يلزم من جواز وقوع الأمور الاعتبارية بلا سبب جواز وجود الممكن بلا سبب وهذا هو مختار صدر الشريعة في التوضيح، وهو في غاية المتانة. وقد حققناه في حواشينا على المقدمات الأربعة، أو بالتزام التسلسل في التعلقات لكونها اعتبارية، والقول بأن التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية النفس الأمرية أيضاً محال لجريان برهان التطبيق، فسيجيء الكلام فيه إن شاء الله تعالى . أخذت قوله : (جملة الحوادث إلخ) يعني إذا جميع الحوادث الموجودة إلى الآن التي بعضها مجتمعة وبعضها متعاقبة وسواء قلنا بتناهيها أو بعدم تناهيها من حيث إنها جملة بحيث لا يشذ منها واحد فلا شك في حدوثها وإمكانها لأن حدوث الجزء وإمكانه يستلزم حدوث الكل
And in it is a discussion: that the free agent, even if He prefers one of His two capabilities by His will, yet if His will for one of them is equal to His will for the other, in itself, the question arises: why is He described with one of the two wills rather than the other? For if He ascribes this preference of will to another will, we transfer the discussion to it, and an infinite regress of wills follows. And if He does not ascribe it to anything, then one of the two equals has become preponderant over the other without a cause. And if it is said: the will is one, but its attachments multiply according to the objects of will, we say: then an infinite regress in attachments follows. The seventh doubt: the totality of events that have occurred up to now, as a totality, is without doubt contingent. And you have known its rebuttal either by choosing that the attachment is eternal and there is no delay, or by choosing that it is renewed and its occurrence is specified by the will itself, or that it is actual without a cause, and the permissibility of the occurrence of mental considerations (al-umūr al-i‘tibārīyah) without a cause does not necessitate the permissibility of the existence of a possible being without a cause. And this is the choice of Ṣadr al-Sharī‘ah in al-Tawḍīḥ, and it is extremely solid. And we have verified it in our glosses on the Four Premises. Or by committing to infinite regress in attachments because they are mental considerations. And the saying that infinite regress in mental considerations in re (al-nafs al-amrīyah) is also impossible due to the running of the proof of application (burhān al-taṭbīq) – the discussion will come about it, God willing. He took his saying: (the totality of events etc.), meaning indeed all events existing up to now, some of them simultaneous and some successive, whether we say they are finite or infinite, as a totality such that none is excluded, there is no doubt in their occurrence and contingency, because the occurrence of the part and its contingency necessitates the occurrence of the whole.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion. It begins with an objection: if the free agent's will for one action is equal to His will for the other, then why is He described with one specific will rather than the other? If we attribute this preference to another will, we get an infinite regress of wills. If not, then one equal becomes preponderant without a cause, which is impossible. A possible reply that the will is one with multiple attachments is also shown to lead to infinite regress in attachments. The passage then moves to the seventh doubt: the totality of events up to now is contingent. The author acknowledges that the objection has been rebutted through several options: either the attachment of will is eternal and does not delay, or it is renewed and its occurrence is specified by the will itself, or it occurs without a cause — and the permissibility of mental considerations (like attachments) occurring without a cause does not imply the permissibility of a possible being existing without a cause. This is the position of Ṣadr al-Sharī‘ah. Another option is to accept infinite regress in attachments because they are mental. The author notes that the claim that infinite regress in mental considerations is also impossible due to the proof of application will be discussed later. Finally, the passage explains the seventh doubt: the totality of events, whether finite or infinite, is contingent because each part is contingent, and the contingency of the whole follows from the contingency of its parts.

Key Terms
الإرادة
التسلسل
التعلقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the objection only targets one aspect and can be easily dismissed by saying 'God just wills it'.
Correction: The objection shows that if God's will is equal for two objects, the specific actualization requires an explanation; without it, the principle of sufficient reason is violated.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what happens if the free agent attributes the preference of one will to another will?
A) The problem is solved.
B) An infinite regress of wills follows.
C) The will becomes one.
D) The preference is uncaused.

Key Takeaway
Equality of will forces a choice between infinite regress of wills or an uncaused preference, and the proposed solution of multiple attachments only shifts the regress to attachments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face two equally good options, do you recognize that your own will requires a reason to prefer one? How does this awareness humble you before God, whose will is perfect and needs no external cause?
?
Think of a time you justified a decision by appealing to 'gut feeling' or 'no reason'. Does this passage challenge you to examine whether you are relying on uncaused preferences? How can you align your choices with God's wisdom rather than arbitrary whims?
?
If God's will is not arbitrary but rooted in His wisdom, how does that change your trust in His decrees? Are there areas of your life where you doubt God's choice because you cannot see the reason? What would it take for you to surrender that doubt today?

The Seventh Doubt and Its Rebuttals
Seventh doubt and four rebuttals including mental considerations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh doubt about the contingency of the totality of events and the multiple rebuttals, including the view of Ṣadr al-Sharī‘ah and the issue of infinite regress in mental considerations.

Core Idea
The totality of events is contingent, but its contingency is rebutted via eternal attachment, renewed will, uncaused mental considerations, or acceptance of regress.

The seventh doubt builds on the previous discussion: the entire series of events up to now is undoubtedly contingent. This poses a problem for those who deny a beginning. The author lists several rebuttals: the eternal attachment of will, the renewed specification by will, the view that mental considerations can occur without a cause (attributed to Ṣadr al-Sharī‘ah), and even acceptance of infinite regress in attachments because they are mental. The passage also notes that the proof of application (burhān al-taṭbīq) will later address the impossibility of such regress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seventh doubt
Read the opening: the totality of events up to now is without doubt contingent. Understand why this is problematic.
The doubt is that if the series is infinite, its contingency is still asserted from the parts.
2
Learn the first rebuttal
The attachment of the will can be eternal with no delay—so no regress in real beings.
3
Learn the second rebuttal
The attachment is renewed and its occurrence is specified by the will itself—no external cause needed.
4
Learn the third rebuttal (Ṣadr al-Sharī‘ah)
The attachment occurs without a cause; mental considerations do not require a cause. This does not imply possible beings can exist without a cause.
The author praises this as extremely solid.
5
Examine the fourth option
Accept infinite regress in attachments because they are mental (i‘tibārī), not real. The proof of application will later be discussed.

The Dilemma of the Cause of All Contingent Events
First half of the passage, presenting two horns of a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the entire set of contingent events cannot have either a temporal or an eternal cause without leading to a contradiction or infinite regress.

Core Idea
If the set of all events had a cause, it would be both inside and outside—impossible.

Al-Sanusi presents a piercing dilemma: if the whole collection of contingent events required a cause, that cause would have to be either temporal or eternal. Both options lead to absurdities. This lesson walks through both horns to show why the initial assumption must be rejected—paving the way for the solution that the causing attribute is merely mental.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial assumption
Read the opening statement: if temporal origination or possibility were what necessitates an effecter, then the whole set would have a cause. Grasp why this is proposed.
2
Analyze the temporal cause horn
If the cause were temporal, it would be one of the events (inside the set) yet must be outside its effect—a contradiction.
Notice the logical structure: inside and outside simultaneously.
3
Analyze the eternal cause horn
If the cause were eternal, its effecting must be either eternal or temporal. Both lead to absurdities: if eternal, events would be eternal; if temporal, a new attribute arises requiring another cause, leading to infinite regress.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وممكنة، فلو كان الحدوث أو الإمكان محوجاً إلى المؤثر لكان لتلك الجملة علة، لكن ( لا) علة لها وإلا فإما حادثة فتكون تلك العلة ( داخلة في الجملة) الشاملة لجميع الحوادث بحيث لا يشذ عنها شيء منها، (وهي) أي تلك العلة (خارجة عنها )؛ لأن المؤثر في الجملة لا بد أن يكون خارجاً عن الأثر فتكون داخلة وخارجة معا، وهذا خلف . ( وإما قديمة فصدورها لا لمؤثر إذ لا يجوز أن يؤثر ذلك القديم فيها، لأن تأثيره فيها إن كان قديماً لزم قدم الحوادث، إذ لا يعقل تأثير حقيقي بلا حصول أثر ، وإن كان حادثاً لزم أن يتصف القديم بصفة متجددة هي المؤثرية، فتكون محتاجة إلى مؤثرية أخرى فننقل الكلام إليها فيلزم التسلسل . ( والجواب : أنها ) أي المؤثرية صفة ( ذهنية) ( فنختار أن المؤثر في جملة الحوادث ،قديم، وأن له تأثيراً متجددا، لكنه صفة ذهنية اعتبارية يتصف بها القديم من غير حاجة إلى تأثير آخر، فلا يتسلسل
And possible, so if temporal origination (al-ḥudūth) or possibility (al-imkān) were the necessitate of the effect (al-muʼaththir), then that whole (al-jumla) would have a cause, but it has no cause; otherwise if it were temporal, that cause would be included in the whole encompassing all temporal events such that no event escapes it, and it (i.e., that cause) would be outside it, because the effecter in the whole must be external to the effect, so it would be both inside and outside, which is a contradiction. Or if it were eternal, its emanation would be without an effecter, since it is not permissible for that eternal to effect in it, because if its effecting in it were eternal, then the temporal events would be eternal, since no real effect is conceivable without the occurrence of an effect, and if it were temporal, then the eternal would be described by a renewed attribute which is effecting (al-muʼaththiriyya), so it would need another effecting, and we transfer the discussion to it, leading to infinite regress. The answer: that (i.e., effecting) is a mental attribute (ṣifa dhihniyya), so we choose that the effecter in the whole of temporal events is eternal, and that it has a renewed effecting, but it is a mental, relational attribute by which the eternal is described without needing another effect, so no regress occurs.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents a theo-philosophical argument about the cause of the entire set of contingent events. It begins by positing: if temporal origination (ḥudūth) or possibility (imkān) were what necessitates an effecter, then the whole set of contingent events would itself have a cause. The argument then examines two possibilities for that cause: either it is itself temporal or it is eternal. If it were temporal, it would have to be both inside the set (since it is one of the events) and outside it (since the cause must be external to its effect), leading to a contradiction. If it were eternal, two sub-possibilities arise for its effecting: if the effecting were eternal, then the effects (temporal events) would be eternal (contradicting their temporality); if the effecting were temporal, the eternal would acquire a new attribute of 'effecting', which itself would require another cause, leading to infinite regress. The response (al-jawāb) is that the attribute of 'effecting' (al-muʼaththiriyya) is merely a mental consideration (ṣifa dhihniyya), not a real attribute requiring a separate cause. Thus, we maintain that the effecter of all events is eternal and has a renewed effecting, but this effecting is a mental relation that does not generate a regress. The argument follows a reductio ad absurdum format, systematically eliminating alternatives.

Key Terms
الحدوث
الإمكان
المؤثر
الجملة
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the entire set of contingent events must have a cause just like individual events do.
Correction: The passage shows that applying the same reasoning to the whole leads to contradictions, so the set as a whole does not require a cause in the same sense.

Checkpoint
What contradiction arises if the cause of the whole set is temporal?
A) It would cause infinite regress.
B) It would be both inside and outside the set.
C) It would be eternal and temporal simultaneously.
D) It would have no effect.

Key Takeaway
The entire set of contingent events cannot have a temporal or eternal cause without absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly trust that the very existence of this present moment, and every moment before it, hangs on nothing less than the will of the Self-Subsisting Lord? Or do you secretly rely on a chain of causes that you can predict and control? This passage strips away every chain. What cause do you run to in your heart when your world shakes? Will you let it go and depend only on the One who needs no cause?
?
The argument shows that the whole set of events cannot have a cause that is itself temporal or eternal in the wrong sense. Does your mind still try to pin God down within your mental categories, expecting Him to fit into your logic box? Are you willing to let your intellect fail in humility before the Reality that cannot be contained by causality?
?
If the entire universe’s contingency proves it cannot be self-causing, then its dependence is absolute. When you look at your own life, do you live as if you are contingent every second? Or do you act as if you have an independent streak that can manage without Him? This lesson calls you to radical dependence. Will you answer that call today?

The Solution: The Causing Attribute is Mental
Second half of the passage, providing the answer that effecting is mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attribute of effecting (al-muʼaththiriyya) is a mental consideration, not a real attribute, thus avoiding infinite regress.

Core Idea
Effecting is a mental relation, not a real attribute needing another cause.

After the dilemma shows the impossibility of a real cause for the whole set, al-Sanusi offers the solution: the attribute of effecting in the eternal is merely mental. This allows the eternal to have a renewed effect without requiring another cause, stopping the regress. This lesson unpacks this crucial answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer carefully
Read the passage from 'The answer' to the end. Note the key term 'mental attribute' (ṣifa dhihniyya).
2
Understand the concept of mental attribute
The attribute of effecting is not a real, separate quality but a relational consideration in the mind. It does not require a real cause.
This solves the regress problem.
3
Apply to the dilemma
Realize that the eternal can have a renewed effect without needing a new attribute real cause—it's just a mental relation.
Thus, we choose the eternal cause option with a mental effecting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وممكنة، فلو كان الحدوث أو الإمكان محوجاً إلى المؤثر لكان لتلك الجملة علة، لكن ( لا) علة لها وإلا فإما حادثة فتكون تلك العلة ( داخلة في الجملة) الشاملة لجميع الحوادث بحيث لا يشذ عنها شيء منها، (وهي) أي تلك العلة (خارجة عنها )؛ لأن المؤثر في الجملة لا بد أن يكون خارجاً عن الأثر فتكون داخلة وخارجة معا، وهذا خلف . ( وإما قديمة فصدورها لا لمؤثر إذ لا يجوز أن يؤثر ذلك القديم فيها، لأن تأثيره فيها إن كان قديماً لزم قدم الحوادث، إذ لا يعقل تأثير حقيقي بلا حصول أثر ، وإن كان حادثاً لزم أن يتصف القديم بصفة متجددة هي المؤثرية، فتكون محتاجة إلى مؤثرية أخرى فننقل الكلام إليها فيلزم التسلسل . ( والجواب : أنها ) أي المؤثرية صفة ( ذهنية) ( فنختار أن المؤثر في جملة الحوادث ،قديم، وأن له تأثيراً متجددا، لكنه صفة ذهنية اعتبارية يتصف بها القديم من غير حاجة إلى تأثير آخر، فلا يتسلسل
And possible, so if temporal origination (al-ḥudūth) or possibility (al-imkān) were the necessitate of the effect (al-muʼaththir), then that whole (al-jumla) would have a cause, but it has no cause; otherwise if it were temporal, that cause would be included in the whole encompassing all temporal events such that no event escapes it, and it (i.e., that cause) would be outside it, because the effecter in the whole must be external to the effect, so it would be both inside and outside, which is a contradiction. Or if it were eternal, its emanation would be without an effecter, since it is not permissible for that eternal to effect in it, because if its effecting in it were eternal, then the temporal events would be eternal, since no real effect is conceivable without the occurrence of an effect, and if it were temporal, then the eternal would be described by a renewed attribute which is effecting (al-muʼaththiriyya), so it would need another effecting, and we transfer the discussion to it, leading to infinite regress. The answer: that (i.e., effecting) is a mental attribute (ṣifa dhihniyya), so we choose that the effecter in the whole of temporal events is eternal, and that it has a renewed effecting, but it is a mental, relational attribute by which the eternal is described without needing another effect, so no regress occurs.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents a theo-philosophical argument about the cause of the entire set of contingent events. It begins by positing: if temporal origination (ḥudūth) or possibility (imkān) were what necessitates an effecter, then the whole set of contingent events would itself have a cause. The argument then examines two possibilities for that cause: either it is itself temporal or it is eternal. If it were temporal, it would have to be both inside the set (since it is one of the events) and outside it (since the cause must be external to its effect), leading to a contradiction. If it were eternal, two sub-possibilities arise for its effecting: if the effecting were eternal, then the effects (temporal events) would be eternal (contradicting their temporality); if the effecting were temporal, the eternal would acquire a new attribute of 'effecting', which itself would require another cause, leading to infinite regress. The response (al-jawāb) is that the attribute of 'effecting' (al-muʼaththiriyya) is merely a mental consideration (ṣifa dhihniyya), not a real attribute requiring a separate cause. Thus, we maintain that the effecter of all events is eternal and has a renewed effecting, but this effecting is a mental relation that does not generate a regress. The argument follows a reductio ad absurdum format, systematically eliminating alternatives.

Key Terms
المؤثرية
صفة ذهنية
اعتبارية
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the eternal has a new attribute of effecting, that attribute itself must be caused, leading to regress.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the attribute is mental, not real, so it does not require a cause and the regress stops.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what type of attribute is effecting (al-muʼaththiriyya)?
A) A real attribute requiring another cause.
B) A mental, relational attribute.
C) An eternal attribute.
D) A temporal attribute.

Key Takeaway
The causing attribute is mental, allowing the eternal to effect without causing regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate God’s creative power renewing each moment, do you unconsciously imagine a new attribute being added to Him? How does knowing that His effecting is a mental relation change that image—and what will you do the next time that feeling arises?
?
If God’s causing is not a real attribute but a mental consideration, does that make His creation less real for you? How does that challenge your desire to 'see' God’s action as something tangible, and what does it say about your need for control?
?
You have just learned that infinite regress is avoided by making the causing attribute mental. Is there a chain of excuses or dependencies in your own life that you treat as 'real' and unbreakable? Which link will you cut today by recognizing it as merely a mental relation that does not bind you?

Objection on Mental Attribute Needing a Specifier
Objection and response about the need for a specifier for mental attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that even a mental attribute requires a specifying determinant, and why the appeal to divine will is considered problematic.

Core Idea
Every temporal description needs a specifier, and will is not a sufficient answer.

The interlocutor raises a sharp objection: if being described by a temporal attribute is itself a temporal matter, then it must have a cause that specifies it. The standard reply is that divine will suffices, but this response is shown to be insufficient by reference to earlier difficulties. This lesson unpacks the objection and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the claim: even a non-existential attribute requires a specifying determinant.
2
Examine the proposed answer
Note that the text dismisses the appeal to will by referring to an earlier problem.
3
Reflect on the difficulty
Consider why the will itself might be problematic as a specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : الاتصاف بحادث وإن كان عدمياً محتاج إلى مرجح مخصص، فإن قيل : الإرادة كافية في ذلك، قلنا قد مر آنفاً وجه الإشكال فيها . الشبهة
And someone may say: being described by a temporal thing, even if it is non-existential (ʿadamī), needs a specifying determinant (murajjiḥ mukhaṣṣiṣ). Then if it is said: the will (al-irāda) is sufficient for that, we say: the aspect of difficulty (wajh al-ishkāl) in it has passed earlier. The doubt (al-shubha).
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-response unit. The objector raises a point: the eternal being's being described by a temporal attribute (even if that description is a mental or relational matter, as previously argued) is itself a temporal 'happening' that requires a specifying determinant (murajjiḥ mukhaṣṣiṣ) to select it over its opposite. The suggested counter is that the divine will (al-irāda) suffices as that determinant. However, the text's response dismisses this by saying 'we have already discussed the problem with that'—referring to an earlier discussion (possibly about the nature of divine will and its own need for a cause). The word 'al-shubha' (the doubt) may be a heading indicating this is a problematic point. The passage is terse, setting up a problem that is not fully resolved here, but points to prior reasoning.

الشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection is fully answered by pointing to divine will.
Correction: The text indicates that the will itself is problematic, so the objection is not resolved.

Checkpoint

A) It is impossible for the eternal to be described by a temporal attribute.
B) Being described by a temporal attribute needs a specifying determinant even if the attribute is non-existential.
C) The will is not a sufficient specifier.
D) The attribute is actually real, not mental.

Key Takeaway
The objection that even a mental attribute needs a specifier is not resolved by appealing to will, because the will's own causal status is questionable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objector points out that even mental attributes need a 'specifier'—what mental attributes or assumptions do you hold that might themselves need deeper examination, and are you willing to question them?
?
If the will of God is not a sufficient answer to the problem of causality, then what does that mean for how you understand your own will and decisions? Are you prepared to explore this unsettling thought, or do you prefer to leave it unresolved?

The Claim of Necessity in Human Power and the Fleeing Person
Refutation of the claim that we know necessarily that human power causes actions and that choice without a reason occurs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī response to the necessitarian claim that human power causes actions and that choice without a reason occurs.

Core Idea
Choosing without a motive is possible for a free agent—only an external cause is impossible.

The opponents of Ashʿarī causality argue that we know by necessity that our power causes our actions, and they cite examples like the fleeing person to prove that choice can occur without a determinant. However, the Ashʿarī response distinguishes between internal and external causes, denying only the latter. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the structure: a claim presented as 'the eighth', followed by an answer.
The claim is presented as a known necessity; the answer references a previously discussed point.
2
Identify the two sub-claims
The claimant asserts two things known necessarily: (1) that human power causes actions in accordance with will, and (2) that the fleeing person chooses without a determinant. Find these in the text.
The second sub-claim also includes the thirsty person example.
3
Understand the Ashʿarī answer
The answer states that preference (tarjīḥ) by a free agent without a motive (dāʿī) is not impossible—only preference without an external cause is impossible. Grasp this distinction.
The text also hints that this answer has its own problems, indicating ongoing debate.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the Ashʿarī position matters: it preserves divine omnipotence and human responsibility without necessitating external determinants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامنة: دعوى الضرورة في قدرة العبد وفي قضية الهارب من السبع أي نعلم بالضرورة أن قدرة العبد مؤثرة على وفق إرادته، وأن أفعاله صادرة عنه بمجرد اختياره ونعلم بالضرورة أيضاً أن الهارب من السبع إذا عن له طريقان متساويان فإنه يختار أحدهما بلا مرجح؛ لأنه مع شدة احتياجه إلى الفرار يستحيل منه أن يقف ويتفكر في رجحان أحدهما على الآخر، وكذا الحال في العطشان إذا أحضر عنده قدحان من الماء متساويان، فقد وجد ممكن حادث بلا سبب . ( والجواب : ما قد عرفت ) من مثل ذلك ترجيح من فاعل مختار بلا داع وليس بمستحيل إنما المحال ترجح أحد طرفي الممكن بلا سبب مرجح من خارج، وقد عرفت أيضاً ما في هذا الجواب
The eighth: The claim of necessity (al-ḍarūra) in the power of the servant (qudra al-ʿabd) and in the case of the one fleeing from a beast (al-hārib min al-sabuʿ). That is, we know necessarily that the power of the servant is effective in accordance with his will, and that his actions proceed from him by mere choice (mujarrad ikhtiyārih). And we also know necessarily that the one fleeing from a beast, if two equal roads appear to him, he chooses one of them without a determinant (lā murajjiḥ), because with his intense need to escape, it is impossible for him to stand and reflect on the preference of one over the other. Likewise the case of the thirsty person when two equal cups of water are brought before him, for a possible thing has occurred without a cause. And the answer: what you have already known, such as the preference (tarjīḥ) from a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) without a motive (lā dāʿī), and it is not impossible; only impossible is the preference of one of the two sides of a possible without an external specifying cause. And you have also known what is in this answer.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents a claim ('the eighth') and its refutation. The claimant asserts two things known by necessity (ḍarūra): first, that human power causes actions in accordance with will, so actions come from mere choice; second, that a person fleeing a beast, when faced with two equally good roads, will choose one without any determinant—because urgency precludes deliberation—and similarly a thirsty person with two equal cups of water will choose without a cause. This is offered as evidence that a possible event can occur without a cause. The answer (al-jawāb) reminds the reader of a previously known point: that preference (tarjīḥ) by a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) without a specific motive (dāʿī) is not impossible; what is impossible is the preference of one of two sides of a possible without an external specifying cause. The text then notes that the reader already knows the problems with this answer (insinuating it is controversial or not fully accepted). The passage exemplifies the Ashʿarī position that a free agent can act without a determining motive, contrary to necessitarian views.

Key Terms
الضرورة
قدرة العبد
الهارب من السبع
المرجح
الفاعل المختار
الداعي
السبب المرجح من خارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the fleeing person case proves that actions can occur without any cause at all, thereby undermining the Ashʿarī commitment to divine causation.
Correction: The Ashʿarī answer clarifies that the cause is the agent's own free choice (tarjīḥ without a specific dāʿī), not an external specifying cause. Thus, every event still has a cause—the free agent's will.

Checkpoint
According to the Ashʿarī answer, what is not impossible?
A) A possible event occurring without any cause.
B) Preference by a free agent without a motive.
C) Two equal roads being available to a fleeing person.
D) The power of the servant causing actions independently.

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarī position defends divine omnipotence by denying that human power causes actions independently, and by allowing free choice without a determining motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment today when you chose between two equally appealing options. Did you feel a determining force inside you, or could you have chosen otherwise? According to this lesson, your choice without a motive is metaphysically possible. How does that realization change your sense of responsibility before Allah for that choice? Will you now pause before acting, knowing that your will is not just a passive effect but a genuine exercise of agency?
?
The claimant says we know necessarily that our power causes our actions. But if you are honest, do you ever sense that your actions are not entirely your own—that they depend on Allah's creation of the act? How does the Ashʿarī view that human power is not an independent cause affect your reliance on Allah? Would you still feel accountable for your sins if you believed you were not the ultimate cause? If not, what does that tell you about your current understanding of tawḥīd?
?
When you face a difficult moral choice, do you assume that your desires or external circumstances determine your decision? This lesson suggests that a free agent can prefer without a motive. Are you willing to accept that claim for yourself? If so, then you can break free from the excuse 'I had no choice.' Reflect on a past sin you justified by saying 'I couldn't help it.' How would you now answer Allah if He questions that sin? Resolve to never use that excuse again.

Claim of Necessity in the Servant's Power and the Fleeing Person
Sanusi presents an objection and its answer regarding necessity in human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that we know by necessity the servant's power is effective and that the fleeing person chooses without a preponderant factor, and the Ash'ari answer that such cases involve preponderance from a free agent without motive, which is not impossible.

Core Idea
Perceived necessity in human action is just free choice, which is not impossible.

This passage presents a key objection against the Ash'ari position on causality. The objector claims we know by necessity that the servant's power is effective and that a fleeing person chooses without a preponderant factor. Sanusi then provides the answer, distinguishing between a preponderance from a free agent without motive (possible) and a preponderance without any cause (impossible).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the objection to the answer.
The passage is structured as a claim followed by a response.
2
Identify the objector's claims
Find the two examples given: the servant's power being effective, and the fleeing person choosing between equal paths. Understand that the objector sees these as known by necessity and as instances of a contingent occurring without a cause.
3
Analyze the answer
Focus on the answer: this is like preponderance from a free agent without a motive, which is not impossible. What is impossible is preponderance without any external cause.
The author notes that the reader already knows issues with this answer, but the core distinction is key.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why this matters for the debate on causality and free will. How does this answer defend Ash'ari occasionalism?
This answer rebuts the objection that observable causality contradicts Ash'ari teaching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الرابع: في أبحاث الممكن لذاته ( الثامنة: دعوى الضرورة في قدرة العبد وفي قضية الهارب من السبع) أي نعلم بالضرورة أن قدرة العبد مؤثرة على وفق إرادته، وأن أفعاله صادرة عنه بمجرد اختياره ونعلم بالضرورة أيضاً أن الهارب من السبع إذا عن له طريقان متساويان فإنه يختار أحدهما بلا مرجح؛ لأنه مع شدة احتياجه إلى الفرار يستحيل منه أن يقف ويتفكر في رجحان أحدهما على الآخر، وكذا الحال في العطشان إذا أحضر عنده قدحان من الماء متساويان، فقد وجد ممكن حادث بلا سبب . ( والجواب : ما قد عرفت ) من مثل ذلك ترجيح من فاعل مختار بلا داع وليس بمستحيل إنما المحال ترجح أحد طرفي الممكن بلا سبب مرجح من خارج، وقد عرفت أيضاً ما في هذا الجواب
The Third Observatory - The Fourth Purpose: On the Investigations of the Contingent in Itself. (The eighth: the claim of necessity in the power of the servant and in the case of the one fleeing from a lion.) That is, we know by necessity that the power of the servant is effective in accordance with his will, and that his actions proceed from him by mere choice. And we also know by necessity that the one fleeing from a lion, if two equal paths appear to him, he chooses one of them without a preponderant factor; because due to his intense need to flee, it is impossible for him to stand and reflect on the preponderance of one over the other. The same applies to the thirsty person when two equal cups of water are presented to him. Thus, a contingent originated thing has occurred without a cause. (The answer: what you have already known) from such as that: a preponderance from a free agent without a motive is not impossible; rather, the impossible is the preponderance of one side of a contingent without a preponderant cause from outside. And you have also known what is in this answer.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth issue in the investigation of the contingent: a claim of necessity regarding the effective power of the servant and the case of the fleeing person. The passage begins by stating the objector's claim: we know by necessity that the servant's power is effective according to his will and that his actions are produced by his mere choice. Similarly, we know by necessity that the person fleeing from a lion, when faced with two equal paths, will choose one without any preponderant factor, because his intense need to flee prevents him from deliberating. The same applies to the thirsty person with two equal cups. The objector concludes that a contingent originated thing has occurred without a cause. The answer follows: this is like the preponderance from a free agent without a motive, which is not impossible. What is impossible is the preponderance of one side of a contingent without an external preponderant cause. The author then notes that the reader already knows the issues with this answer.

Key Terms

ضرورة (necessity)

داع (motive)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The examples show that a contingent effect occurs without any cause at all, proving causality from the servant.
Correction: The answer clarifies that there is a cause: the free agent's choice. What is impossible is preponderance without any external preponderant cause, not preponderance from a free agent without a motive.

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim to know by necessity in the eighth issue?
A) That the servant's power has no effect at all
B) That the servant's power is effective according to his will and actions proceed from his mere choice
C) That all actions are caused by external factors only
D) That the fleeing person cannot choose without a preponderant factor

Key Takeaway
The objection's claim of necessary causality is answered by noting that preponderance from a free agent without a motive is possible, and only preponderance without any cause is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a decision you think is forced by circumstances, do you remember that you are a free agent and can choose without external necessity? How might this change your attitude of blame or helplessness?
?
The objector assumes necessity in your power. Do you ever act as if your choices are predetermined, excusing yourself from responsibility? What step can you take today to own a choice you've been avoiding?
?
If even the fleeing person is not compelled by necessity, then your every action is your own. Which action in your daily life do you most often attribute to 'having no choice'? How can you reclaim that choice as a free agent accountable before Allah?

The Need for a Cause: Origination, Not Possibility
Sanusi presents the mutakallimūn's view with proofs and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimūn argue that the need for a cause is due to origination (ḥudūth) rather than possibility (imkān), and how they respond to a key objection.

Core Idea
Origination, not mere possibility, demands a cause.

In this concluding section of the discussion on the contingent, Sanusi presents the core argument of the mutakallimūn: it is the fact that things come into existence (ḥudūth) that requires a cause, not the mere fact that they could exist. He supports this with three arguments and then critically examines the first one, offering a nuanced understanding of causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage presents three proofs for the thesis. Find each one and note how they differ. The first uses an analogy, the second appeals to intuition, the third is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the refutation
After stating the proofs, Sanusi notes they are all subject to scrutiny. Then he refutes the first proof in detail. Pay attention to his criticism of the builder analogy and the distinction between efficient and preparatory causes.
4
Reflect on the key distinction
Consider the central claim: the need for a cause arises at the moment of origination, not from the essence’s possibility. How does the refutation refine this understanding? This is a fundamental point in kalām causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة) للبحث الأول من أبحاث الممكن ( قال المتكلمون : المحوج إلى السبب ( هو الحدوث ) ، لا الإمكان لأن الممكن إنما يحتاج إلى المؤثر في خروجه من العدم إلى الوجود، أعني الحدوث ؛ إذ ماهيته لا تفي بذلك، فإذا خرجت إلى الوجود زالت الحاجة، ولهذا يبقى بعد زوال المؤثر كبقاء البناء بعد فناء البناء، وأيضاً إذا لاحظ العقل حدوث شيء طلب علته، وإن لم يلاحظ معه شيئاً آخر. وأيضاً لو كان المحوج هو الإمكان لأحوج في جانب العدم، فيلزم أن تكون الأعدام الأزلية معللة مع كونها مستمرة والكل منظور فيه، أما الأول فلانه ليس لماهية الممكن خروج من العدم إلى الوجود مسمى بالحدوث، وإلا لكانت حالة الخروج عارية عنهما معاً، بل ليس لها إلا الاتصاف بالعدم أو الاتصاف بالوجود فاحتياجها إلى المؤثر في هذا الاتصاف. وقضية البناء كاذبة، فإن البناء ليس علة موجدة للبناء حقيقة وكلامنا في العلة الموجدة ، بل هو بحركة يده مثلاً علة لحركات الآلات من الخشبات واللبنات، وتلك الحركات علة معدة لأوضاع مخصوصة بين تلك الآلات، وتلك الأوضاع مستندة إلى علل فاعلية غير تلك الحركات المستندة إلى حركة البناء، فلا يضرها عدم شيء منها
(Concluding section) for the first investigation of the investigations of the contingent. The mutakallimūn said: The cause of the need for a cause is origination, not possibility; because the contingent only needs the efficient cause in its exiting from non-existence to existence, i.e., origination; since its essence does not suffice for that. So when it has exited to existence, the need ceases, and for this reason it remains after the removal of the efficient cause, like the remaining of the building after the death of the builder. Also, if the intellect observes the origination of something, it seeks its cause, even if it does not observe anything else with it. Also, if the cause of need were possibility, it would necessitate in the side of non-existence, leading to the consequence that eternal non-existences would be caused despite their being continuous. And all of this is subject to scrutiny. As for the first: it is not the case that the essence of the contingent has an exiting from non-existence to existence called origination; otherwise the state of exiting would be devoid of both, but it has nothing but the attribute of non-existence or the attribute of existence, so its need for the efficient cause is in this attribute. And the claim of the building is false, for the builder is not truly an efficient cause of the building—and our speech is about the efficient cause—rather, he by moving his hand, for example, is a cause for the movements of the tools, the planks and bricks, and those movements are a preparatory cause for specific positions among those tools, and those positions are attributed to efficient causes other than those movements attributed to the builder's movement, so the removal of none of them harms it.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is the concluding section (khatima) of the first investigation of the contingent. It presents the position of the mutakallimūn that the cause of need for a cause (al-muḥawwij ilā al-sabab) is origination (al-ḥudūth), not possibility (al-imkān). They give three proofs. First, the contingent only needs the efficient cause in its passage from non-existence to existence, which is origination; once it exists, the need ceases, analogous to a building remaining after the builder dies. Second, the intellect, when observing origination, seeks a cause even without observing anything else. Third, if possibility were the cause, it would necessitate a cause in the side of non-existence, leading to the unacceptable conclusion that eternal non-existences would be caused. The author then notes that all of this is subject to scrutiny (wa l-kullu manẓūrun fīh). He then critiques the first proof: the essence of the contingent does not have an exiting from non-existence to existence called origination; otherwise the state of exiting would be devoid of both existence and non-existence. In reality, the contingent only has the attribute of non-existence or the attribute of existence, so its need is for the efficient cause in this attribution. The analogy of the building is false because the builder is not a true efficient cause; he only causes movements of tools, which are preparatory causes for specific positions, and those positions themselves depend on other efficient causes.

Key Terms
خاتمة
محوج
حدوث
إمكان
مؤثر
علة موجدة
علة معدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the need for a cause arises from the essence's possibility itself, even before existence.
Correction: The mutakallimūn argue that the need is specifically at the moment of origination—the transition from non-existence to existence—not from the essence considered abstractly.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument of the mutakallimūn for the need being due to origination?
A) The builder analogy: the building remains after the builder dies
B) The intellect seeks a cause for any originated thing
C) Possibility would require a cause for eternal non-existence
D) The contingent essence exits from non-existence to existence

Key Takeaway
The need for a cause stems from origination (ḥudūth), not possibility (imkān); and the first proof's builder analogy is refuted by distinguishing efficient from preparatory causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your own existence is dependent on a cause at every moment, or do you feel self-sufficient? Reflect on the analogy of the building—if the builder is not the true cause, who is the true cause of your continued existence? What does that demand of you?
?
The intellect seeks a cause when it observes something originated, yet we often act as if random events have no purpose. When you see something come into being, do you instinctively acknowledge the Creator? If not, what blocks you?
?
The passage distinguishes between efficient cause and preparatory cause. In your life, do you mistake preparatory causes (like your efforts) for the true efficient cause (God)? How can you align your gratitude and reliance accordingly?

Responding to the Objection of Eternal Non-Existences
Commentary addresses objection that imkan would necessitate eternal non-existences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that contingency would require eternal non-existences to be caused is answered, and why this answer defends the contingency argument.

Core Idea
Eternal non-existences are not caused; their apparent causality is just the absence of existence's effect.

Sanusi now addresses an important objection to the argument that contingency is the necessitator for a cause. The objector claims that if contingency were the reason for need, it would also apply to non-existence, leading to eternal non-existences being caused. The answer clarifies that non-existence affecting non-existence is not real action, and real effect can be continuous, thus the objection does not hold.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is posed: if contingency is the necessitator, it would necessitate in the side of non-existence, making eternal non-existences caused.
Notice the assumption that contingency applies equally to non-existence.
2
Identify the key claim
The objection claims that if contingency were the cause, eternal non-existences would be caused and continuous, which seems contradictory to the idea of causality.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer states that non-existence affecting non-existence is merely the non-effect of existence on existence, not a real action. Real effect can also be continuous.
Distinguish between real action and the absence of action.
4
Connect to the contingency argument
Understand how this defense preserves the role of contingency as the necessitator without leading to absurdities about eternal non-existences.
The objection is neutralized because the apparent causality is not genuine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً لو كان المحوج هو الإمكان لأحوج في جانب العدم، فيلزم أن تكون الأعدام الأزلية معللة مع كونها مستمرة والكل منظور فيه، قوله : ( من أن عدم المعلول إلخ لأن تأثير العدم في العدم ليس إلا عدم تأثير الوجود في الوجود، فليس ها هنا فعل وانفعال حقيقة حتى ينافي الاستمرار على أن التأثير الحقيقي أيضاً لا ينافي الاستمرار لجواز أن يكون التأثير والأثر كلاهما مستمرين.
And also, if the necessitator was contingency, it would necessitate in the side of non-existence, leading to the conclusion that eternal non-existences are caused while they are continuous, and all of it is considered. He said: (From that non-existence of the effect etc.) Because the effect of non-existence on non-existence is nothing but the non-effect of existence on existence, so there is no action and passion in reality such that it contradicts continuity, on the grounds that real effect also does not contradict continuity because it is possible that the effect and the effect are both continuous.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer regarding the implication that if contingency (imkan) were the cause for need, it would also necessitate in the side of non-existence, leading to eternal non-existences being caused. The commentary responds that the effect of non-existence on non-existence is merely the non-effect of existence on existence, which is not a real action that would contradict continuity. Real effect can also be continuous, so the objection does not hold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection assumes that need must be for existence only, but need applies to both attributes.
Correction: Contingency is a property that can apply to both existence and non-existence; the answer shows that the apparent causality of non-existences is not real action.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does the objection about eternal non-existences not disprove that contingency is the necessitator?
A) Because eternal non-existences are not real things.
B) Because the effect of non-existence on non-existence is not a real action, and real effect can be continuous.
C) Because contingency only applies to existents, not non-existents.
D) Because the objection confuses eternal non-existences with existents.

Key Takeaway
The objection that contingency would necessitate eternal non-existences is invalid because non-existence's effect is not real action, and real effect can be continuous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Three Views on What Necessitates a Cause and Al-Razi's Critique
Enumeration of three views and critique by al-Razi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three theological perspectives on what requires a cause (contingency, temporal origination, or both) and why Imam al-Razi argues that temporal origination cannot be part of the necessitator.

Core Idea
Temporal origination is a property of existence and cannot itself be the cause of need.

In classical kalam, scholars debated what exactly makes a contingent being require a cause. Is it its contingency (imkan), its having come into existence (huduth), or a combination of both? This passage presents three answers and then delivers a powerful critique by Imam al-Razi, who argues that temporal origination is too far down the causal chain to play any necessitating role.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
Read the passage and note the two specific views: (1) both contingency and origination are parts of the cause, (2) contingency is the cause but conditioned by origination. The third view is that all three are weak.
2
Understand al-Razi's argument
Trace his chain: origination is a property of existence → posterior to existence → existence posterior to effect → effect posterior to need → need posterior to cause of need. Therefore origination cannot be the cause of need.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider why origination cannot be a necessitator even if it is a property of contingent things.
This shows that only intrinsic contingency (imkan) can be the necessitator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وقيل : المحوج إلى المؤثر هو الإمكان مع الحدوث )، فيكون كل منهما جزءاً من العلة المحوجة (وقيل): المحوج هو (الإمكان بشرط الحدوث ) فيكون الإمكان علة محوجة، والحدوث شرطاً لعليتها، وتأثيرها، قالوا: دليل الفريقين السابقين يقتضي اعتبار كل من الإمكان والحدوث، فيعتبر الحدوث إما شرطاً وإما شطراً. ( وقيل : (الكل) أي كل واحد من الأقوال الثلاثة (ضعيف). قال الإمام الرازي: لأن) الحدوث صفة للوجود لأنه عبارة عن مسبوقية الوجود بالعدم، فيكون صفة له قطعاً ( فيتأخر ) الحدوث ( عن الوجود)، لأن صفة الشيء متأخرة عنه . (وهو) أي الوجود ) متأخر عن تأثير العلة ) أي عن الإيجاد ) المتأخر عن الحاجة )؛ لأن الشيء إذا لم يحتج في نفسه إلى مؤثر لم يتصور تأثيره فيه، كما في الواجب والممتنع ( المتأخرة عن علة الحاجة ) بالضرورة (فيلزم) على تقدير كون
(And it was said: The necessitator for the cause is contingency with temporal origination), so each of them is a part of the necessitating cause. (And it was said): The necessitator is (contingency with the condition of temporal origination), so contingency is a necessitating cause, and temporal origination is a condition for its causality and effect. They said: The proof of the two previous groups requires consideration of both contingency and temporal origination, so temporal origination is considered either a condition or a part. (And it was said: (All) i.e., each of the three statements (is weak). Imam al-Razi said: Because) temporal origination is a property of existence, for it is the precedence of existence over non-existence, so it is a property of it definitively, (so temporal origination is posterior to existence), because the property of a thing is posterior to it. (And it (i.e., existence) is posterior to the effect of the cause) i.e., to the bringing into existence, (which is posterior to the need); because if a thing does not need in itself a cause, its effect in it cannot be conceived, as in the necessary and the impossible, (which is posterior to the cause of need) necessarily, (so it follows) on the supposition of being...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of three views on what necessitates a cause for a contingent being. The first view holds that both contingency (imkan) and temporal origination (huduth) together are the necessitator, each a part. The second view holds that contingency is the cause, but only when conditioned by origination. The third view declares all three preceding views weak. The passage then presents Imam al-Razi's argument against considering origination: origination is a property of existence (since it is the precedence of existence over non-existence), so it is posterior to existence. Existence is posterior to the effect of the cause, which is posterior to the need. Origination is therefore too far down the causal chain to be the necessitator itself. The chain of posteriority shows that origination cannot be the cause of need.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal origination can be a cause of need.
Correction: Imam al-Razi shows that origination is a property of existence and thus too far down the causal chain to be the necessitator.

Checkpoint

A) Because origination is a property of contingency, not existence.
B) Because origination is a property of existence and is posterior to it, and existence is posterior to the effect and need.
C) Because origination is prior to existence.
D) Because origination is the same as contingency.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi's critique shows that temporal origination, being a property of existence, cannot be the very source of a thing's need for a cause.

?
Consider the chain: your existence depends on Allah's action, and that action depends on your need, and your need depends on the fact that you are not necessary. Go through that chain in your own life. Where in your daily habits do you forget that you are contingent? What is one concrete step you can take today to live out this dependency?

Three Views on the Cause of Need and Their Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents three views on what necessitates the effect and al-Razi's criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three views on what necessitates the need for a cause in possible beings and al-Razi's logical criticism that they all lead to a vicious regress.

Core Idea
Temporality cannot be the cause of need because it is posterior to existence, leading to self-reference.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents three theological positions on what exactly necessitates the need for a cause in contingent beings. The debate centers on whether possibility alone, possibility combined with temporality, or possibility conditioned by temporality is the true necessitating factor. Imam al-Razi then subjects all three to a devastating logical critique, showing that each leads to a vicious regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Pay attention to the structure: the passage first references earlier replies, then introduces three views, and finally presents al-Razi's criticism.
2
Identify the three views
Look for the phrases 'وقيل' and 'قالوا' to find the three formulations: (1) possibility alone, (2) possibility together with temporality (both as parts), (3) possibility conditioned by temporality (temporality as condition). Note that the first view is not explicitly restated but is implied from the context.
The passage opens with 'وأما الثاني' which refers back to earlier material; the three views are given in the second half.
3
Understand al-Razi's argument
Al-Razi argues that temporality (الحدوث) is an attribute of existence, so it is posterior to existence. Existence is posterior to the effect of the cause, which is posterior to the need, and the need is posterior to the cause of need. Thus, if temporality were a cause of need, it would be posterior to itself, which is impossible.
The key is the chain: temporality → existence → effect of cause → need → cause of need → temporality.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that all three views are weak (ضعيف) because they imply a self-contradiction. Consider why this matters for kalam arguments about causality: it shows that temporality cannot be a genuine factor in the causal relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني فلان العقل لو جوز وجود الحادث لذاته لما طلب علته أصلاً، فظهر أن ذلك الطلب لملاحظة إمكانه الناشئة من ملاحظة اتصافه بالعدم أولاً، وبالوجود ثانياً . أن وأما الثالث فلما عرفت في جواب الشبهة الرابعة من عدم المعلول لعدم العلة، وإن كانا مستمرين ( وقيل : المحوج إلى المؤثر هو ( الإمكان مع الحدوث )، فيكون كل منهما جزءاً من العلة المحوجة (وقيل): المحوج هو (الإمكان بشرط الحدوث ) فيكون الإمكان علة محوجة، والحدوث شرطاً لعليتها، وتأثيرها، قالوا: دليل الفريقين السابقين يقتضي اعتبار كل من الإمكان والحدوث، فيعتبر الحدوث إما شرطاً وإما شطراً. ( وقيل : (الكل) أي كل واحد من الأقوال الثلاثة (ضعيف). قال الإمام الرازي: لأن) الحدوث صفة للوجود لأنه عبارة عن مسبوقية الوجود بالعدم، فيكون صفة له قطعاً ( فيتأخر ) الحدوث ( عن الوجود)، لأن صفة الشيء متأخرة عنه . (وهو) أي الوجود ) متأخر عن تأثير العلة ) أي عن الإيجاد ) المتأخر عن الحاجة )؛ لأن الشيء إذا لم يحتج في نفسه إلى مؤثر لم يتصور تأثيره فيه، كما في الواجب والممتنع ( المتأخرة عن علة الحاجة ) بالضرورة (فيلزم) على تقدير كون الحدوث علة للحاجة أو جزءاً لها أو شرطاً ( تأخره عن نفسه بمراتب) أربع، على التقدير الأول والثالث وخمس على التقدير الثاني؛ لأن جزء العلة متقدم عليها. والأظهر في العبارة أن يقال فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه بمراتب، والمال في : المعنى واحد
As for the second [reply], it is because the intellect, if it allowed the existence of a temporal event by itself, would not seek its cause at all. So it became clear that that seeking is due to observing its possibility arising from observing its being qualified by non-existence first, and by existence second. As for the third [reply], it is as you came to know in answering the fourth doubt: that the effect fails to exist due to the failure of the cause, even if both are continuous. And it was said: That which necessitates the effect is possibility together with temporality, so each of them is a part of the necessitating cause. And it was said: That which necessitates is possibility conditioned by temporality, so possibility is the necessitating cause, and temporality is a condition for its causality and effect. They said: The proof of the two preceding groups requires that both possibility and temporality be considered, so temporality is considered either as a condition or as a part. And it was said: All three statements are weak. Imam al-Razi said: Because temporality is an attribute of existence, since it is the precedence of existence by non-existence, so it is an attribute of it decisively. Thus temporality is posterior to existence, because the attribute of a thing is posterior to it. And existence is posterior to the effect of the cause, i.e., to bringing into existence, which is posterior to the need; because if a thing does not in itself need an effect, its being affected by it is inconceivable, as in the case of the necessary and the impossible. And [the effect] is posterior to the cause of the need necessarily. So on the assumption that temporality is the cause of the need or a part of it or a condition, there follows its being posterior to itself by stages: four on the first and third estimates, and five on the second, because the part of the cause is prior to it. The clearer expression is to say: there follows the priority of the thing over itself by stages, and the result is one.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of three views on what necessitates the need for a cause in a possible being, followed by a criticism that all three are weak. The author first explains two preceding replies (not fully quoted here) and then introduces three opinions: (1) the necessitating cause is possibility alone, (2) it is possibility together with temporality, each being a part, (3) it is possibility conditioned by temporality, where possibility is the cause and temporality a condition. Then a group argues that the proofs of the two earlier groups require considering both possibility and temporality, so temporality is either a condition or a part. Then it is reported that all three statements are weak. Imam al-Razi provides a proof of weakness: temporality is an attribute of existence, so it is posterior to existence; existence is posterior to the effect of the cause; the effect of the cause is posterior to the need; the need is posterior to the cause of need. Therefore, if temporality is the cause (or part or condition) of the need, it would be posterior to itself by multiple stages (four or five depending on the estimate), which is absurd. The clearer formulation is that the thing would be prior to itself by stages. This argument shows the logical difficulty of regarding temporality as part of the causal nexus.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الحدوث
المحوج
العلة
الوجود
التأخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That at least one of the three views on the cause of need might be correct if we consider temporality as a condition rather than a part.
Correction: Al-Razi's argument shows that regardless of whether temporality is a cause, part, or condition, it still leads to self-posteriority because the chain of posteriority does not depend on the mode of involvement.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what does the claim that temporality is a cause of need entail?
A) That temporality is prior to existence.
B) That temporality is posterior to itself.
C) That possibility is posterior to temporality.
D) That need is prior to temporality.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's criticism shows that any view making temporality a cause of need leads to a logical contradiction, undermining those positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical refutation that exposes a flaw in your own reasoning, do you quickly dismiss it or examine it sincerely? This lesson shows that even respected scholars' views must be scrutinized. Reflect on a time you defended a flawed position out of pride. What would change if you applied al-Razi's rigorous approach to your own beliefs?
?
The argument here reveals a subtle self-reference. Are there beliefs you hold that, when analyzed deeply, lead to contradictions? How willing are you to subject your most cherished ideas to logical scrutiny, knowing it may demand humility and change?
