The Fallacy of Confusing Mental and External Matters
Al-Jurjani refutes al-Razi's argument as a fallacy of confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani refutes the objection by distinguishing between mental concepts and external causal relations.

Core Idea
Confusing mental concepts with external causes leads to logical fallacies.

This lesson examines al-Jurjani's decisive response to a common objection. He argues that critics have mistakenly treated mental judgments as if they were external causal relations. By clarifying the true meaning of the kalam scholars' statements, he dissolves the objection and reaffirms the correct understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the main claim: that the criticism is a fallacy arising from confusion.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the confusion
The confusion is between mental matters (الذهنية) and external matters (الخارجية). The scholars' statement about temporality causing need is a mental judgment, not an external causal relation.
Note the term 'اعتباريان' – both temporality and need are mental considerations.
3
Understand the alternative explanation
Al-Jurjani explains that the scholars meant only that the mind judges need upon observing temporality. This is not a claim of external causation, so the objection that it leads to temporal priority is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المصنف : ) ولا يخفى أنه أي ما ذكره هذا القائل ( مغالطة ) نشأت من اشتباه الأمور الذهنية بالخارجية وتنزيلها منزلتها ( لأنهم لم يريدوا) بقولهم : أن الحدوث علة الحاجة أو جزؤها أو شرطها ) إلا أن حكم العقل بالحاجة لملاحظة الحدوث، إما وحده، أو مع الإمكان، وهذا حق لا شبهة فيه، (لأن الحدوث علة في الخارج للحاجة. ( فيوجد) الحدوث في الخارج أولاً، فتوجد الحاجة فيه ثانيا؛ لأن الحدوث والحاجة أمران ،اعتباريان فكيف يتصور كون أحدهما علة للآخر في الخارج حتى يرد عليه أنه يلزم منه تقدم الشيء على نفسه بمراتب . ونحن نقول : إن قولنا الممكن محتاج في وجوده إلى مؤثر قضية صادقة نفس الأمر، فيكون الممكن موصوفاً في حد ذاته بالحاجة إلى غيره، فكما أن اتصاف الشيء بالصفات الوجودية يحتاج إلى علة ذات الموصوف أو غيره، كذلك اتصافه
The author said: And it is not hidden that what this speaker mentioned is a fallacy arising from confusing mental matters with external ones and treating them as such. Because what they meant by their saying 'temporality is the cause of need or its part or its condition' is only that the mind's judgment of need is due to the observation of temporality, either alone or with possibility. And this is true with no doubt. For temporality is not a cause of need in the external world. Thus temporality does not exist externally first and then need exists second, because temporality and need are two mental considerations. So how can one imagine that one of them is a cause for the other in the external world, such that there would follow the objection that it entails the priority of the thing over itself by stages? And we say: Our statement 'the possible is in need of an effect in its existence' is a true proposition in reality. So the possible is qualified in its very essence by the need for something else. Just as the qualification of a thing with existential attributes needs a cause, either the essence of the qualified or something else, similarly its qualification...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the previous criticism. The author (al-Jurjani) states that the earlier argument is a fallacy arising from confusing mental concepts with external realities. The kalam scholars, when they say 'temporality is a cause of need or part of it or a condition,' mean only that the mind's judgment of need arises from observing temporality (alone or with possibility). They do not mean that temporality is an actual external cause of need. If it were an external cause, then temporality would have to exist before need in the external world, but temporality and need are both mental considerations (اعتباريان). Therefore, the objection that this leads to the thing preceding itself by stages does not apply, because causation here is not external but a relation of mental judgments. The author then affirms that the proposition 'the possible needs an effect' is true in reality, so the possible is inherently characterized by need for another. The explanation cuts off but was presumably continuing to argue that such need is a real attribute that requires a cause, but the confusion arises from misapplying external causal relationships to mental constructs.

Key Terms
مغالطة
الأمور الذهنية
الخارجية
حكم العقل
الاعتباري

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the kalam scholars treat temporality as an actual external cause of need.
Correction: They mean only that the mind's judgment of need arises from observing temporality; temporality is not an external cause.

Checkpoint

B) Because it confuses mental matters with external ones.
C) Because it assumes temporality is an attribute of existence.
D) Because it ignores the role of the intellect.

Key Takeaway
The kalam scholars' statements about temporality and need are mental judgments, not claims of external causation, so objections based on temporal priority are invalid.

The Regress Objection Refuted: Mental vs. External
Al-Jurjani refutes the regress objection to huduth as cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the regress objection against huduth as a cause of need is a fallacy and how the distinction between mental and external matters resolves it.

Core Idea
The regress objection confuses mental judgments with external causation.

In this passage, al-Iji and al-Jurjani address a common objection: if origination (huduth) is a cause of need, it would lead to an infinite regress. Their response is sharp and foundational: the objection only works if we confuse a mental judgment with an external causal relationship. This distinction is vital for understanding how kalam arguments work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Note the technical terms: الحدوث (origination), الحاجة (need), العلة (cause), المغالطة (fallacy).
The passage is dense; take your time to follow the argument.
2
Identify the claimed regress
The objection states that if huduth is a cause, part, or condition of need, then the thing would precede itself by degrees (4 or 5). Find this in the text and understand the calculation.
The author corrects the expression to say 'preceding itself by degrees' more clearly.
3
Understand the fallacy
Al-Jurjani declares this a fallacy 'arising from confusing mental matters with external ones.' Grasp that the objection mistakenly treats a mental judgment as if it were an external causal chain.
This is the key insight of the passage.
4
Appreciate the correct intention
The intended meaning is that the intellect judges need based on observing huduth. This is a mental judgment, not an external causation. Thus no regress occurs.
This preserves the soundness of the huduth argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الرابع في أبحاث الممكن لذاته
·
،
الحدوث علة للحاجة أو جزءاً لها أو شرطاً ( تأخره عن نفسه بمراتب) أربع، على التقدير الأول والثالث وخمس على التقدير الثاني؛ لأن جزء العلة متقدم عليها. والأظهر في العبارة أن يقال فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه بمراتب، والمال في : المعنى واحد قال المصنف : ) ولا يخفى أنه أي ما ذكره هذا القائل ( مغالطة ) نشأت من اشتباه الأمور الذهنية بالخارجية وتنزيلها منزلتها ( لأنهم لم يريدوا) بقولهم : أن الحدوث علة الحاجة أو جزؤها أو شرطها ) إلا أن حكم العقل بالحاجة لملاحظة الحدوث، إما وحده، أو مع الإمكان، وهذا حق لا شبهة فيه، (لأن الحدوث علة في الخارج للحاجة. ( فيوجد) الحدوث في الخارج أولاً، فتوجد الحاجة فيه ثانيا؛ لأن الحدوث والحاجة أمران ،اعتباريان فكيف يتصور كون أحدهما علة للآخر في الخارج حتى يرد عليه أنه يلزم منه تقدم الشيء على نفسه بمراتب .
The third observatory - the fourth objective: on investigations of the contingent in itself. Origination is a cause of need, or a part of it, or a condition (then it would be delayed from itself by degrees): four on the first and third estimates, and five on the second estimate, because the part of the cause is prior to it. The more apparent in expression is to say: then there follows the thing preceding itself by degrees. The point is one. The author said: 'And it is not hidden that it—i.e., what this speaker mentioned—is a fallacy arising from confusing mental matters with external ones and treating them as such.' Because they did not intend by their saying that origination is a cause of need or a part of it or a condition—except that the judgment of the intellect concerning need is due to observing origination, either alone or with contingency, and this is true with no doubt—[they did not intend] that origination is a cause in the external world for need. (So there would exist) origination in the external world first, then need would exist in it second; because origination and need are two mental considerations. How then can one be conceived as a cause of the other in the external world such that it is objected that there follows from it the thing preceding itself by degrees?
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. It begins by stating the claim: if origination (huduth) is a cause of need, or a part of it, or a condition, then it would lead to the thing preceding itself by multiple degrees—four on two estimates, five on another. The commentator corrects the expression, saying the proper phrasing is that it leads to the thing preceding itself by degrees. He then quotes the text of al-Mawaqif, which declares this argument to be a fallacy born of confusing mental matters (al-umur al-dhihniyya) with external matters (al-umur al-kharijiyya). The intended meaning is not that origination is an external cause, but that the intellect judges need based on observing origination—either alone or together with contingency. This judgment is true. The imagined external causation would require origination to exist first and need to exist second, but both are mental considerations. Thus the regress objection is invalid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The regress argument shows that huduth cannot be a cause of need at all.
Correction: The regress only invalidates the mistaken view that huduth is an external cause; the correct view is that the intellect's judgment based on huduth is valid and involves no regress.

Key Takeaway
The regress objection is invalid because it mistakes a mental judgment for an external causal relation.

The Reality of Need as an Attribute of Contingents
Al-Jurjani explains that need is a real attribute requiring a cause for its attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why need is a real attribute of contingent beings and how its causal structure differs from existential attributes.

Core Idea
Need is a real description of the contingent, not just a mental construct.

Al-Jurjani argues that the statement 'the contingent is in need of a cause' is objectively true. This means need is a genuine attribute of the contingent itself. He then explains how this privative attribute resembles and differs from existential attributes, and how its attribution is causally grounded.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Pay attention to the contrast between existential and privative attributes.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice that Al-Jurjani starts by asserting the proposition 'the contingent is in need' is true in reality. This establishes need as a real attribute.
This is the foundation of the entire discussion.
3
Distinguish existential vs. privative
The passage draws a clear distinction: existential attributes require a cause for both their attribution and their existence, while privative attributes require a cause only for attribution since they have no existence.
The blindness example illustrates this.
4
Examine the blindness example
It is rational to ask why Zayd is described as blind, but not to ask why blindness exists in itself. This shows privative attributes lack independent existence.
This solidifies the distinction.
5
Understand the extension to mental considerations
Al-Jurjani extends the same causal logic to 'mental considerations' (اعتباريات). Just as one positive attribution can explain another, so can mental considerations.
This shows the principle is broader than just privative attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نقول : إن قولنا الممكن محتاج في وجوده إلى مؤثر قضية صادقة نفس الأمر، فيكون الممكن موصوفاً في حد ذاته بالحاجة إلى غيره، فكما أن اتصاف الشيء بالصفات الوجودية يحتاج إلى علة ذات الموصوف أو غيره، كذلك اتصافه بالصفات العدمية محتاج إليها والفرق بين الوجودية والعدمية أن الوجودية تحتاج إلى العلة في وجودها أيضاً، دون العدمية، إذ لا وجود لها ألا ترى أنه إذا قيل : لم اتصف زيد بالعمى كان سؤالاً مقبولاً عند العقلاء، بخلاف ما لو قيل: لأي شيء وجد العمى في نفسه، وكما يجوز أن يعلل اتصاف الشيء بوصف من الأوصاف الثبوتية باتصافه ببعض آخر منها كذلك يجوز أن يعلل اتصافه ببعض الاعتباريات ببعض آخر منها. وكما أن العلل هناك موصوفة بالتقدم على معلولاتها كذلك هاهنا موصوفة به أيضاً، إذا عرفت هذا
And we say: that our statement 'the contingent is in need in its existence of an effective cause' is a proposition true in reality, so the contingent is described in itself as being in need of another. Just as the attribution of a thing with existential attributes requires a cause—either the subject itself or another—so too its attribution with privative attributes is in need of it. The difference between existential and privative [attributes] is that existential attributes require a cause also for their existence, contrary to privative attributes, since they have no existence. Do you not see that when it is said: 'Why was Zayd described with blindness?' it is a question accepted by the rational, unlike if it were said: 'For what thing did blindness exist in itself?' And just as it is permissible to explain the attribution of a thing with a positive attribute by its attribution with another of them, so too it is permissible to explain its attribution with some mental considerations by another of them. And just as the causes there are described as prior to their effects, so too here they are also described with it. If you understand this,
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a positive explanation by the commentator. He asserts that the statement 'the contingent is in need of a cause for its existence' is true in reality, so the contingent actually possesses the attribute of need. Then he analogizes: just as existential attributes (e.g. life, knowledge) require a cause for their attribution—either the subject itself or an external factor—so too privative attributes (like blindness or need) require a cause for their attribution. He then distinguishes between the two: existential attributes require a cause for both their attribution and their existence, while privative attributes need a cause only for their attribution because they have no real existence. He gives the example of blindness: it is valid to ask why Zayd is described as blind, but invalid to ask why blindness exists in itself. He further notes that attribution of one mental consideration can be explained by another, and that the causes of such attributions are prior to their effects. The passage prepares for the next discussion by concluding 'if you understand this'.

Two Views on the Cause of Need and the Objection
Al-Jurjani presents the two views on the cause of need and the objection to the later view based on priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two views on the cause of need in contingent beings and the objection against the later theologians' view based on essential priority.

Core Idea
Cause must be prior, but origination is posterior — a key objection.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a crucial debate: what makes contingent beings need a cause? The ancients said contingency; later theologians said origination. But a logical objection is raised: origination is essentially posterior to existence, so it cannot be the cause of need, which is also posterior to existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question and two views
Read the passage and note the central question: what is the cause of need? Then identify the two answers: ancients (contingency) and later theologians (origination alone or with other).
Notice the terms 'القدماء' and 'المتأخرون'.
2
Grasp the objection's logical structure
The objection argues that origination is essentially posterior to existence, existence is posterior to creation, and need is posterior to that. Therefore origination cannot be prior to need.
The key concept is 'التأخر بالذات' (essential posteriority).
3
Reflect on why essential priority matters
Consider why a cause must be prior to its effect. This is a basic logical principle. The objection uses this to reject origination as a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فالمقصود في هذا المقام بيان أن علة اتصاف الممكن بالحاجة في نفس الأمر ماذا ؟ فذهب القدماء إلى أن تلك العلة ،بالإمكان وذهب جمهور المتأخرين إلى أنها اتصافه بالحدوث وحده، أو مع غيره. فورد عليهم أن اتصاف الحادث بالحدوث في نفس الأمر متأخر بالذات عن بالوجود فيها واتصافه بالوجود متأخر كذلك عن الإيجاد، وهو أيضاً متأخر كذلك احتياجه، فلا يمكن أن يكون اتصافه بالحدوث علة لاتصافه بالحاجة. وهذا كلام
If you understand this, then the purpose in this context is to explain: what is the cause of the contingent's being described with need in reality? The ancients held that that cause is contingency, and the majority of the later [theologians] held that it is its being described with origination alone or with something else. An objection was raised against them: that the thing's being described with origination in reality is posterior in essence to its being described with existence, and its being described with existence is likewise posterior to the act of bringing into existence, and it (i.e., need) is likewise posterior to it. So it cannot be that its being described with origination is a cause for its being described with need. This is speech.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the central question of the section: what is the cause of the contingent's attribute of need in reality? The ancients (al-qudama')—referring to the philosophers—said it is contingency (al-imkan). The majority of later theologians (jumhur al-muta'akhkhirin) said it is its attribute of origination (al-huduth) alone or together with something else. The commentator then records an objection against the later view: the attribute of origination in reality is posterior (muta'akhkhar) in essence to the attribute of existence, and the attribute of existence is posterior to the act of bringing into existence (al-ijad), and need is also posterior to that. Therefore origination cannot be the cause of need because the cause must be prior to its effect. This objection undermines the later theologians' position.

Key Terms
القدماء
المتأخرون
الإمكان
الحدوث
الحاجة
العلة
التأخر بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that huduth can be a cause of need because it is temporally prior, but the argument shows it is essentially posterior to existence and need.
Correction: Essential priority is what matters for causality, not temporal priority. Huduth depends on existence, so it cannot be the cause of need which also depends on existence.

Checkpoint

B) Huduth is essentially posterior to existence and need
C) Imkan is the only possible cause

Key Takeaway
The later theologians' view that origination causes need fails because origination is essentially posterior to existence, and need is also posterior to existence and origination.

The Nature of Accidents and the Distinction of Attributes
Al-Jurjani explains the dispute over accident existence and its impact on attribute classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two competing views on the existence of accidents and how they affect whether attributes are considered real or mental.

Core Idea
The nature of accidents determines whether attributes are real or merely conceptual.

Al-Jurjani begins by clarifying a foundational disagreement about accidents. One view holds that an accident's existence in itself is separate from its existence in a subject; the other view holds they are identical. This dispute directly impacts whether attributes are considered real existential qualities or merely mental privations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on accidents
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the two views presented.
Focus on the phrase 'وجود العرض في نفسه' and 'وجود العرض في الموضوع'.
2
Identify the two views
Note the first view (the commentator's chosen view) that the existence of an accident in itself is other than its existence in the subject. Then note the second view (of al-Dawwani and al-Taftazani) that they are identical.
3
Understand the consequence for attributes
See how each view leads to a different understanding of the difference between existential and privative attributes. On the second view, existential attributes are real, privative attributes are merely mental (intiza'i).
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters for kalam arguments about God's attributes and the nature of creation.
Hint: It affects how we talk about God's knowledge, power, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والفرق إلخ هذه مبني على ما اختاره الشارح قدس سره فيما سيجيء، من أن وجود العرض في نفسه غير وجوده في الموضوع ، ولذا يقال، وجد السواد فقام بالجسم، وأما على ما هو التحقيق من أن وجود العرض في نفسه هو وجوده في الموضوع كما نقله المحقق الدواني في حواشيه عن تعليقات ،الشيخ، وإليه ذهب المحقق التفتازاني فالفرق أن الاتصاف بالصفات الوجودية حقيقي، بخلاف الصفات العدمية فإنه انتزاعي .
قوله : ( فذهب القدماء) أي الأوائل وهم الحكماء، ويؤيده ما وقع في بعض النسخ في أن مقابلته . وذهب جمهور المتكلمين وفي بعض جمهور المتأخرين أي المتكلمين، ولا يتوهم المراد قدماء المتكلمين المتأخرين منهم، فإنه لم يذهب قدماؤهم إلى علية الإمكان أصلاً، كما هو منصوص في الكتب، ثم إن هذا الاختلاف إنما يتأتى إذا كان الاتصاف بالحاجة معللاً بعلة سوى ذات الممكن ولم يجوز أن يكون ذلك مقتضى ذاته من غير أن يكون للإمكان أو الحدوث مدخل في ذلك، فانحصار الاختلاف في الحدوث والإمكان يشعر بأن الاختلاف علة في الحكم بالاتصاف ويؤيده استدلال الفريقين بأن ملاحظة الإمكان وحده، أو الحدوث وحده، يكفي الحكم بالاحتياج. وكذا استدلالهم على ثبوت الواجب بإمكان العالم أو حدوثه يؤيد ذلك، وعلى هذا يجوز أن يكون كل من الإمكان والحدوث علة للحكم بالحاجة، إذ لا تنافي بين أن يكون لمطلوب واحد دليلان كما وقع في شرح المقاصد : من أن كلام الفريقين في الإبطال مغالطة، وأما في الإثبات فكلام المتأخرين أظهر وبالقبول أجدر .
قوله: (اتصافه بالحدوث إلخ) وتعليل بعض الاعتباريات ببعض لا ينافي القول باستناد جميع الموجودات الممكنة إليه تعالى ابتداء .
His statement: '(and the difference etc.)' is based on what the commentator—may his secret be sanctified—chose in what will come, that the existence of an accident in itself is other than its existence in the subject. For this reason it is said: 'Blackness existed, then it subsisted in the body.' But according to what is the truth, that the existence of the accident in itself is its existence in the subject—as transmitted by al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani in his glosses from the comments of the Shaykh, and adopted by al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani—the difference is that the attribution with existential attributes is real, contrary to privative attributes, for it is mental. His statement: '(so the ancients went)' means the first ones, and they are the philosophers. This is supported by what occurs in some copies... while the majority of theologians—and in some [copies] 'the majority of the later'—meaning the theologians. And let it not be imagined that the intended meaning is the earlier theologians among the later ones, for their earlier ones did not at all hold the causality of contingency, as is stated in the books. Furthermore, this disagreement only arises if the attribution with need is explained by a cause other than the essence of the contingent, and it is not allowed that it be a requirement of its essence without contingency or origination having any entry in it. So the restriction of the disagreement to origination and contingency indicates that the difference is a cause in the judgment of attribution. This is supported by the argument of both groups that observing contingency alone, or origination alone, suffices for judging need. Likewise their argument for the existence of the Necessary by the contingency of the world or its origination supports that. Based on this, it is permissible that each of contingency and origination be a cause for the judgment of need, for there is no incompatibility between a single demand having two proofs—as occurred in Sharh al-Maqasid: that the speech of the two groups concerning refutation is a fallacy, but concerning proof, the speech of the later ones is more evident and more worthy of acceptance. His statement: '(his being described with origination etc.)' and explaining some mental considerations by others does not contradict the statement that all contingent existents are dependent on Him, exalted, from the beginning.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a collection of glosses (ta'liqat) by the commentator on specific phrases. The first gloss explains the phrase 'and the difference': it addresses a dispute about the nature of accidents—whether their existence in themselves is separate from their existence in a subject. The commentator's chosen view (as he will later argue) is that they are separate, so the distinction between existential and privative attributes is based on that. The alternative view (attributed to al-Dawwani and al-Taftazani) holds they are identical, so the difference is that existential attribution is real while privative attribution is merely mental (intiza'i). The second gloss clarifies the identity of the ancients: they are the philosophers (hukama'), not the early theologians. It warns against confusing them with the later theologians, and notes that the disagreement only applies if need is caused by something other than the essence of the contingent. It then observes that the restriction of the debate to contingency and origination suggests that both can serve as causes for the judgment of need, and supports this with examples and the principle that a single effect can have multiple proofs. The third gloss confirms that explaining certain mental considerations by others does not negate the ultimate dependence of all contingent beings on God.

Key Terms
العرض
الموضوع
الاتصاف
الصفات الوجودية
الصفات العدمية
الانتزاعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all attributes are real in the same way, but the passage shows that the nature of accidents affects whether attributes are real or mental.
Correction: According to al-Dawwani and al-Taftazani, existential attributes are real attributions while privative attributes are merely mental considerations (intiza'i).

Checkpoint
According to al-Dawwani and al-Taftazani, what is the relationship between the existence of an accident in itself and its existence in the subject?
A) They are different
B) They are identical
C) Accidents do not have existence
D) Accidents have only mental existence

Key Takeaway
The nature of accidents determines whether attributes are real or merely mental considerations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The truth view holds that existential attributes are real, but privative attributes are merely mental. Consider an attribute of Allah like 'al-Qahhar' (The Subduer). Is this an existential attribute or privative? If it is existential, what does it mean for it to be a real attribution? How does this affect your feeling of being subdued by Him? Commit to one way you will live in light of this reality this week.
?
Now reflect on your own attributes: your knowledge is real, but your ignorance is a privation. According to this lesson, your ignorance is merely a mental consideration. Does that make you less accountable for it? Or does it mean that what you lack is not a real thing, so you should focus on acquiring real knowledge? How does this shift your approach to learning and repentance? Resolve to act on one concrete step today.
?
Imagine you meet someone who denies the reality of Allah's attributes, claiming they are all mental constructs. Using the truth view from this passage, how would you argue that at least some attributes are real? More importantly, do you personally struggle to feel that Allah's attributes are real and active in your life? What would change if you fully embraced this? Ask Allah to make these realities alive in your heart.

Identifying the Ancients: Philosophers vs Early Theologians
Al-Jurjani identifies the 'ancients' as philosophers and warns against confusion with early theologians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand who 'the ancients' refers to in this debate and why they are not the early theologians.

Core Idea
The ancients are the philosophers, not the early theologians.

Al-Jurjani clarifies a common terminological confusion. When speaking of 'the ancients' (al-qudama'), it is important to know whether this refers to the philosophers (hukama') or the early theologians. The passage firmly identifies them as the philosophers, and warns against misinterpreting the phrase as referring to early theologians of the later school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the ancients
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to how the commentator identifies the ancients.
Look for the phrase 'أي الأوائل وهم الحكماء'.
2
Identify the correct reference
Note that 'al-qudama' means the first ones, and they are the philosophers (hukama'). The passage also mentions a variant reading in some manuscripts that supports this.
3
Understand the warning
The commentator explicitly warns against thinking that 'the ancients' refers to the early theologians among the later school. He states that the early theologians did not hold the causality of contingency at all.
This is a key clarification to avoid misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والفرق إلخ هذه مبني على ما اختاره الشارح قدس سره فيما سيجيء، من أن وجود العرض في نفسه غير وجوده في الموضوع ، ولذا يقال، وجد السواد فقام بالجسم، وأما على ما هو التحقيق من أن وجود العرض في نفسه هو وجوده في الموضوع كما نقله المحقق الدواني في حواشيه عن تعليقات ،الشيخ، وإليه ذهب المحقق التفتازاني فالفرق أن الاتصاف بالصفات الوجودية حقيقي، بخلاف الصفات العدمية فإنه انتزاعي .
قوله : ( فذهب القدماء) أي الأوائل وهم الحكماء، ويؤيده ما وقع في بعض النسخ في أن مقابلته . وذهب جمهور المتكلمين وفي بعض جمهور المتأخرين أي المتكلمين، ولا يتوهم المراد قدماء المتكلمين المتأخرين منهم، فإنه لم يذهب قدماؤهم إلى علية الإمكان أصلاً، كما هو منصوص في الكتب، ثم إن هذا الاختلاف إنما يتأتى إذا كان الاتصاف بالحاجة معللاً بعلة سوى ذات الممكن ولم يجوز أن يكون ذلك مقتضى ذاته من غير أن يكون للإمكان أو الحدوث مدخل في ذلك، فانحصار الاختلاف في الحدوث والإمكان يشعر بأن الاختلاف علة في الحكم بالاتصاف ويؤيده استدلال الفريقين بأن ملاحظة الإمكان وحده، أو الحدوث وحده، يكفي الحكم بالاحتياج. وكذا استدلالهم على ثبوت الواجب بإمكان العالم أو حدوثه يؤيد ذلك، وعلى هذا يجوز أن يكون كل من الإمكان والحدوث علة للحكم بالحاجة، إذ لا تنافي بين أن يكون لمطلوب واحد دليلان كما وقع في شرح المقاصد : من أن كلام الفريقين في الإبطال مغالطة، وأما في الإثبات فكلام المتأخرين أظهر وبالقبول أجدر .
قوله: (اتصافه بالحدوث إلخ) وتعليل بعض الاعتباريات ببعض لا ينافي القول باستناد جميع الموجودات الممكنة إليه تعالى ابتداء .
His statement: '(and the difference etc.)' is based on what the commentator—may his secret be sanctified—chose in what will come, that the existence of an accident in itself is other than its existence in the subject. For this reason it is said: 'Blackness existed, then it subsisted in the body.' But according to what is the truth, that the existence of the accident in itself is its existence in the subject—as transmitted by al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani in his glosses from the comments of the Shaykh, and adopted by al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani—the difference is that the attribution with existential attributes is real, contrary to privative attributes, for it is mental. His statement: '(so the ancients went)' means the first ones, and they are the philosophers. This is supported by what occurs in some copies... while the majority of theologians—and in some [copies] 'the majority of the later'—meaning the theologians. And let it not be imagined that the intended meaning is the earlier theologians among the later ones, for their earlier ones did not at all hold the causality of contingency, as is stated in the books. So the restriction of the disagreement to origination and contingency indicates that the difference is a cause in the judgment of attribution. This is supported by the argument of both groups that observing contingency alone, or origination alone, suffices for judging need. Likewise their argument for the existence of the Necessary by the contingency of the world or its origination supports that. Based on this, it is permissible that each of contingency and origination be a cause for the judgment of need, for there is no incompatibility between a single demand having two proofs—as occurred in Sharh al-Maqasid: that the speech of the two groups concerning refutation is a fallacy, but concerning proof, the speech of the later ones is more evident and more worthy of acceptance. His statement: '(his being described with origination etc.)' and explaining some mental considerations by others does not contradict the statement that all contingent existents are dependent on Him, exalted, from the beginning.

Multiple Causes for the Same Effect: Contingency and Origination
Al-Jurjani discusses the compatibility of contingency and origination as causes for the world's need for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how both contingency and origination can serve as valid causes for the world's need for God, without contradiction.

Core Idea
Multiple causes can lead to the same conclusion in kalam.

The debate between philosophers and theologians often centers on whether contingency or origination explains the world's dependence on God. Al-Jurjani explains that this disagreement is only meaningful under certain conditions, and that both can be valid proofs for the same conclusion. He also notes that explaining mental considerations by others does not undermine the ultimate dependence of all things on God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on causality
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the conditions for the disagreement and the conclusion that both contingency and origination can be causes.
Look for the phrase 'فانحصار الاختلاف في الحدوث والإمكان'.
2
Understand the condition for disagreement
Note that the disagreement only arises if the attribution of need is caused by something other than the essence of the contingent, and if it is not a requirement of its essence. That is, the need must have an external cause.
3
See the evidence for multiple causes
Observe that the text gives examples: both groups argue that observing contingency alone or origination alone suffices for judging need. Also, both use the contingency or origination of the world to prove the Necessary Existent.
This indicates that each can serve as a cause.
4
Reflect on the compatibility
The passage concludes that it is permissible for both contingency and origination to be causes for the judgment of need, because a single demand can have two proofs. This is supported by Sharh al-Maqasid.
The final gloss adds that explaining mental considerations by others does not contradict the direct dependence of all contingent beings on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله : ( فذهب القدماء) أي الأوائل وهم الحكماء، ويؤيده ما وقع في بعض النسخ في أن مقابلته . وذهب جمهور المتكلمين وفي بعض جمهور المتأخرين أي المتكلمين، ولا يتوهم المراد قدماء المتكلمين المتأخرين منهم، فإنه لم يذهب قدماؤهم إلى علية الإمكان أصلاً، كما هو منصوص في الكتب، ثم إن هذا الاختلاف إنما يتأتى إذا كان الاتصاف بالحاجة معللاً بعلة سوى ذات الممكن ولم يجوز أن يكون ذلك مقتضى ذاته من غير أن يكون للإمكان أو الحدوث مدخل في ذلك، فانحصار الاختلاف في الحدوث والإمكان يشعر بأن الاختلاف علة في الحكم بالاتصاف ويؤيده استدلال الفريقين بأن ملاحظة الإمكان وحده، أو الحدوث وحده، يكفي الحكم بالاحتياج. وكذا استدلالهم على ثبوت الواجب بإمكان العالم أو حدوثه يؤيد ذلك، وعلى هذا يجوز أن يكون كل من الإمكان والحدوث علة للحكم بالحاجة، إذ لا تنافي بين أن يكون لمطلوب واحد دليلان كما وقع في شرح المقاصد : من أن كلام الفريقين في الإبطال مغالطة، وأما في الإثبات فكلام المتأخرين أظهر وبالقبول أجدر .

?

?

Reflect on whether your attachment to a particular argument has ever caused you to reject a sincere believer's approach. What would change if you embraced the principle that 'one conclusion can have multiple proofs'?
?
This lesson teaches that even if some arguments are fallacious in refutation, the positive proofs can still be sound. Where in your life do you confuse the weakness of a counter-argument with the weakness of the truth itself? Commit today to distinguishing between flawed rejections and valid affirmations, especially when the matter is your relationship with God.

Refutation of the Claim that Non-Existence is Easier
Refutes the claim that non-existence is inherently easier for the possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation of the claim that non-existence is easier for the possible establishes that preponderance is not inherent in the possible's essence.

Core Idea
Ease of non-existence is not inherent but relative to external factors.

In this passage, Sanusi examines a specific claim about the possible entity: that non-existence is easier because its existence requires many conditions. He refutes this by showing that such ease does not arise from the possible itself. He then addresses two additional opinions that try to specify conditions for preponderance, demonstrating that they too fail to prove inherent preponderance.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a claim, a refutation, then two opinions and a final refutation.
2
Identify the claim and its refutation
The claim is that non-existence is easier because existence requires many conditions. The refutation points out that this ease is relative to external factors, not inherent.
3
Examine the two additional opinions
The first opinion involves the efficient cause and condition; the second only the cause. Note how they attempt to specify preponderance.
Both are ultimately rejected.
4
Understand the conclusion
The final refutation shows that both opinions fail because they base preponderance on external factors. Thus, no inherent preponderance exists in the possible itself.
This reinforces the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يتحقق وجودها إلا بتحقق جميع أجزاء عللها، فالعدم أسهل وقوعاً. وهو مردود بأن سهولة عدمها بالنظر إلى غيرها لا يقتضي أولويته لذاتها. وقال بعضهم : إذا وجد المؤثر وعدم الشرط كان الوجود أولى بالممكن من العدم، وإذا عدم المؤثر ووجد الشرط كان العدم أولى به، وقيل إذا وجد العلة فالوجود أولى وإلا فالعدم، وفسادهما ظاهر لأن تلك الأولوية مستندة إلى الغير لا إلى ذات الممكن.
And its existence is not realized except by the realization of all parts of its causes, so non-existence is easier in occurrence. And it is refuted by [saying] that the ease of its non-existence with respect to other than it does not necessitate its preponderance for its essence. And some said: if the efficient cause exists and the condition is absent, then existence is more preponderant for the possible than non-existence; and if the efficient cause is absent and the condition exists, then non-existence is more preponderant. And it was said: if the cause exists then existence is more preponderant, otherwise non-existence. And their corruption is apparent because that preponderance is based on other than the essence, not on the essence of the possible.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage refutes a claim about the possible (mumkin): that non-existence is easier because its existence requires all parts of its causes. The refutation points out that this ease is relative to external factors (ghayr), not to the essence (dhat) itself. The commentator then mentions two opinions that attempt to specify conditions for preponderance based on the presence of the efficient cause and condition, or the cause in general. Both are rejected because the preponderance in those cases derives from external factors, not from the essence of the possible. Thus, the inherent preponderance remains unproven.

Key Terms
ممكن
عدم
أولوية
علة
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence is inherently easier for the possible regardless of external causes.
Correction: The passage argues that any ease or preponderance is relative to external factors, not inherent to the possible's essence.

Checkpoint
How do the refuters respond to the claim that non-existence is easier for the possible?
A) They affirm that non-existence is indeed easier.
B) They argue that ease is relative to other factors, not to its essence.
C) They claim existence is always easier.
D) They say both are equal.

Key Takeaway
The ease or preponderance of existence or non-existence for a possible entity is not inherent but always depends on external factors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume that avoiding a responsibility is easier than fulfilling it, are you mistaking a circumstantial ease for an inherent truth? How will you examine your own assumptions about what is 'easier' in light of this lesson?
?
Think of a time you judged a situation as inherently better or worse without considering the external factors. Does this lesson show you that your judgment may have been based on illusion? What will you do the next time you feel an inclination towards inaction?
?
If the possible has no inherent preponderance, then your own choices are not guided by any built-in ease. Are you ready to take full responsibility for your actions, knowing that no outcome is predetermined as 'easier' for you personally?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
6 min
Proof Against Self-Preponderance: The Two Branches and the Objection About the Cause
Explains the two branches of the proof and answers an objection about the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the proof against self-preponderance and why the cause of the occurrence of the other side cannot be the absence of self-preponderance.

Core Idea
If the other side occurs with a cause, that cause cannot be the absence of preponderance, or the possible wouldn't be possible.

This passage continues the commentary on the proof that a contingent thing cannot prefer one side over another without a cause. The commentator explains the two branches of the proof and then tackles an objection: could the cause be the absence of self-preponderance? The answer reveals a deep logical contradiction that ultimately shows preponderance cannot arise from the essence alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the flow of the argument from the two branches to the objection and its refutation.
The passage is dense; do not worry about every detail on first reading.
2
Identify the two possibilities when the other side is not impossible
The first part states: if the other side is not impossible, it may occur. Then two possibilities are given: either it occurs without a cause (impossible) or with a cause. Focus on how the impossibility of occurrence without a cause is argued.
The argument for impossibility relies on the equality principle: if an equal is impossible without a cause, a less preponderant side is even more so.
3
Understand the objection and the attempted solution
The objection says: the cause for the other side's occurrence could be the absence of self-preponderance. This would mean that the preponderance of the first side depends only on the essence, not on any external factor. Analyze why this seems plausible.
4
See why the objection leads to a contradiction
The refutation: if the cause is the absence of self-preponderance, then the absence of preponderance would be impossible (since preponderance is essential). This makes the other side's occurrence impossible, contradicting the assumption that the possible is contingent.
The contradiction is that the possible would not be possible—this is a 'khalaf' (reductio ad absurdum).
5
Grasp the conclusion about the cause and preponderance
Since the cause cannot be essential, it must be external. Therefore, the preponderance of the first side depends on the absence of that external cause, meaning preponderance is not from the essence alone. This proves the need for an external cause for preponderance.
This supports the broader argument against self-preponderance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإلا أي وإن لم يمتنع إلخ) أي إن لم يمتنع الطرف الآخر جاز وقوعه، فإما أن يقع إلخ . قوله : ( وإما أن يقع بعلة) فإن قلت يجوز أن تكون تلك العلة عدم الأولوية الذاتية، فلا يتوقف ثبوت الأولوية للطرف الأول على عدم شيء آخر سوى ذات الممكن، حتى يلزم خلاف المقدر. قلت حينئذ يكون عدم تلك الأولوية ممتنعا لأن الأولوية مقتضى ذات الممكن فيكون وقوع الطرف الآخر ممتنعاً، فلا يكون الممكن ممكناً، هذا خلف. فلا بد أن تكون علته غير مستند إلى ذات الممكن، فيتوقف ثبوت أولوية الطرف الراجح على عدم تلك العلة، فلا تكون تلك الأولوية ناشئة عن الذات
His saying: 'and if not' i.e., if the other side is not impossible, then the other side may occur, so either it occurs without a cause — and that is evidently impossible, because the equal [side] when its occurrence without a cause is impossible, then the less preponderant is more worthy of being impossible without a cause — or it occurs with a cause. Then if you say: it is possible that that cause is the absence of self-preponderance, so the affirmation of preponderance for the first side does not depend on the absence of anything other than the essence of the possible, so as to contradict the assumption. I say: then the absence of that preponderance would be impossible, because preponderance is a requirement of the essence of the possible, so the occurrence of the other side would be impossible, so the possible would not be possible — this is a contradiction. Therefore, its cause must be not based on the essence of the possible, so the affirmation of the preponderance of the preponderant side depends on the absence of that cause, so that preponderance is not arising from the essence.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the proof. The commentator explains the two branches of the conditional: if the other side is not impossible, it must occur either without a cause (impossible) or with a cause. An objection is then raised: could the cause be the absence of self-preponderance? The answer is that this would make the absence of that preponderance impossible (since preponderance is essential), leading to a contradiction. Thus, the cause must be external, so the preponderance of the first side depends on the absence of that cause, meaning it is not from the essence alone.

Key Terms
طرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the cause for the other side's occurrence could be the absence of self-preponderance without contradiction.
Correction: If the cause is the absence of self-preponderance, then that absence would be impossible (since preponderance is essential), making the other side's occurrence impossible, which contradicts the contingent nature of the possible.

Checkpoint
In the proof, what happens if the other side occurs with a cause?
A) The preponderance is still from essence.
B) The preponderance becomes dependent on the absence of that cause.
C) The other side becomes necessary.
D) The proof fails.

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that preponderance cannot come from the essence alone; it requires an external cause, otherwise the possible would not be possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think you can choose freely between options. But this argument says that without an external cause, you could not preponderate one option over another. Where in your life do you assume self-sufficiency in making choices? How does that assumption crumble when you consider that your very preference might need a cause beyond you?
?
If preponderance cannot come from your own essence, what does that imply about your ability to do good or avoid evil without divine guidance? Are there areas in your life where you act as if your own nature is enough to tilt the scale toward righteousness?
?
The proof forces you to accept that your existence and even your inclinations are dependent on something outside. Does that thought make you uncomfortable? Or does it draw you to seek the only cause worthy of being the preponderator—the Creator? What would change if you truly lived as if every preference were sustained by Him?
